Foreword
!e study of human sound has a long history, harking back, I suspect, 
to ancient documents on oratory by Cicero, Seneca, Quintilian, and 
others. !e history of the singing act itself is more compressed, but 
with remarkable achievements over a relatively short period of time. To 
garner some understanding of how far we’ve come in the investigation 
of the human vocal instrument, one may try to imagine the enormous 
excitement surrounding the invention of the laryngoscope, along with 
the /f_irst glimpse of the vocal folds in action, and compare that with 
the rather commonplace status of stroboscope today. Manuel Garcia’s 
invention began an initially timorous, self-conscious courtship of science 
and pedagogy that ultimately merged into a marriage where stormy 
beginnings have—I think it’s safe to say—matured into a healthy, symbiotic 
relationship. !e oﬀspring of that union is a proliferation of research and 
writing about the human voice that has virtually exploded in the last half 
of the twentieth century and into the twenty-/f_irst. We have now attained a 
point of historical perspective at which we can trace a scienti/f_ic/pedagogic 
ancestry that generates a clearer understanding of the present and oﬀers 
direction for the future.
!e book you are holding in your hands is an important contribution 
to the literature, a unique and at the same time necessary expansion of the 
study of voice. A cursory reading of the title may lead one to think of 
acoustics in an external sense, that is, space acoustics and their eﬀect, and 
perhaps studio voice recording. !e subtitle, however, narrows the 
de/f_inition toward an internal orientation, requiring examination of voice 
as an acoustic instrument consisting of power source, sound source, and 
sound modi/f_iers. It diﬀers from typical voice pedagogy texts in that it does 
not oﬀer discrete chapters on breathing, registration, articulation (per se), 
interpretation, and the like; rather, it focuses on the pedagogic implications 
of the voice as an acoustic phenomenon. If much of the material seems 
more directly oriented toward the male singing voice, it is because men 
experience more complex acoustic phenomena than do women. 
PRAA.indb   12PRAA.indb   12 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
Foreword xiii
Nonetheless, a great deal of female acoustic strategy is oﬀered as well. 
Additionally, acoustic technology such as VoceVista and the Madde 
Synthesizer is being increasingly used as instructional aids in the voice 
studio, and Bozeman, who, if not a pioneer, is certainly an authoritative 
champion in the application of the latter, oﬀers the reader expert tutorials 
in each.
For a few decades now, I have known Kenneth Bozeman both personally 
and professionally as a highly respected singer, scholar, teacher, and 
author. I have been present at several of his workshops and master classes, 
and it has been my privilege to have published some of his work in the 
Journal of Singing . !at research, along with a great deal of additional 
material, has found elaboration and expanded context in this book, and I 
can think of few more highly quali/f_ied to oﬀer a study of this nature. In a 
subject area where one can soon sink in a sea of unfamiliar terminology, 
technology, and concepts, Bozeman navigates with a lucid, commonsense 
approach. !ere is much information, cogently arranged and clearly 
presented in accessible language, at the same time avoiding pitfalls of 
oversimpli/f_ication. His chapter on formants, for example, is one of the 
most easily understood treatments of the subject of which I’m aware, a 
paradigmatic primer that should be required reading for all singers and 
singing teachers.
To continue the genealogic metaphor introduced above, Bozeman’s 
commitment to distill matters from voice science—especially acoustics—
into practical voice pedagogy illustrates a lineage to the late iconic Richard 
Miller, whose insistence that all voice science regarding the act must 
ultimately serve the art of singing forms the philosophic and pedagogic 
underpinnings of this volume. !is is a very good book, written by an 
expert teacher for teachers.
Richard Dale Sjoerdsma, PhD 
Editor in Chief, Journal of Singing 
Professor Emeritus, Carthage College
Kenosha, Wisconsin 
April, 2013

Preface
At the 1981 convention of the National Association of Teachers of Singing 
in Minneapolis, Minnesota, !omas Cleveland presented a paper on the 
relationship of the singer’s formant frequency to voice category. During 
his presentation, a synthesized tenor voice was played singing an F major 
scale on the vowel [a]. Remarkably, the tenor’s vocal quality migrated 
through the color changes associated with the tenor passaggio, though no 
changes in the vocal tract formants or source input had been programmed 
other than the change of pitch (fundamental frequency). !ough passaggio 
was not the focus of the presentation, at that moment I was struck with 
the notion that the timbral shi/f_ts of vocal cover were not due to changes 
in laryngeal register, rather to some resonance phenomenon not yet 
understood. !us began my journey into the area of vocal acoustics.
Eight years later, in a voice lesson with the late Richard Miller during a 
sabbatical at Oberlin, my voice “turned over” while singing an [e] vowel 
on D4 . F r o m  R i c h a r d ’ s  w r i t i n g s ,  I  h a d  p r e v i o u s l y  t h o u g h t  t h a t  t h e  
biological male voice began to prepare for the upper voice during the 
zona di passaggio—roughly D4-G4 for a tenor—but only turned over near 
the secondo passaggio , at G4. I was also surprised at the quality of the 
vowel, which sounded odd to my internal hearing. I asked Richard if what 
I had just done was acceptable. He had me sing the passage again, and 
without further explanation, said that it was /f_ine. !at was the second 
major step in this journey. I was practicing during the sabbatical in the 
new laboratory Richard was developing at Oberlin at the time, and was 
regularly using the Kay Elemetrics Sona-Graph 5500 with its real-time 
spectrography. In very short order, I realized that the voice turned over 
and “closed” in a parallel relationship to the /f_irst formant locations of 
vowels. It would be a few years before I knew precisely what the factors 
were, but from that point on, my teaching of non-treble passaggio was 
changed.
Over the next decade, I tried a few times to interest members of the 
voice science community in testing this hypothesis, but without success. I 
PRAA.indb   14PRAA.indb   14 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
Preface xv
didn’t know that at about the same time, Donald Miller and Harm Schutte 
were observing this and other acoustic phenomena in their lab in 
Groningen, Netherlands. It was not until I was provided with Svante 
Granqvist’s Madde voice synthesizer that I could prove with precision 
what I had long suspected: that biological male voices turn over when the 
second harmonic rises above the /f_irst formant of the sung vowel. By this 
time, Miller and Schutte had documented this and related phenomena, 
and in a subsequent visit to Lawrence University, where I teach, Donald 
con/f_irmed my observations. I have since enjoyed very informative 
conversations with Donald and a number of other voice scientists, most 
of whom now acknowledge the phenomenon of the F1:2fo turn of the 
voice.
I am not a voice scientist. I am a voice teacher. It is not my intention 
with this bit of personal history to encourage voice teachers to presume to 
know what we don’t—there’s enough of that already in every profession—
rather to realize that we each bring something of great value to the 
conversation. While voice teachers need to be open to learning from the 
voice science community, and willing to relinquish ideas that run counter 
to reality, we also have vitally important things to contribute. Ongoing 
open and honest collaborations between voice teachers and voice 
scientists—both of whom are thoughtful and humble enough to consider 
perspectives other than their own—are needed to advance the pedagogy 
and art of singing.
Second Edition
!is present edition is an updated, further attempt to distill insights 
and principles emerging from voice research that have proven fruitful 
for studio pedagogy, and to present them in a manner that is accessible 
to the general voice community. It aspires to contribute both to better 
pedagogy and to more productive, better-informed, mutually respectful, 
and bene/f_icial conversations between the pedagogic and scienti/f_ic 
communities.
Changes in this edition include an attempt at more gender-inclusive 
language, an updating of acoustical terms and notation, and the inclusion 
of the transient theory of voice. !ere is some inclusion of Ian Howell’s 
work on spectral tone color and auditory roughness, and Chadley 
PRAA.indb   15PRAA.indb   15 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
 Preface xvi
Ballantyne’s work on vibrotactile sensation, which are elaborated in 
greater detail in Kinesthetic Voice Pedagogy 2 (2021). !ere is also some 
additional content addressing both treble voice acoustics and vocal styles 
other than Western classical.
I am indebted to many souls for what I have learned in the area of 
voice acoustics, from the writings of William Vennard, Johan Sundberg, 
Ingo Titze, and Donald Miller (who also spent a week as a guest in my 
home discussing aspects of the /f_irst edition and advancing my 
understanding, especially of treble voice resonance strategies), to 
enlightening conversations with scientists Ron Scherer and with 
knowledgeable voice colleagues such as Richard Miller, Scott McCoy, 
John Nix, Christian Herbst, Stephen Austin, and Dan Ihasz. More recent 
conversations with Robert Loewen, Nicholas Perna, and Brad Story, and 
my collaborations with Ian Howell and Chadley Ballantyne, have further 
advanced my understanding.
Several colleagues read dra/f_ts of the /f_irst edition and gave helpful 
feedback: Christian Herbst, Donald Miller, John Koopman, Richard 
Sjoerdsma, Stephen Austin, Scott McCoy, Steven Spears, and Dale 
Duesing. A dra/f_t of the second edition was reviewed with feedback by 
Yvonne Gonzales Redmann, Ian Howell, Chadley Ballantyne, Carol 
Mastrodomenico, and Joanne Bozeman.
I owe a special thanks to my good friend, Kathleen Wilson, for urging 
me to write the /f_irst edition, for her subsequent enthusiasm for the project, 
and for insisting that I make it clear and accessible to those less familiar 
with the jargon of voice science.
I thank my students from whom and through whom I have learned 
over the years, especially those who contributed the recorded examples 
and exercises (now available at http://www  .kenbozeman .com or http://
faculty .lawrence .edu /bozemank /664 -2/): Luke Randall, Davey Harrison, 
Willson Oppedahl, Phillip Jindra, Jon Stombres, Mitchell Kasprzyk, Cayla

pedagogic model: Liam McNally, César Castro, and Shaofeng Zheng. I 
also thank my colleague David Berk, Lawrence’s expert in instructional 
technology, who gave generously of his time and expertise in producing 
the early recordings.

About the Author
Kenneth Bozeman , tenor, Emeritus Professor 
of Music at Lawrence University Conservatory 
of Music in Appleton, Wisconsin (1977–2020), 
holds performance degrees from Baylor 
University and the University of Arizona. He 
subsequently studied at the State Conservatory of 
Music in Munich, Germany, on a fellowship from 
Rotary International. He was chair of the voice 
department at Lawrence, where he taught voice, 
voice science, and pedagogy.
Mr. Bozeman has received both of Lawrence University’s Teaching 
Awards, was awarded the Van Lawrence Fellowship by the Voice 
Foundation and NATS in 1994 for his interest in voice science and 
pedagogy, and is on the editorial boards of the NATS Journal of Singing 
and the Voice Foundation Journal of Voice. He has presented on acoustic 
vocal pedagogy for many venues including the Voice Foundation, 
Physiology and Acoustics of Singing, the Pan European Voice Conference, 
the European, British, and Latvian Voice Teacher Associations, the 
International Congress of Voice Teachers, and NATS conventions. Mr. 
Bozeman was twice a master teacher for the NATS Intern Program (2013 
and 2017), was inducted into the prestigious American Academy of 
Teachers of Singing in 2019, and received a Lifetime Achievement Award 
from the New CCM Vocal Pedagogy Institute in 2024. Mr. Bozeman was 
an active recitalist and performer of oratorio, having performed with the 
Milwaukee Symphony, the Wisconsin Chamber Orchestra, the Green 
Lake Music Festival, and the Louisville Bach Society. His former students 
have sung at major opera houses, including Deutsche Oper Berlin, New 
Y ork City Opera, Santa Fe, Seattle, Houston Grand, San Francisco Opera, 
Chicago Lyric, Malmö Sweden, Dresden, and the Metropolitan Opera.

Practical Vocal Acoustics
1
Introduction
Voice teachers have been addressing vocal acoustics in some manner for as 
long as there has been voice instruction. Given the history of excellence in 
singing, singers, and teachers, the historic empirical approach clearly has 
had success. However, our scienti/f_ic knowledge about and understanding 
of vocal acoustics have grown exponentially in the last sixty to eighty 
years, and will certainly continue to be re/f_ined by the growing number 
of ongoing collaborations between voice scientists and voice teachers 
interested in voice science. With sophisticated yet inexpensive sound 
analysis technology now widely available, more voice teachers are curious 
about its potential value for the studio and the practice room, and seeing 
the need to understand and be well-informed about the acoustics of vocal 
registration—at the very least, as a means to more e#cient pedagogy, but 
also as an essential element of voice pedagogy courses. !ere are several 
excellent texts available that delve deeply into the physics of vocal acoustics 
(See Selected References), and on instructional technology and pedagogic 
application, but there is still a need for texts that explain the pedagogic 
implications of acoustics in a manner accessible to the majority of 
teachers and students. !is book represents an attempt to place principles 
emerging from voice science in a clear and practical pedagogic context for 
voice teachers and voice pedagogy students. An eﬀort has been made to 
distill from the science of vocal acoustics those factors that are essential 
for teachers at the beginning of the twenty-/f_irst century to understand 
and that are most likely to be productive for improving our pedagogic 
e#ciency, and to present them in language that is generally accessible. It 
is the author’s view that while re/f_inement of our scienti/f_ic understanding 
of vocal acoustics will surely continue, the basic elements presented here 
will be immediately helpful and are su#ciently well-established to survive 
PRAA.indb   1PRAA.indb   1 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  2
Introduction
inevitable further developments. !is text will be limited to acoustic 
information that is relevant to teaching and will not address in any detail 
non-acoustic aspects of singing. It is also based primarily on the historic 
aesthetics and practices of the non-ampli/f_ied Western classical singing 
tradition, which necessitates optimal acoustic e#ciency to sustainably 
achieve phonations across a wide range that can /f_ill large venues. Since 
all singers inhabit relatively similar internal acoustic “landscapes, ” many 
of the acoustic principles presented here are relevant for and applicable to 
other vocal styles. !is edition will elaborate further on those connections.
Tools
First a brief word about technology in the studio: !ere is a growing array 
of so/f_tware applications available for studio use. For those who object to 
the amount of voice science and instructional technology invading the 
studio, consider these observations: the teacher who can’t teach a good 
voice lesson without it won’t be able to teach a good voice lesson with 
it. Most technology is merely sophisticated feedback that can give more 
concrete evidence of what good teachers were already listening or looking 
for. !is is no diﬀerent in principle than the use of a mirror, an audio 
recording device, or a video recording device. !e teacher is still just as 
dependent on a re/f_ined ability to hear, sense, and observe what the student 
is doing, but instructional technology can improve the speci/f_icity of 
instruction, the learning curve of students, quickly settle arguments over 
sounds just made, and generally make teaching more e#cient. Of course, 
one can certainly imagine use of the same technology becoming intrusive, 
too prominent, or ine#cient if not well managed, much like an obsession 
with the mirror might be.
Later chapters will explain two excellent instruments of instructional 
technology: the Madde voice synthesizer and the VoceVista Video Pro 
sound analysis program. A number of points in the text will be illustrated 
by suggested explorations using the Madde voice synthesizer (see 
Appendix 1, p. 172). Possible studio uses of VoceVista will be explained 
and illustrated in a subsequent chapter. In order to understand and 
eﬀectively use these instructional tools, the teacher and student will need 
a basic understanding of vocal acoustics. Readers su#ciently informed 
PRAA.indb   2PRAA.indb   2 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
 Introduction 3
about harmonics, resonances, and formants are encouraged to read the 
instructional technology chapters /f_irst and then do each Madde exploration 
as it is suggested in the text. (Seeing and hearing the Madde explorations 
in some cases dramatically and convincingly illustrate the principle being 
discussed.) Others may /f_ind it more productive to return to the Madde 
explorations only a/f_ter /f_irst processing the chapters on harmonics, 
resonances, and formants. Madde is designed for PC computers and 
requires additional so/f_tware for use on Apple computers.
Semantics: Language and Models
All language is metaphorical or representative, not the thing itself. It 
involves assigned and agreed-upon labels for things or concepts. Similarly, 
explanatory models are simpli/f_ied representations of more complex 
phenomena and are used to assist more summary understanding. As 
simpli/f_ications, models deliberately exclude some information in order to 
highlight overarching principles. !erefore, “all models are wrong; some 
are useful” (George Box, statistician). !is principle will obtain in the 
models of acoustics used in this text—being incomplete simpli/f_ications, 
they are heuristic: not absolutely true in all details, but nonetheless useful 
for general understanding and practical application.
Knowledge
Our knowledge base for physical activity is of two kinds: declarative 
knowledge, which describes the objective, physical realities involved; 
and procedural knowledge , which describes the subjective, sensorial 
experiences of and motivations for the phenomena. Objective, declarative 
knowledge involves things that can be measured, documented, and 
subsequently reported with clarity. It is, therefore, accessible to scienti/f_ic 
investigation. However, the objective description of function is of limited 
value as a vocal intention, since motor behavior is best organized by 
simple output targets rather than by complex micromanagement of the 
parts or processes involved. A/f_ter all, all human experience is subjective, 
being mediated through our senses and interpreted by our brain as 
PRAA.indb   3PRAA.indb   3 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  4
percepts. Procedural knowledge, simply put, is what it feels like to do the 
thing—in this case, to sing well. Said another way, procedural knowledge 
is quite plainly ability, skill—the brain guiding the body with stored 
sensorial memories reactivated as intentions of motor behavior. Being 
subjective and sensorial, they are challenging to articulate or describe, yet 
they constitute precisely the kind of knowledge that the singer needs and 
which the teacher must somehow convey. Successful historic pedagogy is 
rife with procedural terminology: terms that are descriptive of functional 
sound and somatosense: head voice, chest voice, pharyngeal voice, whistle 
voice, covering, appoggio, girare, voce aperta, voce chiusa , and so on. 
Our only viable option as teachers, besides demonstration, is to use the 
representative, metaphorical, subjective vehicle of descriptive language 
and models. Vocal directives must be simple enough to execute as motor 
intentions. However, the models and metaphors we choose bene/f_it greatly 
from being informed not only by successful historic pedagogy but also by 
the growing body of increasingly accurate declarative knowledge—that 
is, by science-informed pedagogy. Declarative knowledge can inform 
procedural choices, adding valuable speci/f_icity, but procedural knowledge 
is needed for eﬀective motor intentions, those o/f_ten subconscious, 
sensorial messages from the brain to the body that organize its physiologic 
responses.

Practical Vocal Acoustics Preliminary Basics
2
Preliminary Basics
Sound
Sound to human perception is comprised of pressure variations of some 
su#ciently elastic medium, typically air; variations that are both abrupt 
enough and of enough intensity to fall within the processing capacity 
of the human sense of hearing, that is, from approximately 20 to 20,000 
oscillations per second within su#cient intensity parameters.
Simple Sounds
If an oscillation of air pressure is simple and periodic (sinusoidal and 
regular), it comprises a single frequency. Such simple sounds rarely 
happen in nature but can be generated by a computer. Most sounds are 
complex, containing multiple additive frequency components.
Noise
Aperiodic (non-patterned, irregular) pressure waves are comprised 
of many frequencies that are out of sync with each other. Being 
non-periodic, there is no regularly repeated pattern of variation, no 
common denominator frequency, rather, a theoretically in/f_inite sweep 
of frequency content, and o/f_ten no clear sense of pitch. Like white 
PRAA.indb   5PRAA.indb   5 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  6
light, which is comprised of all light frequencies, a noise containing 
all audible frequencies at approximately equal intensity is termed 
“white noise. ” A white noise input can be shaped su#ciently by a 
resonator to feature a frequency peak and thus impart a sense of pitch. 
For example, the dominant frequency area of noise-based consonants 
helps to determine their identity: this sequence of fricative noises /s ç 
ʃ/ ranges from higher to lower in “pitch” percept (depending upon their 
articulation).
Musical Tone
Musical tones that have a clear pitch percept are sounds whose 
complex waveforms can be understood as being constructed from 
a set of frequencies, all of which are multiples of a lowest common 
denominator, called the fundamental frequency .1 Each of these 
frequency components is called a harmonic . Together they form a 
sound pressure pattern ( pressure waveform ) that repeats itself at 
the frequency of that lowest common denominator. Our perception 
of the frequency of that complex wave—essentially equivalent to its 
fundamental frequency—is called its pitch. Individual frequencies have 
discrete tone colors to human perception—a relative height, brightness, 
and vowel-like tone color (Howell 2016, 2025). !erefore, the relative 
strength of all of the frequency components—the fundamental and the 
higher frequencies or harmonics that comprise the spectral content 
of a particular sound—determines the “color” or “quality” of that 
tone. Our perception of pitch can be aﬀected by the spectral content 
of the sound—stronger high or low-frequency content can slightly 
raise or lower (sharpen or &atten) our sense of the pitch. In a more 
recent attempt at consensus notation among voice science disciplines, 
harmonic frequencies, which were previously reported as H 1, H 2, H 3, 
etc., are now designated as multiples of the fundamental oscillation; 
that is, as 1 fo, 2fo, 3fo, etc. Since musically notated pitch is logarithmic 
in relation to frequency, the musical intervals between successive 
frequencies of the harmonic series decrease in size, starting with an 
octave, a perfect /f_i/f_th, a perfect fourth, a major third, a minor third, 
etc., as shown in Figure 2.1 .
PRAA.indb   6PRAA.indb   6 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
 Preliminary Basics 7
Voice Acoustics
Voice acoustics are modeled in at least two ways: the non-linear source-
/f_ilter model (steady state) which prioritizes the frequency domain, and 
the transient model which prioritizes the time domain. Both models are 
useful to our understanding, yet necessarily incomplete and to that extent 
“incorrect. ” !e currently more o/f_ten encountered source-/f_ilter model may 
imply an independence and chronological sequence of its components 
that misrepresents the immediacy and complexity of their interactions. 
!e more functionally accurate transient model, by de-emphasizing 
harmonics, may distract attention from the simple predictive relevance 
of the intervallic, interactive relationships between potential harmonic 
frequencies and vocal tract resonances to acoustic registration events.
!is text will incorporate aspects of both models, but will primarily 
feature the non-linear source-/f_ilter model, in which voice is described as 
being comprised of two interactive factors: (1) the voice source —the 
complex waveform arising in the vocal tract from the interaction of the 
periodic modulations of the airstream by vocal fold oscillation, and (2)  a 
/f_ilter function, the immediately overlapping eﬀect of the transfer 
function (sound transfer characteristics) of the vocal tract, which 
selectively strengthens or weakens acoustic energy introduced into it by 
the glottal modulation of air&ow. !e term source-/f_ilter is a semantic 
conundrum. Glottal closures, generating the most abrupt changes in 
pressure, are dominant in exciting the vocal tract air column. A single 
glottal pulse closure excites a broadly “shaped” noise response in the vocal 
tract air column. In other words, a spectrum image of a single pulse does 
Figure 2.1  Intervallic relationship of the ﬁrst eight frequencies of the 
harmonic series of the pitch C3.
PRAA.indb   7PRAA.indb   7 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  8
not generate the harmonic set of a sung pitch; rather, it generates a noise 
that maps the resonance structure of the current vocal tract shape. In this 
way, the so-called /f_ilter (the vocal tract) participates in the creation of the 
initial voice source sound, a /f_ilter-shaped noise. Only the periodic 
reiteration of the glottic impulse subsequently /f_ilters that shaped voice 
source noise further for frequencies that oscillate in sync with its 
periodicity, that is, that are whole integer multiples of its fundamental 
iteration frequency. !at periodicity results in a pitch percept. In that way, 
the vocal fold oscillations participate in both source and /f_ilter functions. 
!e intensities of rebounding frequencies that are in sync with subsequent 
glottic closures are reinforced relative to how o/f_ten they are in sync. 1fo is 
in sync every return oscillation and therefore is potentially strongly 
reinforced; 2fo every second return, so somewhat less strongly; 3 fo every 
third return, 4 fo every fourth return, etc., so that increasingly higher 
harmonics weaken in intensity at a decibel rate per octave in what is 
termed a spectral slope, tilt, or roll-oﬀ. Y et, we must bear in mind that 
the intensities of those returning oscillations from each individual glottal 
impulse had already been “shaped” by the transfer function of the vocal 
tract, such that even before “returning, ” some were already stronger—if 
close to a vocal tract resonance peak, and some weaker—if in the “valley” 
between resonances. Hence, we get a subset of strengthened, radiated 
harmonics, so that only those frequencies that are both near vocal tract 
/f_ilter frequency peaks and are also harmonic with the glottic fundamental 
frequency oscillation survive to be represented in the radiated waveform.
Interactivity
!e di#culty in modeling this process in linear time is akin to the age-
old question of which comes /f_irst, the “chicken or the egg. ” !e accurate 
answer is: both. Glottal action excites the inherent reverberation attributes 
of the shape of the air column in the vocal tract. !us, the sound 
source originates in the vocal tract itself. !is interaction is essentially 
simultaneous, not linear nor chronological. !en that single “resonance-
shaped” noise is /f_iltered further by being periodically reiterated by the 
subsequent glottic impulse closures into a subset of harmonic frequencies. 
In sum, the source is also a /f_ilter and the /f_ilter is also a source—a rather 
PRAA.indb   8PRAA.indb   8 7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM7/10/2025   4:38:47 PM

completely non-linear, looping interactivity. !e eﬀect of the interactions 
between these two factors across range is the primary concern of acoustic 
vocal pedagogy. !ough in function they are overlappingly interactive, 
for simplicity’s sake, the next chapter (“!e Voice Source”) will explore 
aspects of glottal valve function in isolation: especially the way in which 
various closure characteristics of the glottic oscillation contribute to the 
voice source, thus modeling that part of the acoustic raw material from 
which the voice is built up. A later chapter (Resonances and Formants) 
will explore the characteristics, contribution, and tuning of the vocal tract 
as a /f_ilter, followed by a third chapter (Resonance:Harmonic Interactions) 
that examines how these two factors coordinate and interact across range, 
resulting in acoustic registration.

The Voice Source
!e complex waveform excited in the air column of the vocal tract 
by the oscillation of the vocal folds is called the voice source. In non-
noisy styles of singing, 1 it can be deconstructed as a subset of harmonic 
frequencies of the fundamental frequency of that oscillation. !at set 
of frequencies arises from a combination of the way in which each 
oscillation of the vibrator (the vocal folds) is simultaneously modulated 
from a tracheal airstream into resonance-shaped impulses, which are 
/f_iltered further by the ongoing periodicity of vocal fold oscillations. Only 
those frequencies that initially align with vocal tract resonances and are 
also in sync with the ongoing, reiterated vocal fold periodicity survive. 
Harmonic frequencies can only arise from the fundamental frequency 
of the pitch being sung (assigned by the composer or improvised by the 
performer), with all harmonics being limited to whole integer multiples 
of the fundamental frequency or /f_irst harmonic of that assigned pitch. In 
the typically vibrant tone of Western classical singing, these frequencies 
also oscillate regularly above and below the target frequency set (the 
intended pitch) at a rate of approximately six times per second, creating 
the phenomenon of vocal vibrato. !ere may also be some noise element, 
but the better or “cleaner” the phonation, the higher the harmonic 
to noise ratio —that is, the less aperiodic noise there will be. In an 
excellent tone in the classical aesthetic, there is no perceivable noise; 
in other words, no non-harmonic frequency components of signi/f_icant 
intensity. If such a glottic oscillation could be isolated from the vocal 
tract and introduced into open space—as in a lip brr—a complete set 
of harmonics would occur with a “ roll-oﬀ” or weakening in power the 
higher they are above the /f_irst harmonic (the fundamental frequency) 
at a rate that is aﬀected by several variables, but ideally at about 12 
PRAA.indb   11PRAA.indb   11 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  12

decibels (dBs) per octave. !e resultant pattern of intensity reduction 
per frequency is called its spectral slope . (Figure 3.1 ) An unresonated, 
pitched “laryngeal buzz” (as might be generated by an excised larynx) is 
surprisingly unattractive (see Madde exploration 1).

The Origin of Harmonics
A legitimate question arises concerning the physical origin of harmonics 
in voice, a key component of the source-/f_ilter model. Unlike stringed 
instruments, whose string lengths approximately subdivide and oscillate 
at many nearly harmonic frequencies, the glottal opening/closing event 
only oscillates at a fundamental frequency. !is modulates the rate of 
escaping air&ow in such a way as to create pressure variations, converting 
direct current (DC) &ow into an alternating current (AC) form of acoustic 
pressure waves, consisting of air pressure compressions and rarefactions. If 
Figure 3.1 Power spectrum of the voice source with a spectral slope or 
roll-off of ca. 12 dB’s/octave. A power spectrum displays power on the 
vertical and frequency on the horizontal.
PRAA.indb   12PRAA.indb   12 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
 The Voice Source 13
the glottis didn’t actually close, but merely modulated &ow symmetrically, 
that is, sinusoidally, we would only have a single fundamental frequency, 
since such a sinusoidal variation in air&ow would cause sinusoidal air 
pressure variations. With vocal fold closure, however, the &ow, and the rate 
of change of &ow become rather complex and asymmetrical, with a gradual 
increase in &ow at glottal opening, to a brief moment of peak &ow, followed 
by an increasingly steep, abrupt declination of &ow into glottal closure. !at 
sharp declination in &ow, plus the absence of &ow at glottal closure, cause 
a sudden pressure rarefaction, a kind of abrupt “jerking back, ” creating the 
strongest part of the acoustic signal. !is asymmetry, called the maximum 
/f_low declination rate, rapidly accelerates through the theoretical closing 
rates of all higher frequencies, resulting in the excitation of that sweep of 
frequencies that are compatible with the preferential frequency response 
of the vocal tract. As those favored frequencies oscillate back and forth, 
some of them that are in consonant sync with the next glottal closure event 
will be subsequently and repeatedly re-energized, as o/f_ten as their return 
oscillation aligns with the next fundamental glottal closure. !e /f_irst 
harmonic aligns and might receive an appropriately timed strengthening 
rarefaction “pull” on each return. A potential second harmonic would get 
an encouraging pull every second return swing, the third harmonic every 
third return swing, and so on. Being less frequently re-energized per return 
swing results in a weakening of successively higher harmonics, resulting in 
a spectral slope. !e returning swings of frequencies that are not multiples 
of the fundamental arrive at the glottis at an inopportune moment within 
their respective cycles and are suppressed, receiving an eﬀect contrary to 
and at odds with their phase. As mentioned above, impulse frequencies 
were previously enhanced or de-enhanced by the response of vocal tract 
resonances, a topic we will explore in the following chapters.
Voice Source Harmonic 
Characteristics
!e potential number and intensity of harmonics from the voice source 
that are available for resonation are primarily determined by four factors: 
mode of phonation, laryngeal register, intensity, and fundamental 
frequency.

Mode of Phonation
!e mode of phonation has to do with the relationship of three factors: 
transglottal air pressure diﬀerence, air&ow, and glottal (valve) resistance. 
Of these, the amount of breath pressure (via torso compression and 
resultant lung volume reduction) and the degree and type of glottal 
adductive resistance are the active, causal, controlling factors, with the 
amount of air&ow being the mediated result. Air &ows from areas of 
higher pressure to areas of lower pressure. Tracheal air pressure (below the 
glottis) is raised to be above the average pressure in the vocal tract (above 
the glottis). !is is referred to as the transglottal pressure diﬀerence . 
!at pressure diﬀerence is casually referred to simply as “breath pressure. ” 
Since transglottal pressure diﬀerence determines the amount of glottal 
resistance necessary to achieve competent vocal fold closure, and since 
the level of glottal resistance determines the collision force of the vibrating 
tissues, having an appropriately low transglottal pressure diﬀerence is 
essential to vocal fold health. A high transglottal pressure diﬀerence and 
collision force, over time, can result in tissue irritation and pathology. 
We will learn that favorable acoustic tunings of the vocal tract can raise 
the eﬀective “back pressure” in the vocal tract to reduce the transglottal 
pressure diﬀerence to comfortable, sustainable levels, even when higher 
subglottic pressure is needed for powerful phonations.
!ese three factors can be modeled in a phonation equation:2 Breath 
pressure/air&ow = glottal resistance. While there are many possible ratios 
between these factors, they can either be in balance, or imbalanced in one 
of two ways: too much air&ow or too much glottal resistance.
 z Pressed phonation (a phonation with excessive glottal resistance 
and insu#cient air&ow) has a moderately strong fundamental 
frequency or /f_irst harmonic (1fo), a shallower (less steep) spectral 
slope with a more gradual roll-oﬀ in power, and thus relatively more 
and stronger high harmonics. It is therefore less warm and more 
metallic, brassy, or overly bright in timbre. Ex.: 1/.5 = 2
 z Breathy phonation (a phonation with excess air&ow and insu#cient 
glottal resistance) has a moderate to strong 1 fo, but a steep spectral 
slope roll-oﬀ in power, and therefore fewer and weaker high partials, 
and o/f_ten some air turbulence noise element. It is therefore airy, 
&uty, and sometimes noisy in timbre. Ex.: 1/2 = .5
PRAA.indb   14PRAA.indb   14 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM

 z Fllow phonation has a strong 1fo, a moderate spectral slope roll-oﬀ  
(about 12 decibels per octave) and a relatively ample number of high 
harmonics. It is therefore balanced in timbre. Ex.: 1/1 = 1
A strong fundamental frequency requires that the amplitude of glottal 
opening be su#cient in each oscillatory cycle. Strong upper partials 
require complete glottal closure with a rapid closing rate, that is, an 
abrupt &ow declination rate. Such a generous opening and abrupt closure 
occurs in balanced &ow phonation, especially if initiated with pleasurable 
expression from gentle but complete glottal closure (a well-executed coup 
de glotte) immediately prior to phonation.
Laryngeal Register
Laryngeal register refers to the muscular adjustment of the vocal folds 
for pitch regulation. !is is primarily controlled by the thyroarytenoids 
(TA), the vocal fold shortening, thickening muscles that make up the 
deeper body of the vocal folds, and the cricothyroids (CT), the vocal 
fold-stretching, thinning muscles that lie partly outside of the larynx. 
!e muscles that close the vocal folds (interarytenoids and lateral 
cricoarytenoids) also play a role. Shorter, thicker, looser folds include 
the TA muscles in the oscillation, vibrate at lower frequencies, and are 
“chestier” in laryngeal register, while longer, tauter, thinner folds shi/f_t  
to vocal ligament  oscillations, vibrate at higher frequencies, and are 
“headier” in laryngeal register. (See Laryngeal Registration Revisited,  
p. 141, for more detail and for training suggestions.)
 z Chestier (shorter, thicker) laryngeal adjustments have a vertical 
phase diﬀerence  (a greater depth of vocal fold contact, with an 
undulatory motion from lower to upper vocal fold margins in 
each cycle) creating a more complex pressure waveform with a 
shallower (less steep) spectral slope, more, stronger high harmonics, 
and greater auditory roughness (buzziness). !ey are therefore 
inherently brassier in timbre. !is thicker vocal fold shape is 
increasingly referred to as vibrational mechanism one (M1).3
 z Headier (longer, thinner) laryngeal adjustments “chop” the airstream 
more simply (little to no vertical phase diﬀerence) and less abruptly, 
creating a more sinusoidal pressure wave pattern with a strong 1 fo, 
but fewer, weaker high harmonics, a steeper spectral slope and 
PRAA.indb   15PRAA.indb   15 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  16
minimal auditory roughness. !ey are therefore smoother, &utier 
in timbre. !is thinner vocal fold shape is referred to as vibrational 
mechanism two (M2).

 z Louder phonations usually have more and stronger high harmonics.
 z Quieter phonations have a steeper roll-oﬀ, with fewer and weaker

Madde Exploration 2: /T_he Eﬀect of Mode of Phonation and Range on

Absolute Spectral Tone Color
Research from the /f_ield of psychoacoustics—how the brain processes 
sound—has recently been brought into voice pedagogy by Ian Howell 
(Howell 2016, 2025). Psychoacoustics observes that, like human eyes 
and brains process light frequencies as colors, our ears and brains 
process sound frequencies as lower or higher, on a darkness to brightness 
continuum, and as vowel-like tone colors. Frequencies below C5 are 
low, dark, and [~u]-like in spectral tone color. Frequencies above C7 are 
high, bright and [~i]-like in spectral tone color. Frequencies between C5 
and C7 are intermediate in brightness and spectral tone color. ( Figure 
3.2) A frequency or su#ciently narrow spectral band from any sound 
source will share an absolute spectral tone color to the same individual 
auditor’s perception. !is understanding is proving to be transformative in 
understanding with predictive speci/f_icity the mechanisms of timbre, vowel 
formation, migration, and modi/f_ication across range and vocal register.
Necessary Roughness
A second observation from the psychoacoustic work of Howell is 
the percept of necessary roughness . As mentioned above, pitch is 
logarithmic, with increasingly smaller intervals between increasingly 
higher harmonics. !e /f_irst /f_ive voice source harmonics are well spaced and 
reinforce a pure, smooth percept of the pitch or fundamental frequency. 
Beginning with harmonic six (6 fo), we have adjacent harmonics that are 
a minor third or less apart. Harmonics in close proximity introduce a 
beating “roughness” to human perception; the closer together, the 
rougher the percept. Harmonics above the eighth harmonic not only 
introduce an even stronger roughness factor but also achieve perceptual 
independence from the fundamental frequency or sense of pitch, existing 
PRAA.indb   17PRAA.indb   17 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  18
as a buzzy or ringing quality alongside the pitch percept. Nota bene:  
psychoacoustic roughness is a buzzing or sizzling quality generated by 
closely clustered, harmonic (periodic) frequencies, and is not the same 
as aperiodic roughness caused by asymmetrical vocal fold vibration, 
false vocal fold vibration, the deliberate distortion used in some vocal 
genres, or noises induced by vocal fold pathology. Competent vocal fold 
closure results in a longer closed phase in each vibratory cycle, generating 
higher, more closely clustered harmonics and an increase in roughness 
percept. All of the factors cited above that aﬀect the number and strength 
of harmonics—mode of phonation, laryngeal register, intensity, and 
fundamental frequency—also aﬀect the amount and quality of timbral 
roughness. Auditory roughness is a “necessary” correlate to competent 
vocal fold closure for much of the range. Being perceptually associated 
with laryngeal registers—buzzier seeming “chestier” and smoother 
seeming “headier”—auditory roughness migrates across range, register, 
and voice type from buzzier low tones to smoother high tones.
Spectral Slope Contribution
!e main contribution of the glottal valve to the perception of timbre is 
via spectral slope, since spectral slope determines both relative brightness 
and auditory roughness. It also contributes to the strength of the more 
o/f_ten, vowel-identifying second formant contribution. A steep spectral 
slope will seem warmer, rounder, smoother, and therefore “headier, ” 
and will feature more of the lower, warmer, /f_irst formant tone color 
contribution. A shallow spectral slope will seem brassier, rougher, and 
therefore “chestier, ” and feature more of the second and higher formant 
tone color contributions.
Take-Away Message for Harmonics
!e voice source’s /f_ilter-shaped spectral content comprises the essential 
raw material from which the radiated complex waveform is constructed. 
!e resonator (vocal tract) does not make sound; it shapes and reinforces, 
PRAA.indb   18PRAA.indb   18 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
 The Voice Source 19
or not, acoustic energy introduced into it via the excitation of abrupt 
glottal closure. !erefore, the singer needs a su#ciently robust waveform 
with enough spectral content, freely gotten, appropriate in balance and 
roll-oﬀ for the pitch, dynamic, and aﬀect they are given to perform. 
!is healthy voice source is dependent upon the establishment of &ow 
phonation, usually through clean, complete, /f_irm but gentle glottal closure, 
an eﬀective, responsive breath/voice connection and coordination, 
appropriate laryngeal registration for the situation, and a smooth, gradual 
steepening of spectral slope with pitch ascent.
Breath management and even laryngeal registration are topics that will 
not be covered to any great extent in this text. Nonetheless, eﬀective 
coordination of breath with phonation, as well as an ability to make the 
laryngeal adjustments in vocal fold shape necessary for all pitches of the 
range, are requisite, foundational factors in establishing optimal resonance. 
We will later learn that idealized resonator adjustments can signi/f_icantly 
assist both breath management and vocal fold vibration. Vocal tract 
resonance can interact with the glottal valve in such a way that glottal 
closure is lengthened without resorting to the excess glottal resistance of 
pressed phonation. In other words, skillful resonance tuning can eﬀectively 
raise the pressure of air in the vocal tract to keep the transglottal pressure 
diﬀerence comfortably low.
Roles of the Glottal Valve
!e vocal folds have two primary jobs in phonation: glottal resistance—
the amount of closure strength (glottal competence) needed to manage the 
transglottal pressure diﬀerence and resultant air&ow from the trachea—and 
shaping for pitch—adjusting their length, mass, thickness/thinness, and 
stiﬀness in order to create all of the pitches of the singer’s range. !e more 
eﬀort they expend for the former (pressure resistance), the less &exibility 
and capability they will have for the latter (pitch shaping), limiting range 
and ease of function. Transglottal pressure diﬀerence determines both the 
degree of glottal resistance required for competent glottal closure as well 
as the impact force of the colliding vocal fold tissues. !erefore, /f_inding 
an appropriate balance of pressure, &ow, and resistance is essential not 
only to e#ciency and range but also to vocal health. Fortunately, skillful 
PRAA.indb   19PRAA.indb   19 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  20
acoustic tuning of the vocal tract can raise the air pressure above the 
glottis to counterbalance the higher tracheal (subglottic) pressures needed 
for powerful phonations, eﬀectively reducing the transglottal pressure 
diﬀerence.

circumstance (a dramatic tenor loud C5 versus a countertenor mezzo

purposes, singing teachers needn’t worry about this distinction.

Overview of the Changing 
Theories of Vocal Resonance
Our understanding of how the vocal tract transfers acoustic energy from 
the voice source to the outside world has evolved considerably over the 
last /f_i/f_ty years. Before an explanation of the details of the current theory 
is given, a cursory overview of how theories of vocal resonance have 
developed will provide some context.
Resonance
Resonance is the tendency of an object or system (like the air contained 
by the vocal tract) to respond (oscillate) longer and more strongly to 
particular frequencies introduced into it. !e oscillation of frequencies 
generated by the voice source that are at or near the resonance peaks of the 
vocal tract will be greater than those frequencies that are “in the valleys” 
between and farther away from those resonance peaks.
Coupled Helmholtz Resonators
One early model of vocal resonance was based on the conception of a 
series of successively coupled Helmholtz resonators: the /f_irst two being 
the pharynx and the oral cavity, with a narrowing created by a tongue 
bulge dividing them. !e basic principle in determining the “pitch” 
PRAA.indb   21PRAA.indb   21 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  22
Overview of the Changing Theories of Vocal Resonance
(resonance frequency) of each resonator was thought to be the size of the 
contained air space and the relative size of the exit of that space. !e larger 
the space and the smaller the exit of a Helmholtz resonator, the lower its 
resonance frequency. !erefore, an [i] with its large pharyngeal space and 
narrow, rather frontal exit into the oral cavity was thought to create a low 
“pitched” /f_irst resonance. !at resonance would then be coupled with the 
small space in front of the tongue hump with a larger exit (relative to the 
small oral space) at the lips, creating a higher “pitched” second resonance. 
!e explanatory eﬀectiveness of this theory was best for front vowels 
like [i], but less convincing for back vowels like [u], which has a low /f_irst 
resonance even though it has a relatively smaller pharyngeal space. !e 
coupled resonator theory le/f_t the tuning mechanism of higher resonances 
somewhat in question. It also did not account for the epilaryngeal tube, 
nor consider the functioning of the entire vocal tract as a single tube with 
multiple resonance peaks. !e accurate observation that larger spaces 
with smaller exits create lower resonances contributes to the innate but 
misleading sense that the pharynx is more associated with the lower, 
darker /f_irst resonance and the oral cavity with the higher, brighter second.
Linear Source-Filter Model
A more recent model—the linear source-/f_ilter model—presents 
vocal acoustics as being comprised of three elements: a power source 
(breath pressure and &ow), a source vibration (formed by the vocal fold 
oscillation), and a resonator (the vocal tract), which /f_ilters the input from 
the source, selectively strengthening or weakening partials from the voice 
source. !is model recognized that the tuning of vocal tract resonances 
could be more eﬀectively explained from a single, quarter-wave resonator 
model. A quarter-wave resonator is a tube closed at one end (the glottis) 
and open at the other (the lips). Sound waves or wave segments that 
have a pressure node at the lips and an antinode at the glottis will be 
re&ected (“echoed”) productively within the tube, building up standing 
waves, that is, resonance frequencies that are then featured in the radiated 
sound as formants. !is oppositional node:antinode circumstance /f_irst 
happens at one-fourth of a wavelength. !erefore, a closed-open tube is 
a resonator that naturally resonates a sound wave four times its length, 
PRAA.indb   22PRAA.indb   22 7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM7/10/2025   4:38:48 PM
 Overview of the Changing Theories of Vocal Resonance 23
hence its designation, a ¼ or “quarter-wave” resonator. Uniformly shaped 
quarter-wave resonators reinforce sound   waves that are odd-numbered 
multiples of this lowest resonance frequency, all of which have nodes 
and antinodes at opposite ends of the vocal tract. !e waveforms of each 
higher resonance have additional pressure nodes (contextual “zeros”) and 
antinodes (maxima and minima) at various places along the vocal tract 
(Figure 4.1).
Perturbation Theory
!ose resonances can then be retuned by local perturbations (narrowings 
or widenings). A perturbation near a pressure node or antinode of a wave 
eﬀectively changes the length, and hence the resonance frequency of 
that wave. !e /f_irst two, lower vocal tract resonances have fewer nodes 
and antinodes, are therefore more susceptible to fewer, more simple 
perturbations (are more easily tunable). !ey also cover the frequency 
range of all vowel-like spectral tone colors, making the /f_irst two resonances 
both the most available and the most relevant for vowel de/f_inition and 
diﬀerentiation.
Figure 4.1 Schematic of the pressure nodes and antinodes of the ﬁrst 
three resonances of the vocal tract.
PRAA.indb   23PRAA.indb   23 7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  24
!e source-/f_ilter model was initially assumed to be strictly linear: a 
power source (breath) bringing the vocal folds into vibration, generating 
a set of harmonic frequencies which then proceed up and out. Some of 
these source harmonics are then selectively strengthened by the resonances 
of the vocal tract before /f_inally being radiated from the lips. !ough the 
vocal tract is not a uniform tube, this model is conceptually useful and 
explanatory, but erroneously implies an independence and chronologically 
temporal sequence of source and /f_ilter, a somewhat inaccurate origin and 
implied completeness of a harmonic frequency set, and falls short of 
explaining the inherent non-linear interactivity of resonator and vibrator.
Non-Linear Source-Filter Model
An even more recent version proposed a non-linear source-/f_ilter model 
with some degree of inherent interactivity from the onset, and potential 
for great interactive non-linearity with certain resonance tunings. As 
reported above, the voice source signal is interactively generated from the 
start. In certain circumstances (more on this later: See Non-linear Source-
Filter !eory Revisited, p. 73) acoustic energy reverberating in the /f_ilter 
can raise the eﬀective pressure above the glottis, productively reducing the 
transglottal pressure diﬀerence, and assisting the e#ciency and power of 
both vibrator and respiratory coordination.
The Transient Theory of Voice
All of the above cited theories highlight the frequency domain. Sound 
is actually generated in the time domain, in which events, such as 
pressure variations, occur or unfold in succession over time. Frequency 
measures how o/f_ten an event occurs within a given time span, as in the 
“times-per-second” designation of Hertz. An early model of voice, the 
transient theory, models voice as an additive pressure accumulation 
generated by the succession of individual glottal impulses. !is theory 
accurately recognizes that the vocal folds do not generate a complete set 
of harmonics with a spectral slope prior to resonation and /f_iltering by the 
PRAA.indb   24PRAA.indb   24 7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM
 Overview of the Changing Theories of Vocal Resonance 25
vocal tract. Instead, the abrupt closure of each single glottal pulse excites 
the air column of the vocal tract, causing an impulse containing a broad 
sweep of frequencies that immediately re&ects vocal tract resonances, 
that is, the sound transfer function of the vocal tract shape. In this way, 
resonances and radiated formants occur in a simultaneous combination 
of source and /f_ilter at each glottal impulse, prior to the reinforcement of 
harmonic frequency energy and content. Only at the periodic reiteration 
of those resonance-shaped impulses is a subset of the frequencies that 
are in sync with the periodicity of the successive pulse sequence, that is, 
some of its harmonic frequencies, reinforced, while other frequencies 
that are out of phase with the repeated successive glottal closing events 
are suppressed. !e time period during which this occurs is so brief, so 
immediate, as to be eﬀectively simultaneous to human perception. Only 
those frequencies that are both favored by the vocal tract transfer function 
(are near its resonance peaks) in each individual pulse and are also in 
sync (are harmonic) with the periodicity of the subsequent succession of 
glottic pulses are reinforced to survive and be represented in the complex, 
radiated waveform.
Summary
To summarize, though the non-linear source-/f_ilter theory is a helpful, 
simpli/f_ied model, voice is not actually created as a complete set of harmonic 
frequencies arising from the vocal folds with a particular spectral slope, 
that is subsequently /f_iltered by the resonances of the vocal tract. Source 
and /f_ilter are completely complicit and interactive from the start. Voice 
is more a sequence of &ow-interruption-resonance-shaped impulses that 
are then rapidly /f_iltered further in time by the periodicity of successive 
glottal closures. Again, only those frequencies that survived the initial 
/f_ilter characteristics and also are in sync with the period of the ongoing 
glottal impulses are represented in the radiated waveform. !at radiated 
waveform then re&ects the “hills and valleys” of the resonance structure of 
the vocal tract by featuring that subset of harmonics of the fundamental 
frequency that populate those “hills” with an overall spectral sloping of its 
frequency peaks.

overwhelm the comprehension needed for practical application. However, 
since all acoustic transitions of range are predictable by the intervallic 
relationship between the several lower harmonics of the sung pitch and 
the /f_irst resonance of the vowel being sung, those transitions can be clearly 
and simply charted. Both the /f_irst resonance:/f_irst formant locations of 
vowels and the lowest three intervals of the harmonic series of a pitch are 
easily learnable and mappable, informing the attention and awareness of 
both teacher and singer (Figure 4.2).

Resonances and Formants
Vocal Tract
!e vocal tract is a tube that is open at one end (lips) and mostly closed 
at the other (glottis). As mentioned above, that makes it a quarter-wave 
resonator, with a primary resonance or standing wave at the frequency of 
a sound wave that is four times as long as the vocal tract. Such resonators 
also have resonances at higher frequencies, whose standing wavelengths 
within the vocal tract are odd-numbered multiples of one-fourth: 3/4, 
5/4, 7/4, and so on, all of which have nodal and anti-nodal segments 
at opposite ends of the tube ( Figure 4.1). Hard-walled resonators—like 
brass instruments—will only resonate partials at rather speci/f_ic, narrowed 
frequency locations. Being a so/f_t-walled, shapeable tube, the vocal tract 
has resonances with frequency bandwidths that are wider and less sharply 
tuned than other quarter-wave instruments. !is enables the vocal tract 
to reinforce source partials that are merely in the vicinity of its resonance 
frequency peaks. Frequencies that fall midway between widely spaced 
vocal tract resonances will be signi/f_icantly weakened, if not suppressed 
altogether.
Resonances and Formants
Since the vocal tract is a shapeable, non-uniform tube, its natural resonances 
are tunable—within limits—to wavelengths other than those listed above. 
!ese vocal tract resonances  are called formants in much voice science 
and pedagogic literature, treating the two terms as synonymous. Formants 
PRAA.indb   27PRAA.indb   27 7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  28
Resonances and Formants
are actually the spectral peaks of the radiated sound, re&ecting how vocal 
tract resonances have /f_iltered voice source frequencies. Tube resonances 
(the potential of the tube to /f_ilter and transfer sound from the glottis to 
the lips) and formants (the actual spectral peaks of the resultant, radiated 
sound) would only be identical if the resonance peaks are tuned precisely 
to periodicity-reinforced harmonics, or conversely, if harmonics land 
precisely at resonance peaks. !e harmonics of low pitches, whose many 
closely spaced harmonics fall within the lower resonances of the vocal 
tract, su#ciently populate them, so that resonance peaks and radiated 
formant peaks are essentially the same. (See Figure 5.1.) At higher sung 
pitches, potential source harmonics are widely spaced relative to vocal 
tract resonances, and unless the vocal tract is retuned via active vowel 
modi/f_ication, there may be no harmonic at or near a resonance peak. In 
this case, radiated formants vary signi/f_icantly from vocal tract resonances. 
Indeed, there may be rather more potential vocal tract resonances than 
the realized, perceived radiated formants. (See Figure 5.2). !is becomes 
signi/f_icant at higher sung pitches. !is text will herea/f_ter use these terms 
in their narrower de/f_inition for maximal clarity: resonances will refer to 
the sound transfer potential of the vocal tract, and formants will refer to 
the realized spectral peaks in the actual, resultant sound as radiated and 
heard.

Resonance and Formant Locations
!ere are typically only three to /f_ive resonances low enough in frequency 
and resultant formants strong enough in intensity to be of great aural 
signi/f_icance for Western classical singing. Higher spectral content does 
make an aural contribution, somewhat to treble voices and especially to 
many popular genres. !e frequency locations of resonances are the result 
of the length and shape of the vocal tract/tube (Titze, 1994).  Tube length 
determines the general location of the entire set of resonances:
 z the longer the tube, the lower the frequencies of the set of resonances, 
resulting in more radiated formants that are aurally prominent in 
classical genre.
 z the shorter the tube, the higher and more widely spaced the 
frequencies of the set of resonances, resulting in fewer radiated 
formants that are aurally prominent in classical genre.
 z Resonances and spectral content higher than 5000 Hz are especially 
present in the brighter, more speech-like timbres of CCM vocal genres.
Since where the resonances lie depends primarily upon tube length, and 
their resultant formants determine overall vocal timbre, then tube length

is a signi/f_icant factor in determining and maintaining the vocal category

Number of Resonances and 
Formants
Vocal tract resonances are theoretically in/f_inite in number, but only three 
to /f_ive (depending upon voice type or vocal tract length) are of great 
timbral signi/f_icance, falling within the heart of strongest audibility range 
(roughly, the pitch range of the keyboard, below ca. 4200 Hz, and within 
the spectral tone color range that diﬀerentiates vowels, from just below C5 
to just above C7; ca. 400–2100 Hz). 1 Within the keyboard range, basses 
typically have /f_ive resonances, sopranos three, and most everyone else 
four. In scienti/f_ic studies, resonance frequency peaks are notated fR1, fR2, fR3,  
for “the frequency of resonance number one, the frequency of resonance 
two, the frequency of resonance three. ” Formant frequency peaks are 
numbered F1, F2, F3, read as “the /f_irst formant, the second formant, the 
third formant. ” For simplicity, the former can be referred to as the /f_irst 
resonance, second resonance, the third resonance, and the latter, the /f_irst 
formant, second formant, the third formant.

The Roles Resonances and 
Formants Play
Vowel Formants
!e /f_irst two vocal tract resonances are both the most responsive to 
perturbations of the vocal tract shape and also cover the spectral tone 
PRAA.indb   30PRAA.indb   30 7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM7/10/2025   4:38:49 PM

color frequencies needed for vowels. !us, they are the most available, 
tunable, and responsible for diﬀerentiating and de/f_ining vowels. !eir 
resultant radiated formants are therefore referred to as the vowel 
formants. Vocal tract resonances are tuned primarily by adjusting where 
and to what extent a bulge or hump of the tongue internally narrows the 
vocal tract and by the size of the tube exit (jaw and mouth opening and lip 
shape). An internal narrowing nearer to the front (nearer the lips) causes 
a lower /f_irst resonance and a higher second resonance. A narrowing 
nearer the back causes a higher /f_irst resonance and a lower second 
resonance. Lip rounding lowers both resonances, especially the /f_irst, and 
lip spreading raises both resonances, especially the /f_irst. Furthermore, 
the degree of mouth opening and tongue dorsum height dominate the 
tuning of fR1 (opening the mouth and/or lowering the dorsum raises 
fR1) and the tongue dorsum location along and closeness to the palate 
dominate the tuning of fR2 (a fronted dorsum/palatal narrowing raises fR2 
and a backed and/or lowered dorsum narrowing lowers fR2). !is tuning 
of the shape of the vocal tract is generally referenced in speech literature 
as articulation. It is more simply motivated by expression and auditory 
targets (composite over- a n d  under-vowel2 tone color content) than 
by attempting micromanagement of the biomechanics of articulation, 
though some intentional awareness of the latter can be helpful. For 
example, awareness of tongue dorsum height and location relative to 
the palate can initially assist in discovering and approximating the best 
resonance of a vowel, though expression plus intended timbral targets 
and somatosense will eventually tune the system with greater speci/f_icity 
(Figure 5.3).
