Vowel Modiﬁcation
Moving the /f_irst two radiated formants around by reshaping the vocal tract 
amounts to adjusting or even changing the vowel category, retuning its 
resonances, ideally while preserving vocal tract length for overall timbral 
unity. !is is usually what is meant by the term vowel modi/f_ication. Since 
this involves intentional reshaping of the vocal tract from habituated 
speech postures,  this might more accurately be called active vowel 
modi/f_ication, since vowel timbre also necessarily migrates with pitch 
change alone, that is, without shape change. (More later.)
The Roles of the First Formant
!e /f_irst formant contributes to depth, fullness, and roundness of 
timbre, and determines the openness-closeness dimension of vowels. 
Furthermore, as we will learn, all acoustic (as distinct from laryngeal) 
registration events are caused by or related to interactions between the 
/f_irst vocal tract resonance and voice source harmonics moving through it. 
A few signi/f_icant acoustic phenomena also involve the second vocal tract 
resonance.
Depth or Fullness of Timbre
!e /f_irst formant, being the lowest resonance of a voice, is most responsible 
for depth and fullness of timbre, that is, the strength of lower harmonics 
in the sound (also described as warmth or roundness). First formants 
lie between ca. C4 and A5, usually in contact with the treble clef (D4-
G5), in the spectral tone color range of [u], [o], and the beginning of [ ɔ]. 
!erefore, the /f_irst resonance of the vocal tract adds a “warming” [~u] or 
[~o]-like tone color to the composite vowel percept. !e /f_irst resonances 
and formants of any given voice typically vary by about an octave across 
all vowels, from the lowest of an [i] vowel to the highest /f_irst formant of 
an [ɑ]. It is necessary to have at least one source harmonic near (within 
the bandwidth of) the /f_irst resonance frequency peak in order to be fully 
PRAA.indb   32PRAA.indb   32 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
 Resonances and Formants 33
resonant and to have timbral depth. !is becomes especially important 
for the upper range of treble voices. If no source harmonic is being 
ampli/f_ied by the /f_irst resonance, the tone will lack fullness and become 
thin or shrill, being dominated by the bright spectral content featured 
by higher formants. A functional exception to this is the whistle register, 
which is typically much less full in timbre anyway, though in some singers 
remarkably resonant. In the latter case, the fundamental or /f_irst harmonic 
is probably being resonated by a clustering of resonances one and two, or 
possibly just by the second resonance. It is useful to know that the lower 
the frequency of the /f_irst resonance peak relative to the vowel being sung, 
the more settled and unconstricted the throat (laryngopharynx) and 
convergent the resonator is likely to be.
Openness-Closeness Dimension of 
Vowels
!e frequency of the /f_irst formant is also correlated with the openness 
or closeness  of a vowel. Close vowels  such as [i] or [u] have low /f_irst 
formants. Open vowels such as [ ɛ], [ɔ], or [ ɑ] have high /f_irst formants. 
!erefore, the higher the /f_irst formant, the more open the vowel; the lower 
the /f_irst formant, the closer the vowel. As with colors, in which a range of 
frequencies is identi/f_ied by a single color designation (red, for example), 
so it also is with vowels. Within a single vowel identity, the higher the 
/f_irst formant tuning, the more open the pronunciation will sound, and the 
lower the /f_irst formant, the closer the pronunciation. As the /f_irst formant 
moves up or down, at some point the perceived vowel identity may shi/f_t  
to that of a neighboring vowel, just as shades of red at some point morph 
into a shade of orange or of purple. For example, raising the /f_irst formant 
of an [i] eventually may shi/f_t its timbre to [ɪ], and of an [e] to an [ ɛ], or 
vice versa if lowering the /f_irst formant, especially when this also involves 
some retuning of the second formant, whose frequency, for most vowels, 
is critical in determining the vowel-de/f_ining target spectral tone color 
being contributed. !e /f_irst resonance and its resultant formant are raised 
by mouth opening and tongue dorsum lowering. Some independence of 
/f_irst and second formant tuning is possible, but the /f_irst formant target 
frequency can only be raised so far by mouth opening without resorting 
PRAA.indb   33PRAA.indb   33 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  34
to tongue dorsum lowering. Tongue dorsum lowering will also lower the 
second resonance and its formant.
Harmonic Interactions with the 
First Resonance
Whenever a source harmonic passes through the /f_irst vocal tract resonance 
there is an audible eﬀect (some degree of closing or opening of the timbre 
with an accompanying, simultaneous passive vowel modi/f_ication or 
migration), which is perceivable as an acoustic registration phenomenon. 
!ese harmonic:resonance crossings and their acoustic eﬀects are o/f_ten 
instinctively avoided by raising the /f_irst resonance, an innate response that 
must be trained away for classical singing timbre (more later). All acoustic 
registration events have primarily to do with harmonic interactions with 
the /f_irst resonance of the vocal tract.3 It is therefore pedagogically essential 
to know the /f_irst formant locations of the cardinal vowels and the general 
frequency location contour of the entire /f_irst formant set (see Figure 6.4, 
p. 50 and Appendix 2: Approximate First Formant Locations by Voice, p. 179).
The Roles of the Second Formant
!e second formant plays a role in the vowel de/f_ining spectral tone color 
for most vowels, the front-back dimension of vowels (due to a relative 
brightness factor), and in boosting brilliance in upper voice resonance 
strategies of tenors and baritones, as well as in middle voice resonance 
strategies of treble voices and treble voice belters.
Vowel Clarity/Deﬁnition
Although both /f_irst and second formants participate in vowel timbre, the 
second formant is perceptually more responsible for clarity of de/f_inition 
and the actual spectral tone color of most vowels.  !is is perhaps due to 
PRAA.indb   34PRAA.indb   34 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
 Resonances and Formants 35
its innate (though mistaken) perceptual association with the oral cavity, 
with the fact that the second formant varies more consistently across 
all vowels than the /f_irst formant, whose location is duplicated across 
the front and back vowels, and with the fact that the spectral tone color 
frequencies of cardinal vowels other than [u] and [o] lie within second 
formant range. !e frequency peaks of the second resonances of the vocal 
tract lie between ca. F5 and D7, and mostly between G5 and C7. Within 
a given voice the second resonance spans the interval of about a 10th. 
Since this is the spectral tone color range of [ ɔ ɑ a æ ɛ e i], the radiated 
formants of the second resonance de/f_ine these vowels. For these reasons it 
is an instinctive though poor resonance strategy to exaggerate oral shape 
diﬀerences to improve clarity of diction. Excessive orality is usually at odds 
with chiaroscuro balanced resonance (page 38 below) and with bene/f_icial 
interactive feedback of resonance to vocal fold vibration. !e perceptual 
dominance of the second formant in vowel identity is also apparent in 
onomatopoeic sound associations: words descriptive of high sounds (such 
as tweet, scream, sizzle), o/f_ten contain vowels with high-frequency second 
formants, while words descriptive of mid (pound, shout) and low (moan, 
groan, coo) sounds contain vowels with mid and low-frequency second 
formants.
Front-Back Designation of Vowels
!e second formant is also responsible for the perceived front or back 
designation of a vowel. Front vowels, such as [i], have a high second formant 
while back vowels, such as [u], have a low second formant. As mentioned 
above, second formants lie approximately between F5 and D7, starting 
just above the treble clef, and vary about a tenth across all vowels within a 
given voice. !e front/back percept is due to the relative brightness of its 
frequency peak (higher is brighter, lower is darker) and the proximity of the 
second formant to either the /f_irst formant (as in [u]) or third formant (as in 
[i]). Proximate formants are mutually reinforcing. Like party animals, when 
they get together, they make more noise! !erefore, a low second formant 
would reinforce the /f_irst formant, boosting the low-frequency components 
of the timbre, adding more warming [~u] or [~o]-like spectral tone color to 
the composite vowel percept), and creating a “back” vowel percept. A high 
PRAA.indb   35PRAA.indb   35 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  36
second formant would reinforce the singer’s formant cluster, boosting 
the brighter, treble [~e] to [~i]-like spectral components of the timbre, 
creating a “front” vowel percept. Front-back dimension corresponds also 
to tongue shape: a fronted tongue creates a smaller frontal space and a 
higher second formant, while a less fronted tongue creates a narrowing 
nearer the back of the oral cavity and possibly a lower second formant. 
It is a universal, innate percept to sense bright as being in front and dark 
in back. !is percept leads to an unsatisfactory, binary, either/or extreme 
of timbral opposition. Counterintuitively, the front-back tuning of vowels 
does not correspond to their locational somatosense (“placement” percept 
or sensation of vibrational locus) in best vocal function. Resonance tuning 
is more eﬀectively balanced by conceptually reversing this orientation to 
sensing bright tone colors in back and dark in front.
Second Formant Strategies
!e second formant also plays a prominent role in the upper voice 
resonance strategy for a signi/f_icant percentage of premier non-treble 
singers, especially tenors. When the second harmonic of the voice source 
loses power as it surpasses the frequency peak of the /f_irst resonance—
that is, when the voice “turns over” or “closes”—professional tenors and 
baritones will o/f_ten modify resonances in order to boost the second 
formant by tuning the second resonance to activate an available, higher 
source harmonic in order to achieve an especially ringing top (Donald 
Miller 2008). A similar strategy is used by some classical treble voices in 
their middle range and by treble voice belters to extend a mixed belt timbre 
higher. (More on this later: see Second Formant Strategies Revisited, p. 54.)
Singer’s Formant Cluster (SFC)
Higher resonances (three and higher: ( fR3) can be organized with certain 
shape conditions (a settled larynx, a relatively open laryngopharynx, and 
a narrowed epilaryngeal exit) to create a cluster of radiated formants. 
!is forms the so-called singer’s formant cluster (SFC) , that ringing 
quality associated with professional classical singers that gives them 
carrying power over orchestras (Sundberg 1974; Titze and Story 1997). 
PRAA.indb   36PRAA.indb   36 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
 Resonances and Formants 37
It is most characteristic of non-treble voices, but also of treble voices in 
their middle and lower registers, that is, for sung pitches below ca. D5. 
!e singer’s formant cluster lies within the top octave of the piano (C7 
to C8), usually in the middle of that octave (2300–3500 Hz.) where the 
human ear is very sensitive to loudness.  !is is because the human ear 
canal is also a quarter-wave resonator whose primary resonance is in the 
2000–4000 Hz range, where healthy human hearing is most sensitive to 
intensity. Any frequencies entering the ear canal within that range will 
receive a strong resonance boost on the way to the eardrum. Once a sung 
pitch itself is su#ciently high relative to human hearing sensitivity, vocal 
loudness becomes less dependent upon the singer’s formant cluster and 
increasingly dependent upon the strength of the fundamental frequency 
being resonated by the /f_irst resonance and featured as the /f_irst formant 
(whoop timbre). For this reason, the singer’s formant cluster plays a 
decreasing role in the upper registers of treble voices.
Relationship of the SFC to Vocal Fach
!e frequency location of the singer’s formant cluster is primarily 
determined by the length of the vocal tract, and is signi/f_icant for overall 
timbre and thus for vocal Fach (voice category) determination. In general, 
the longer the tube, the lower the SFC, and the lower the resultant vocal 
Fach; the shorter the tube, the higher the SFC and Fach. Keeping tube 
length relatively stable across range and vowels is therefore a key factor in 
unifying and stabilizing the timbre and consistency of the perceived Fach 
of the voice in Western classical genre. !is is somewhat less of a concern 
in CCM (Contemporary Commercial Music) genres.
Relationship of the SFC to 
Resonance Balance and 
Interactivity
Balanced resonance involves some degree of parity between the strength of 
high and low partials of the radiated sound, at least for pitches below ca. D5. 
!is is usually accomplished by a balance between the depth-responsible 
PRAA.indb   37PRAA.indb   37 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  38
/f_irst formant and the ring-responsible singer’s formant cluster. According 
to some studies, the creation of the singer’s formant cluster requires a six 
to one diﬀerential between the area of the pharynx and the outlet of the 
epilarynx (Sundberg 1974; Titze and Story 1997). !is condition is only 
met when the larynx is comfortably low, the laryngopharynx relaxed 
open, and the aryepiglottic exit narrowed. !ose very conditions create a 
convergent resonator, a relatively lower /f_irst formant, and ensure timbral 
depth. !is means that the consistency of the singer’s formant cluster is 
depth-dependent. !at is to say, classic SFC is not just about achieving 
“ring, ” but actually only occurs when accompanied by timbral depth, 
creating the balanced resonance of  chiaroscuro. While a voice may have 
strong higher formants and be “bright” in timbre, it does not have classic 
singer’s formant cluster unless the higher formants are drawn together by 
an accompanying depth. In this way SFC creates a situation of acoustic 
reactance and inertance, interactivity factors that can result in more 
overall acoustic power for less breath pressure or glottal resistance. Other 
genres’ forms of high spectral content (pharyngeal voice, twang, belting, 
etc.) involve various pharyngeal narrowing strategies. (Obert 2019). !ere 
is also newer evidence that the SFC is additionally enhanced by a variety 
of micro-adjustments along the length of the vocal tract (Story 2016).
E/uniFB00ect of the Piriform Sinuses
!e piriform sinuses, spaces which branch oﬀ to the sides of the epilarynx, 
have the eﬀect of negating resonance in the 4000–6000 Hz region, thereby 
highlighting the singer’s formant cluster and suppressing formants that 
might lie just above it (Delvaux and Howard 2014). Narrowing the 
laryngopharynx closes the piriform sinuses, essentially “hugging” the 
epi-laryngeal tube, and boosting a wide spread of high-frequency source 
partials without clustering formants. !is resonance strategy is routinely 
used as one option by various genres other than classical that desire a very 
bright timbre without complementary depth, but may also be a practice of 
some classical singers. It is a way to create the so-called pharyngeal voice 
or voce di strega (witch voice) quality.

Vocal Tract Sound Transfer 
Characteristics
As shown earlier in Figure 3.1, p. 12, a power spectrum displays power 
on the vertical and frequency on the horizontal. A spectral envelope  
outlining the resonance characteristics of a particular vocal tract shape 
would then display that vocal tract’s ability to transfer acoustic energy 
(sound) from the glottis to the outside world (Figures 5.4 and 5.5).
Formants Relative to the Keyboard
If the frequency dimension of a power spectrum is adjusted to be 
logarithmic, like pitch on a piano keyboard, the relative locations of its 
resonances and their formants are more easily grasped by musicians. 
Figure 5.5  shows the same vowel as Figure 5.4 , (an [i]), adjusted to a 
logarithmic scale. !e high location of the second resonance puts all 
resonances other than the /f_irst near or in the top octave of the piano, 
leaving a large “valley” between the /f_irst and second formants of the [i]. 
!is explains its relative “brightness” but also its potential thinness if no

Practical Vocal Acoustics
6
Harmonic:Resonance 
Interactions
Vocal resonance is the result of the interactions between voice source 
partials (frequencies) and vocal tract resonances. In general, we want 
approximate matches between our resonance tunings and those 
harmonics of the pitch being sung that are available for resonation. 
Harmonics may lose power more rapidly on the high side of a resonance 
frequency peak due to reactance (Titze). Since we have no control over 
the frequency location of the harmonics (they are determined by the pitch 
we are required to sing), the main factor that we can aﬀect in order to 
be resonant is the frequency location (tuning) of the /f_irst two vocal tract 
resonances and their resultant radiated formants. !eir tuning results 
from our vowel conception and intention and from expressive, aﬀective 
motivation. !e Western classical singer will want the frequency location 
of the clustered higher formants to be as stable as possible for consistency 
of overall timbre and Fach. However, the /f_irst two resonances can be tuned 
to amplify available harmonics when necessary.
Harmonic:First Resonance 
Interactions
While interaction between source-reinforced harmonics and all 
resonances is important, interactions with the /f_irst resonance are 
especially signi/f_icant. If no harmonics land within the bandwidth of the 
PRAA.indb   43PRAA.indb   43 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  44
Harmonic:Resonance Interactions
/f_irst resonance, ideally below its frequency peak, the overall sound will 
be weaker and thinner. !ere needs to be at least one harmonic near that 
peak to be activated and resonated by it in order to have fullness and 
depth. If there are two or more harmonics below the /f_irst resonance, that 
is, when singing lower pitches, there is little need for resonance/ formant 
tuning, since, in that situation there will usually be plenty of harmonics 
available for resonation by the /f_irst, second, and higher resonances. With 
higher fundamental frequencies, harmonics are more widely spaced 
relative to resonance frequencies, leaving fewer harmonics within the 
bandwidths of the /f_irst two resonances to be featured. !erefore, more 
attention to resonance/formant tuning becomes necessary when singing 
higher pitches.
Possible Resonance:Harmonic 
Interactions
!ere are four possible interactions between resonances and harmonics: 
(1) a resonance can allow a moving harmonic to pass through it (opening 
or closing the timbre); (2) a resonance can move across a stable harmonic 
(a change of vowel and possibly of acoustic register on the same pitch); 
(3) a resonance can be steadily retuned to track a moving harmonic (as in 
yelling, belting, or whooping), sometimes called formant tracking; and 
(4) a resonance can be “detuned” away from a harmonic (by raising fR1 
to stay above the sung pitch to remain in close timbre and avoid whoop 
timbre on close vowels by non-treble voices or belters; or by reducing 
higher formant clustering as a strategy in the upper treble voice range to 
eliminate the shrillness eﬀect of strong higher formants).
Timbral Opening and Closing
Whenever a source harmonic passes through and above the /f_irst resonance, 
there is an aural eﬀect described as a perceptual closing of the timbre. 
Whenever a harmonic drops below the /f_irst resonance, the timbre can be 
heard to open to some degree.
PRAA.indb   44PRAA.indb   44 7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM7/10/2025   4:38:50 PM
 Harmonic:Resonance Interactions 45
Open Timbre
When two or more harmonics lie at or below the /f_irst resonance, the 
timbre is described as open timbre  ( voce aperta  in Italian); the more 
harmonics below the /f_irst resonance, the more open the timbre. In other 
words, open timbre occurs when singing an octave or more below the /f_irst 
resonance of the vowel being sung. Open timbre might be described as 
bright, straightforward, clear, fresh, exposed, coming more directly out of 
the mouth, and—if low enough in pitch—buzzy. If unskillfully taken too 
high, open timbre can become blatant or spread as in a raw yell. Belting 
is a skillful variation of open timbre at higher pitches.  When the /f_irst 
resonance is tuned to the second harmonic, or conversely, as the second 
harmonic approaches the peak frequency of the /f_irst resonance, an fR1:2fo 
acoustic coupling occurs, which forms an especially strong form of open 
timbre, characterized by ringing clarity and power from enhanced higher 
spectral content. !is is such a strong acoustic coupling that we o/f_ten 
instinctively adjust for it when power and projection are needed. Novice 
singers tend to track this higher into yell timbre by an increasingly open, 
divergent vocal tract shaping.
Yell Coupling
If this fR1:2fo open timbre coupling is maintained or tracked above the 
normal fR1 location of the vowel being sung by raising the /f_irst resonance 
in tandem with the rising second harmonic, the singer has moved into yell 
timbre. !is is  a valuable resonance strategy when needed in life and is 
quite common in musical theater, popular, folk, and world music idioms, 
but yell timbre is avoided in Western classical singing. !ese non-classical 
vocal styles can be said to exploit the expressive power and “naturalness” 
of more or less skillful yelling. !e ability to yell is apparently an inherited, 
universal, and strong survival instinct. Overcoming this instinct is crucial 
in training young non-treble voices to negotiate their passaggi successfully 
with Western classical timbre. On the contrary, carefully managing an 
e#cient, skillful form of it is crucial to training musical theater belt timbre 
(Figure 6.1).

Practical Vocal Acoustics  46
Yell Characteristics
Y ell timbre and function are 
accomplished by raising the /f_irst 
resonance through tube shortening 
(larynx raising), pharynx narrowing, 
and mouth widening, that is, by 
creating a divergent resonator shape. 
!e /f_irst two of these maneuvers 
are accomplished by activating 
swallowing muscles which raise 
and constrict the laryngopharynx, 
narrowing the throat, preventing the 
use of the classically described singer’s 
formant cluster and chiaroscuro 
timbre and in&uencing the vibrator 
function toward pressed phonation. Furthermore, yelling is typically done 
on open vowels like [a] or [ ɛ], because their high /f_irst formant locations 
and inherently more divergent shapes make them more compatible with 
the acoustic requirements of the yell (try yelling on a pure [u]!). !e yell is 
chest register (TA) or mechanism one dominant. !ese maneuvers result 
in the raised and forward-reaching chin of the young biological male 
accommodating a rising larynx, plus a widened vowel posture.

Close Timbre
When the second harmonic crosses su#ciently above the /f_irst vocal tract 
resonance, the timbre is heard to close or turn over. !is crossing is the 
primary acoustic registration event of non-treble (bass, baritone, tenor) 
voices. Close timbre ( voce chiusa 1 in Italian) is variously described 
as seeming domed, tipped, smoother, rounder, more collected, or 
concentrated, above the palate or higher in sensation in the head, and 
somewhat less direct or exposed (covered). !is occurs when the sung 
pitch is less than an octave below the /f_irst resonance of the vowel being 
sung. It is a necessary component of Western classical upper voice 
training since, unlike other vocal styles, it enables the non-treble upper 
range and treble middle range to occur without loss of timbral depth or 
warmth. Turning over is accomplished by retaining depth (tube length), 
vowel shape, and relative vowel closeness—all of which maintain 
the /f_irst resonance frequency location—until the second harmonic 
has surpassed it. It is accompanied by some degree of passive vowel 
modi/f_ication or migration, a change in the vowel quality that is the 
result of maintaining the tube shape while the pitch and its harmonics 
move, in contrast to active modi/f_ication, which involves a deliberate 
shape change with a resultant retuning of resonances and resultant 
formants.
Whoop Coupling
When the fundamental frequency: 1fo (equivalent to the pitch one 
is singing) reaches the frequency of the /f_irst resonance, fR1, there is a 
strong fR1:1fo coupling. !is is called whooping 2 or hooting (D. Miller) 
and is characterized by a “hootier” full, round, deep timbre. Whoop 
timbre is dominated by the fundamental frequency; therefore, while 
resonant, it is smoother and less brassy than open timbre. It may seem 
more centralized in the head and darkly frontal, being dominated by 
the [~u, ~o, ~ɔ]-like tone color of the /f_irst harmonic boosted by the /f_irst 
resonance.
PRAA.indb   47PRAA.indb   47 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  48
Whoop Characteristics
Whooping is accomplished by tuning 
the first resonance to the 1fo, that is, 
to the pitch being sung. It is falsetto 
(CT) or mechanism two  dominant, 
tends toward flow phonation, 
facilitates laryngopharyngeal space, 
and a convergent (open to the back, 
narrower to the front) resonator shape. 
It is primarily done on close vowels, 
especially [u], because their low first 
resonance frequency locations facilitate 
fR1:1fo coupling (it is difficult for most 
non-treble voices to whoop on open 
vowels unless very high). Since treble 
voices eventually sing pitches at or 
above the first formants of all of their 
vowels, they are often operating in 
whoop mode, especially in Western classical singing ( Figure 6.2 ). 
Figure 6.2  The fR1:1fo coupling of whoop timbre, very characteristic of

PRAA.indb   48PRAA.indb   48 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
 Harmonic:Resonance Interactions 49
Yell and Whoop Frequency
!ese two resonance strategies are inherently strong acoustically and 
seem to occur innately and universally among humans. For example, 
when desiring to make a lot of sound at sporting events or enthusiastic 
celebrations, humans either yell or whoop. If done to excess, either can 
cause hyper-functional wear. However, whooping is the lower pressure, 
lower risk, vocally healthier of the two options. Cultural acceptability 
is another matter. “Bravo!” in whoop mode is not likely to catch on. 
Either strategy can be skillfully adapted for aesthetic, artistic use, taking 
advantage of their expressive associations “in the wild. ”
Turning Over
!e most prominent and pedagogically relevant of these various 
harmonic:resonance interactions for non-treble voices is the crossing 
of the second harmonic (2 fo) above the /f_irst resonance (fR1). !is is the 
primary acoustic register transition of biological male voices, and at least 
for the open vowels, has been referred to as vocal cover, closing, or turning 
over. It is associated with the laryngeal shi/f_t from what has historically been 
called chest to head voice (actually chest to middle/mixed registration, i.e., 
a lighter version of laryngeal mechanism one) (Figure 6.3). As mentioned 
before, this transition also generates a timbral shi/f_t that could be described 
Figure 6.3 Power spectrum of turning over in which the second harmonic 
(2fo) has just surpassed the ﬁrst resonance peak.
PRAA.indb   49PRAA.indb   49 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  50
as passive vowel modi/f_ication or migration,  since it causes a subtle closure 
and rounding of the vowel timbre. !is modi/f_ication is accomplished not 
through a shape change, but through changing relationships between 
moving source harmonics and stable vocal tract resonances.
The Pitch of Turning
!e pitch of turning  from open to close timbre is determined by the 
location of the /f_irst resonance of the vowel being sung, which is aﬀected by 
how openly or closely the vowel is being pronounced. !e pitch of turning 
is slightly less than an octave below the /f_irst resonance of the vowel, since 
1fo is an octave below 2 fo. !at is, when you are singing a given pitch, 
its second harmonic is an octave higher. Tones sung below the pitch of 
turning for a given vowel (an octave or more below the /f_irst resonance of 
that vowel) will be in open timbre. Notes above the pitch of turning for 
that vowel (less than an octave below the /f_irst resonance of that vowel) will 
be in close timbre. If turning over were the result of laryngeal registration 
events rather than /f_irst resonance locations, one would expect a voice to 
turn over at the same pitch for all vowels. It does not. Maintaining that 
view is no longer a viable pedagogic position (Figure 6.4). 
Figure 6.4  Approximate ﬁrst formant locations of the cardinal vowels 
(modeled on the staff in a musician user-friendly way) for a bass voice. 
Each vowel formant (containing its IPA symbol) is placed in a box on the 
treble clef, with the pitches at which the voice would turn over or close 
notated on the bass clef below each vowel. Note again the overall contour 
of the set of vowel formants. This can be adjusted up or down for other 
voice categories. From highest to lowest voice category, formant locations 
only vary by about a fourth or ﬁfth with average high soprano formants 
spanning approximately G4-G5 and deep bass formants approximately 
C4-C5 (see Appendix 2, p. 179).

 Harmonic:Resonance Interactions 51
As mentioned above,  open vowels (like [ ɑ]) have high first 
formants. Close vowels (like [i] or [u]) have low first formants. The 
more openly you pronounce a vowel, the higher its first resonance 
will be, and the higher it will turn over. The more closely you 
pronounce a vowel, the lower its first resonance will be, and the lower 
it will turn over. The ability to hear the turning of the voice, knowledge 
and anticipation of the locations and sounds of turning, and the 
willingness and ability to allow that timbral shift to occur, all are 
extremely helpful in training non-treble voice upper passaggio  and 
treble voice lower passaggio .
Madde Exploration 4: Open and Close Timbre
From Turning to Whoop
!ere is an octave between the /f_irst and second harmonics of a sung 
pitch. !erefore, if tube length and shape are kept constant—in other 
words, if the frequency location of the /f_irst vocal tract resonance is kept 
constant—there will be just under an octave between turning over into 
close timbre, where the second harmonic rises above the /f_irst resonance, 
and arrival at full whoop timbre, where the /f_irst harmonic matches the 
/f_irst resonance. An acoustic challenge is posed by the octave separation 
between the /f_irst and second harmonics: immediately above the turn, 
where the second harmonic begins to drop in power as it relinquishes 
the advantage of fR1 resonation, the /f_irst harmonic is still too far below 
the /f_irst resonance to match the power the second harmonic  just lost. 
!is loss in power can be exacerbated—especially in treble voices—by 
an earlier shi/f_t in laryngeal registration toward vibrational mechanism 
two, with its greater CT (head voice) dominance, weaker glottal 
resistance, and steeper roll-oﬀ in intensity of upper partials. To address 
this challenge various strategies can be used to strengthen glottal closure 
competence across the laryngeal register transition, and/or to boost 
overall resonance by shi/f_ting to a second resonance ampli/f_ication of a 
higher harmonic.
PRAA.indb   51PRAA.indb   51 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
Practical Vocal Acoustics  52
Resonance Strategies between 
Turning and Whoop
!ere are three basic /f_irst resonance options in this transitional range:

1) active vowel modi/f_ication that lowers the /f_irst resonance to 
   precipitate whoop timbre sooner;
2) maintaining tube shape and /f_irst resonance tuning, allowing a 
   gradual migration into whoop timbre across the octave divide; and
3) active vowel modi/f_ication that raises the /f_irst resonance to 
   postpone or avoid whoop timbre.
   Overlapping with these /f_irst resonance strategies in this transitional register 
   from turning to whoop are second resonance strategies: it is precisely here 
   that coupling the second vocal tract resonance with a higher harmonic is 
   o/f_ten sought to maintain or boost intensity.
   Lowering the First Resonance 
   through Active Vowel 
   Modiﬁcation
   If a closer vowel, a closer pronunciation of the same vowel, or some 
   other tube shape adjusting, timbral deepening strategy (such as tube 
   lengthening) is used that maximizes resonator convergence for the vowel 
   being sung, the /f_irst resonance will be lowered. !is will cause a lower 
   pitch transition into full whoop timbre (an earlier fR1:1fo match). In other 
   words, it will reduce the interval between turning and whooping to rather 
   less than an octave. !is is a common strategy of treble voices (altos, 
   mezzos, sopranos, and countertenors) in Western classical singing, where 
   the opulent, smoother head voice of whoop timbre is desirable throughout 
   most of the range.
   PRAA.indb   52PRAA.indb   52 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
   Harmonic:Resonance Interactions 53
   Maintaining Vocal Tract Shape
   Maintaining vocal tract length and vowel closeness (and thus /f_irst 
   resonance frequency location) when ascending above a vowel’s point 
   of turning will result in a gradually closer, headier timbre until full 
   whoop timbre is achieved about an octave higher. As the fundamental 
   frequency (1 fo) ascends into the peak of the /f_irst resonance and begins 
   to dominate the timbre, tonal sensations may seem increasingly internal 
   and centralized, since whooping is generally felt in the head and vertical 
   pharyngeal column. However, in the lower range of this acoustic register, 
   overall intensity may need assistance from the second formant featuring a 
   higher harmonic or from a clustering of the /f_irst and second resonances, 
   amplifying the second harmonic that lies between them. If appropriate for 
   the voice type and musical style, intensity may also be helped by retaining 
   a more robust (mechanism one, “chestier”) laryngeal registration above 
   the turn or other source or resonance strategies that achieve a longer 
   closed phase of the vocal fold oscillation. !e longer the vocal fold closure 
   during each oscillation, the more, higher partials there will be in the 
   radiated tone.
   Raising the First Resonance 
   through Vowel Opening
   Alternatively, once the voice has turned over into close timbre, the vowel 
   shape can be gradually opened, starting a step or two above the point of 
   turning while staying within close timbre. !is strategy will postpone 
   arrival in full whoop timbre and retain a turned over, yet more robust, 
   buzzier timbral mix. It involves raising the /f_irst resonance by opening 
   the vowel shape in a manner that keeps fR1 positioned well above the 
   pitch being sung, avoiding full whoop timbre, but still below the second 
   harmonic, avoiding reverting to open or yell timbre. !is approach 
   eﬀectively lengthens the pitch interval between turning over and whoop 
   timbre to more than an octave, in some cases avoiding arrival into whoop 
   PRAA.indb   53PRAA.indb   53 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  54
   coupling altogether. As the vowel is opened to raise the /f_irst resonance, 
   it will be important for Western classical timbre that tube length—and 
   timbral depth—not be compromised by larynx raising. Tube shortening is 
   a viable alternative for some CCM genres that do not similarly prioritize 
   timbral depth.
   As mentioned above, in Western classical singing, trained treble voices 
   prefer the /f_irst two strategies, leaving vowels close or even closing them 
   more in order to sound headier and fuller—to reach full whoop timbre—
   lower in the range. Non-treble voices tend to prefer the third strategy, 
   opening the vowel somewhat above its point of turning, especially on 
   close vowels, in order not to proceed into falsetto/whoop timbre. Many 
   premier non-treble singers also seek out second resonance:higher 
   harmonic couplings to boost the virile ring of the voice for more exciting, 
   professional operatic top notes. A version of this strategy is also used in 
   belt mix to extend its characteristic timbre above ca. B4.
   Second Formant Strategies 
   Revisited
   As just mentioned, it is in the range between turning over and arriving 
   at whoop timbre—for open vowels, approximately the pitch range of the 
   treble clef—that strategies involving a more prominent second formant 
   are employed by many professional singers, especially non-treble singers. 
   !is is most likely due to the large intervallic distance between the /f_irst 
   and second source harmonics, as compared to the relatively smaller 
   distances between higher harmonics and their “trailing” harmonics. For 
   example, when the third harmonic surpasses the /f_irst resonance, the 
   second harmonic is less than a perfect /f_i/f_th behind and already su#ciently 
   within the bandwidth of the /f_irst resonance to be ampli/f_ied by it. However, 
   when the second harmonic crosses through the /f_irst resonance, the /f_irst 
   harmonic is almost an octave below. !e resultant loss of power can be 
   countered if a higher harmonic is within the bandwidth of the second 
   resonance. As Donald Miller has pointed out, for front vowels which have 
   a high second formant frequency center, this is likely to be the fourth 
   PRAA.indb   54PRAA.indb   54 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
   Harmonic:Resonance Interactions 55
   or higher harmonic. With the back vowels, which have a low second 
   formant, this is likely to be the third or even the second harmonic. !is 
   seems to be a feature of many premier tenors, generating an especially 
   ringing top range and of treble belters to extend belt into a bright belt 
   mix. Others maintain a stronger singer’s formant cluster to achieve ring. 
   Similar strategies in treble voice singers can be appropriate for the lower-
   middle voice, but for the upper middle voice can cause an overly brilliant 
   sound and a reluctance to relinquish the second resonance dominance 
   in order to establish the /f_irst resonance: /f_irst harmonic dominance of full 
   whoop timbre. Whoop timbre is essential to opulent operatic head voice 
   for pitches nearing the top of the treble clef and above. In any case, in order 
   to have the fullness and depth necessary for classical timbral balance, once 
   the second harmonic has surpassed the /f_irst resonance, the /f_irst harmonic 
   (1fo) will need to be resonated to some su#cient extent.
   First Resonance Tracking of 
   the First Harmonic ( fR1:1fo )
   Normally a classical genre singer would not want to sing a pitch higher 
   than the tuning of the /f_irst resonance, since the tone would lack depth 
   and fullness. !erefore, when a pitch is sung above the normal location 
   of a vowel’s /f_irst formant, the vowel would be actively modi/f_ied to raise 
   the /f_irst resonance and its formant in tandem with the rising pitch in 
   order to maintain full whoop timbre. !ere are two ways to raise the /f_irst 
   formant: tube shortening and vowel opening. Vowel opening is the better 
   option for classical timbre. Opening a vowel involves increasing its overall 
   shape divergence, while trying to preserve something of its characteristic 
   shape relative to other vowels. For example, if it is a tongue-fronted 
   vowel (tongue dorsum humped near the front teeth rather than &at), 
   tongue height and dorsum-palatal-narrowing will be preserved, even if 
   somewhat less frontally, as the vowel and mouth are opened and the head 
   posture slightly elevated. !e higher the pitch being sung, the subtler any 
   vowel distinctions become, but some thought/intention of the intended 
   vowel can assist intelligibility remarkably high in the singing range. If it 
   is a lip-rounded vowel within speaking range, some hint of lip rounding 
   PRAA.indb   55PRAA.indb   55 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  56
   remains above speaking range as the mouth is increasingly opened—as 
   compared to a non-lip rounded vowel—until quite high. Tube shortening 
   raises all formants and therefore changes timbral Fach perception. It 
   should generally be avoided in classical genre except for the extreme high 
   range of non-treble voices (ca. B5 and above). By that pitch, the ability 
   to raise the /f_irst formant by vowel opening alone has been exhausted, so 
   that elevating the larynx is the only remaining means of raising F1. At 
   that point, laryngeal elevation does not necessarily compromise vocal 
   freedom, since tuning the /f_irst resonance to the /f_irst harmonic provides 
   bene/f_icial interactive feedback to the vibrator, reducing the amount of 
   glottal resistance necessary. Besides, the vocal Fach of singers attempting 
   such stratospheric frequencies is rarely in question. Such vowel opening 
   for whoop timbre tuning is used by non-treble singers only for the vowels 
   whose /f_irst formants lie within their singing range, such as [i], [u], and 
   [y]. As mentioned above, trained non-treble voice singers tend to avoid 
   whoop timbre altogether by keeping the /f_irst formant above the pitch they 
   are singing through vowel opening, unless they are seeking a pure head 
   tone or falsetto timbre for occasional special eﬀect. But untrained or less 
   skillful ones o/f_ten neglect to open su#ciently when singing above the /f_irst 
   formant locations (ca. D4 to G4) of their close vowels ([i u y]) resulting in 
   timbral thinness and more eﬀortful phonation.
   Untrained non-treble singers also o/f_ten raise the larynx on close 
   vowels such as [i] and [u] even a/f_ter those vowels have closed, that is, 
   within the octave below the vowel’s /f_irst formant. In this case they are 
   attempting to stay in a more robust laryngeal adjustment without 
   opening the articulation of the vowel when singing pitches that approach 
   its /f_irst resonance. Maintaining a low larynx and close pronunciation in 
   that situation would normally result in the timbre becoming increasingly 
   “heady. ” Raising the larynx to avoid that whoop timbre causes a shallower 
   timbre and pressed or constricted phonation. Opening the vowel shape 
   within close timbre by opening the mouth while maintaining a relaxed 
   and settled larynx and a high dorsum-palatal narrowing between the 
   back upper molars will facilitate robust timbre and postpone or avoid 
   whoop timbre. Classical treble voices (countertenors, mezzos, and 
   sopranos alike) all will need to use the same resonance tuning strategies 
   (see Chapter 7).

   Treble Voice 
   Resonance Strategies
   Treble voices (altos, mezzos, countertenors, sopranos), whose ranges lie 
   primarily in the treble clef, face diﬀerent laryngeal registration issues 
   and resonance:harmonic circumstances than non-treble voices. Where 
   lower voices stay primarily to exclusively in vibrational mechanism one, 
   with its thicker vocal fold shape and larger vertical phase diﬀerence, 
   treble voices in Western classical singing transition from mechanism 
   one, involving TA muscle oscillation, into vibrational mechanism two, 
   with its thin, stretched vocal fold shape, increasingly involving only vocal 
   ligament vibration, in their lower-middle range. A third vibrational 
   mechanism—whistle register, with stiﬀ vocal folds that typically do 
   not fully close, eventually involving epithelial vibration above E6 (Titze 
   2025)—is used for extremely high pitches, above ca. C6. Secondly, since 
   treble ranges are about an octave higher than non-treble ranges, treble 
   voices have half as many harmonics within keyboard range available 
   for resonation. (!is can be dramatically demonstrated on the Madde 
   synthesizer: see Madde Exploration 2, p. 17.) For this reason, tuning the 
   /f_irst resonance to available harmonics is much more crucial for treble 
   singers; the higher the pitch, the more relevant the principle. !irdly, with 
   vocal tracts that are on average 20 percent shorter than male sex vocal 
   tracts, most female sex singers have fewer formants within keyboard 
   range—o/f_ten only three—from which to form their primary timbre. 
   !at nonetheless still covers the necessary bases of vowel formants 
   (resonances one and two) and a possible singer’s formant (resonance 
   three). Once a singer is above ca. C5, the singer’s formant is of decreasing 
   importance to overall resonance and projection, since the fundamental 
   PRAA.indb   59PRAA.indb   59 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  60
   Treble Voice Resonance Strategies
   frequency becomes the dominant harmonic and is high enough in 
   frequency to generate the necessary loudness level. Finally, since /f_irst 
   formants lie in contact with the treble clef, treble voices face the need 
   to resort to the /f_irst resonance tracking of the fundamental (by vowel 
   opening as described above) relatively earlier in their range than non-
   treble voices, from ca. G4-G5. In other words, as soon as a singer reaches 
   the pitch of the /f_irst resonance of a vowel, they will need to gradually 
   open that vowel for higher pitches. 1 Furthermore, some classical treble 
   singers will even close vowels somewhat, lowering the /f_irst resonance in 
   order to track the fundamental for a headier timbre lower in their range. 
   Western classical treble singing is therefore done in whoop mode a high 
   percentage of the time. !is active vowel modi/f_ication will necessarily 
   reduce the diﬀerences between vowels as they all approach the formant 
   tuning of an [ ɑ] above the treble clef, medializing and to some extent 
   neutralizing vowel timbre. Nonetheless, with su#cient textual and 
   consonantal context, plus a continued clear conception of the intended 
   vowel, some vowel diﬀerentiation and perceived intelligibility are 
   possible surprisingly high in the range.
   Middle Voice Challenge of Open 
   Vowels
   Treble voice singers generally prefer close vowels, especially close front 
   vowels, like [i] and [e], in the lower-middle voice. !is is due to the fact 
   that the /f_irst formants of those vowels lie low enough in the treble clef to 
   eﬀectively resonate the fundamental frequency of pitches in that range. 
   !e /f_irst formant of [ɑ], on the other hand, is at the top of the treble clef

   used to strengthen this part of the range:
   z strengthening vocal fold closure through exercising mechanism

   Upper Middle Voice
   When better function is achieved in the upper middle voice, sopranos 
   o/f_ten report a taller, slenderer acoustic sensation of the tone, whose “dome” 
   is “behind the eyes, ” rather than the wider, shorter, lateral, “mouthy” 
   sensation of a “spread” tone. Sensation can be paradoxical. A semi-fronted, 
   elevated tongue dorsum, moderately close, convergent vowel posture, 
   amused aﬀect, and so/f_t palate that feels li/f_ted rather toward its front near 
   the hard palate, as if there is a vertical square mid-mouth between the back 
   molars, all achieve a su#ciently open throat, even though one has little 
   awareness of the throat with this set of actions. Kinesthesia for throat space 
   is notoriously false. (See Open !roat and Noiseless Inhalation, p. 99.)  
   !ese strategies appear to help establish the whoop timbre necessary for 
   the upper voice as the treble voices sing through the upper half of the 
   treble clef into their top register. Some teachers recommend assisting this 
   narrowing sensation with actual lip rounding on non-lip rounded vowels, 
   though if the internal strategies listed above are done su#ciently, the 
   PRAA.indb   61PRAA.indb   61 7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM7/10/2025   4:38:51 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  62
   external appearance of the vowel needn’t be falsi/f_ied. As the sung pitch 
   rises into the /f_irst resonance of the vowel being sung, the vowel color 
   will have maximally migrated toward its warmer, rounder /f_irst formant 
   spectral tone color. Treble voice singers o/f_ten have to be encouraged to 
   allow this timbral migration. It is a rather parallel percept to the vowel 
   migration of non-treble voices an octave lower entering the turn (cover) 
   of their vowels and even of treble voices at those pitches in what is their 
   lower passaggio.
   Whoop Timbre and Beyond
   Once a treble voice has tracked 1 fo with the /f_irst resonance as high as 
   possible through vowel opening (to ca. Bb5), they will need to raise their 
   larynx to some degree for higher pitches to continue tracking the sung 
   pitch. When the /f_irst resonance can be raised no further, the acoustic 
   register makes a transition into whistle register ( mechanism three ) in 
   which the /f_irst and second resonances might possibly be brought close 
   enough together so that the fundamental frequency (sung pitch) can be 
   resonated between them by their clustered bandwidths, or in which the 
   second resonance takes over. Ian Howell’s research on spectral tone color 
   provides the most apt explanation to date of the distinct vowel qualities 
   that can be heard pitch by pitch in these stratospheric resonance tunings. 
   !ey are typically perceived as bright variants of mid to open vowels [ ɔ 
   ɑ a æ], whose spectral tone colors reside in the frequency range of G5 to 
   G6. In some cases, an extremely high pitch may be being resonated by the 
   second resonance.

   Lower Passaggio Training
   !e pitches between the clefs, ca. Bb3 to F4, span an area in which both 
   signi/f_icant laryngeal and acoustic register transitions occur in all voice 
   types: laryngeal registration either migrates from mechanism one to 
   mechanism two or at least to a lighter version of mechanism one; vowels 
   have multiple acoustic register transitions there, including most transitions 
   from open timbre ( voce aperta) to close timbre ( voce chiusa). !is range 
   area constitutes the upper passaggio1 of non-treble voices and the lower 
   passaggio of treble voices. !e acoustic strategies suggested here—timbral 
   and somatosensory migrations and register transitions—therefore are 
   parallel and adaptable for both voice types.
   To review brie&y, a relatively stable tube (vocal tract) length is necessary 
   for consistent depth and balance of timbre in Western classical singing. 
   Both tube length and cross-sectional area are responsible for resonance 
   locations. Eﬀective tube length determines the general location of the 
   entire formant set of a given voice, including the Fach speci/f_ic singer’s 
   formant cluster. However, untrained singers instinctively shorten the vocal 
   tract upon ascending, raising the /f_irst formant in order to preserve the 
   open-timbred sound and somatosense of speech or, upon ascending into 
   the treble clef, the strong fR1:2fo acoustic coupling of the yell, a behavior that 
   appears to be a universal, hardwired survival trait. Y elling results in an 
   increasingly pressed mode of phonation, a heavier (thyroarytenoid [TA] 
   dominant) laryngeal registration adjustment, and inconsistency of timbre 
   and perceived Fach. If vocal tract length and shape are kept stable, /f_irst 
   formant locations will remain stable and the yell will be avoided by allowing 
   2fo to pass through and above fR1. !is transition results in the timbral shi/f_t  
   variously referred to as “closing, ” “covering, ” or “turning over, ” and facilitates 
   an easier laryngeal registration adjustment—either to a lighter “mix” form 
   PRAA.indb   65PRAA.indb   65 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  66
   Figure 8.1 The thyrohyoid space.
   Lower Passaggio Training
   of mechanism one or a complete transition into mechanism two—and a 
   less pressed mode of phonation. Knowledge of the variety of F1 locations 
   by vowel and their predictable fR1:2fo crossings when singing an octave 
   lower, as well as the passive vowel migrations that accompany those 
   crossings, becomes both an accurate means of assessing tube length 
   stability and a reliable basis for developing eﬀective strategies for training 
   tube stability for successful passaggio negotiation.
   Lower Passaggio Objectives
   !e main technical objectives in training this voice transition into the 
   treble middle voice or non-treble upper voice are as follows.
   A Stable Laryngeal Position and Tube 
   Length and a Convergent Resonator
   A relatively stable laryngeal 
   position, tube length, and 
   convergent resonator shape 
   ensures depth and consistency 
   of timbre. A stable tube 
   length is accomplished by 
   avoiding activation of the 
   swallowing muscles that raise 
   the larynx and constrict the 
   laryngopharynx. !is can be 
   monitored in some bodies by 
   palpating the thyrohyoid space 
   toward the back of the larynx 
   on either side. (See Figure 8.1  
   and Monitoring Tube Length 
   Stability, p. 101.) Although some 
   speci/f_ic narrowings within the 
   larynx facilitate e#ciency, the thyrohyoid space can remain relatively 
   uncompressed and open. Muscularly /f_ixing the larynx in a low position 
   PRAA.indb   66PRAA.indb   66 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Lower Passaggio Training 67
   will interfere with vocal freedom. Maintaining timbral depth and 
   degree of vowel closeness until a/f_ter the vowel has turned over assists 
   in discouraging laryngeal elevation upon pitch ascent. A convergent 
   resonator shape—more open near the larynx and closer, either at the 
   dorsum:palatal narrowing or near the lips—facilitates the development 
   of the singer’s formant cluster and chiaroscuro, balanced resonance. It 
   requires some degree of tongue dorsum height, elevation of the so/f_t  
   palate, a shortened front-to-back distance percept, and the least degree 
   of jaw drop necessary for the vowel/pitch combination being sung. For 
   relatively open vowels it should seem to the singer that inner height 
   “leads” and jaw opening follows, rather than vice versa.
   Dynamic Laryngeal Registration
   A dynamic laryngeal registration  with smooth, gradual changes ensures 
   ease and dynamic &exibility. While using appropriate resonance strategies 
   usually improves laryngeal registration, it does not guarantee eﬀective 
   muscular adjustments. Dynamic (ever-changing) laryngeal registration 
   appropriate to pitch requires some separate attention. Expression of 
   feeling aﬀords the most immediate, nuanced access to and control of 
   laryngeal registration. However, knowledge of the eﬀects of vowel on 
   resonance and registration can inform creative exercise strategies and 
   therapies. For example, smoothly gliding from a chesty open vowel 
   to a heady close vowel an octave higher can give laryngeal muscles an 
   opportunity to discover dynamic adjustments. Singing scalar passages in 
   which deliberate, successively smoother timbre and closer vowel choices 
   coincide with ascending pitch can also coax laryngeal registration toward 
   a lighter adjustment, and vice versa (buzzier and more open-timbred) for 
   descent. While changes in the percentages of the TA and CT involvement 
   may be trained to be fairly gradual, acoustic feedback factors will likely 
   precipitate a binary shi/f_t from vibrational mechanism one (short, thick 
   folds, loose cover, vocalis muscle oscillation) to mechanism two (long, 
   taut, thin folds, taut cover, vocal ligament oscillation) at some point in the 
   scale, though skillful singers are able to minimize the timbral eﬀect of this 
   shi/f_t. Non-treble voices resort to laryngeal mechanism two infrequently, 
   but do need lighter adjustments of mechanism one for the upper range.
   PRAA.indb   67PRAA.indb   67 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  68
   Familiarity with Vowel Turning
   A familiarity with the locations, sounds, and sensations of vowel turning 
   is necessary. Since letting the voice turn over—rather than yelling—is 
   not instinctive, knowing when and where to expect vowels to turn over 
   can be very helpful to the young singer. If vowels do not turn over where 
   expected, one of two things has happened to keep the /f_irst formant 
   above the second harmonic: the vowel has been opened (a sometimes 
   reasonable but pitch- and diction-limited strategy) or the larynx has been 
   raised (rarely a good strategy in classical genre), or most likely, both. 
   Experiencing vowel turning on the close vowels [i] and [u], which close 
   well below the traditional lower passaggio location, can be instructive. 
   Close [o] and [e] turn at about the traditionally understood location of the 
   primo passaggio, at the low end of the zona di passaggio. !eir depth and 
   closeness are relatively easy to monitor and coach. An untrained singer 
   will tend to open [o] toward [ ʌ] and [e] toward [ ɛ] rather than allowing 
   the turn. Resonant, in&ective speech sounds that loop above, gliding up 
   and over the predicted pitch of turning and ease back down through it are 
   easy initial ways to explore vowel turning. If the yell instinct is stubborn, 
   suggesting depth, thinking a slightly closer vowel and an internalizing 
   aﬀect or direction (sobby; in, down and out) while ascending, are o/f_ten 
   fruitful strategies. Avoiding chin tuck, and instead, allowing the head to 
   tip slightly back and up as the warmer, darker “ under-vowel” tone color 
   tips up and forward above the palate and the tongue dorsum rises toward 
   the TMJ facilitates the turn as well. !e eventual actual goal is to maintain 
   rather than change tube shape while looping up, allowing the acoustic, 
   smooth yet almost binary somatosensory “&ip” of passive migration that 
   occurs with turning.
   The Ability to Turn Over
   Once a student is familiar with the sounds and sensations of turning, 
   and knows when and where vowels should turn over, the ability to allow 
   vowels to turn over at appropriate pitches should be rehearsed until 
   routinely automated and non-manipulative. If the best resonance tuning 
   is being maintained, an expressively motivated in&ective loop up and 
   down on any vowel will turn over and back with consistent chiaroscuro 
   PRAA.indb   68PRAA.indb   68 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Lower Passaggio Training 69
   timbre, ease, and without a signi/f_icant, inevitable crescendo toward the 
   crest of the loop. !ere may also be a sense of the tone settling in an inner 
   direction both above the palate and below the larynx with the upward 
   pitch in&ection and turn, and back toward the mid-mouth and the hard 
   palate when reopening upon descent.
   Vowel Integrity with Appropriate Vowel 
   Migration
   !e passive vowel migration that accompanies vowel turning should not 
   distort vowels, rather should be in the same natural vowel family, on the 
   same side of the “front-back” street, and not interfere with intelligibility. 
   Speci/f_ically, modi/f_ications on the “back vowel” side of the street—[ɑ ɔ ʊ 
   o]—will move in the direction of a closer neighbor on the same side of 
   the street. Similarly vowels on the “front” side of the street—[ ɛ e ɪ]—will 
   close somewhat and slightly neutralize. Since they will already have closed 
   much lower, the close vowels [i] and [u] will need mouth and jaw opening 
   while maintaining tongue dorsum height. !e [i] can retain identity 
   rather high, since F2 tuning can be maintained via dorsum height even 
   with mouth opening. !e [u] will necessarily open toward an [ ʊ], as it 
   does in celebratory whooping. Avoiding whoop timbre by extending close 
   timbre range via vowel opening through the zona di passaggio will retain 
   the buzzier “virility” of non-treble voice resonance. However, the mixing 
   of back vowels such as [ɑ] or [ɔ] “across the street” toward [ɛ] or toward a 
   mixed vowel such as [œ] by fronting the tongue dorsum will be perceived 
   as vowel distortion (see Vowel Modi/f_ication Revisited below).
   Maintenance of Chiaroscuro Timbre
   While the upper voice of professional non-treble classical singers should 
   have an exciting, ringing quality, there should also be timbral fullness and 
   depth. !is is accomplished by maintaining tube length, an unconstricted 
   throat, and the degree of resonator convergence necessary for the depth-
   dependent singer’s formant cluster, even while the tube shape is being 
   otherwise modi/f_ied (i.e., when resonances one and two are involved in 
   some range speci/f_ic formant tuning). Treble voices transitioning into 
   PRAA.indb   69PRAA.indb   69 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  70
   their middle range tend to migrate into laryngeal mechanism two by, 
   or early in the treble clef, causing the need to maintain su#cient glottal 
   closure competence for a robust sound. Helpful strategies include: 
   maintaining shape convergence, allowing timbral migrations with 
   su#cient complementary tone color and auditory roughness (migration 
   of buzziness), and careful, expressively motivated coup de glotte onsets.
   Vowel Modiﬁcation Revisited
   !e question of vowel modi/f_ication vs. vowel integrity  or “purity” has 
   been much debated across the history of vocal pedagogy. Both concepts 
   require yet elude clear de/f_inition, since human perception is involved. 
   Given our new understanding that, to human perception, sound is a 
   blend of inherent, frequency-dependent spectral tone colors, which 
   therefore necessarily change with every pitch change, and the fact that the 
   tone color contributions of individual frequencies increase and decrease 
   as harmonics move into and through vocal tract resonance peaks, it 
   becomes clear that vowel migration is inevitable. !e concept of vowel 
   modi/f_ication has historically ranged from conscious vocal tract shape 
   changes with the intent of producing a modi/f_ied vowel timbre to outright 
   vowel substitution. !e concept of passive vowel migration introduced 
   here points out that some degree of vowel shi/f_t will dependably, inevitably 
   occur when the vowel shaping of the vocal tract tube is kept stable while 
   the pitch (with its set of potential harmonics) is raised, as harmonics 
   change in tone color and intensity as they move through resonances. !is 
   kind of vowel change will feel to the singer like they are maintaining the 
   vowel—at least in shape—while allowing a subtle migration within the 
   same family, or on the same “side of the street” in terms of front-back 
   designation. What all teachers presumably want to avoid is the perception 
   of a vowel distortion. !is occurs when, for example, an open back vowel, 
   such as [ɔ] takes on some of the quality of a mixed vowel such as [œ]. !is 
   is more indicative of mistaken tongue shape (moving the dorsum-palatal-
   narrowing too far forward) or excess tongue tension than of the acoustics 
   of turning over, and does not result from maintaining the normal speech 
   shape of [ɔ] over the fR1:2fo crossing, which should migrate instead in the 
   direction of [ʊ].
   PRAA.indb   70PRAA.indb   70 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Lower Passaggio Training 71
   !e phenomenon of passive vowel migration invites the question of 
   what vowel modi/f_ication is or should be altogether. Is it a deliberate 
   reshaping of the resonator to that of a diﬀerent speech vowel posture in 
   the attempt to /f_ind a more favorable resonance:harmonic coupling? Or is 
   it rather a perceptual change that occurs as a result of maintaining vowel 
   shape while changing pitch, and the resultant, inherently changing 
   spectral tone colors and resonance:harmonic relationships? Or is it 
   perhaps both, depending upon the circumstance? !e answers to these 
   questions have been signi/f_icantly informed by Howell’s work on spectral 
   tone color, but could bene/f_it from further research. In terms of practice in 
   this author’s opinion, below the turn to just above it, maintaining shape 
   should prevail. Between the turn and whoop timbre, in close timbre, 
   either can be used. At fR1:1fo whoop coupling and higher, some form of 
   active shape change (vowel opening) must be used to track whoop timbre 
   and thus maintain timbral fullness, regardless of how the singer motivates 
   that shape change. Ideally a singer should be able to continue to think the 
   vowel of the word being sung, but with the addition of greater opening of 
   the mouth and mandible while allowing an increasing percentage of the 
   complementary vowel tone color. Some singers get a better result by 
   actually thinking a more open neighbor vowel (vowel substitution). 
   Subtleties of shaping will enable one to preserve the intended vowel 
   identity more or less well relative to other vowels at the same pitch. !at 
   said, if the su#ciently modi/f_ied vocal tract shape necessary for a resonant 
   but convincingly understandable [i] vowel on a tenor or treble voice C5, is 
   used on C4 an octave lower, it will not sound like a speech level [i], rather 
   it will contain excess under-vowel [ ʏ] tone color. Conversely, if a tenor 
   attempts a normal speech range [i] shape on C5, the timbre—if phonation 
   is even possible—will sound thin, strained, or strangled. Changing the 
   shape of the vocal tract in such situations (when singing above the normal 
   F1 frequency) is essential, both for balanced sound and for functional ease.
   One principle at play in all modi/f_ication has to do with the number of 
   possible harmonics that lie below the /f_irst resonance. !e lower the pitch 
   being sung, the more potential harmonics there will be below the /f_irst 
   resonance. Furthermore, when singing lower pitches, usually a higher 
   number harmonic (3 fo or higher) will be rising through the /f_irst 
   resonance, and the harmonic below it will be trailing more closely, o/f_ten 
   already well within the bandwidth (climbing the skirt) of fR1. !e trailing 
   harmonic is therefore positioned to be resonated by fR1 and thus able to 
   PRAA.indb   71PRAA.indb   71 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  72
   assume the prominence just relinquished by its higher neighbor. With 
   multiple harmonics below the /f_irst resonance, there are also more closely 
   spaced harmonics available above the /f_irst resonance to be resonated by 
   resonances two and higher, hence little need to modify the shape for 
   better resonance:harmonic matches. !erefore, active vowel 
   modi/f_ication—beyond a generally good, convergent acoustic poise of 
   the vocal tract—should not be needed in the range where two or more 
   harmonics lie below fR1.
   Higher harmonics (with closer trailing harmonics) rising through the 
   /f_irst resonance at lower sung pitches generate audible, but subtler timbral 
   closings or turnings. However, when the second harmonic rises through 
   the /f_irst resonance, the trailing /f_irst harmonic is almost an octave below, 
   and the resultant timbral shi/f_t (not to mention the interactive feedback 
   eﬀect on the vibrator) is more signi/f_icant. Once whoop timbre is achieved, 
   that is, once 1 fo has arrived at the frequency peak of fR1, no trailing 
   harmonics remain (there are no harmonics below 1 fo—it is by de/f_inition 
   the lowest harmonic), and there are many fewer higher harmonics within 
   primary vowel tone color range (keyboard range) available for resonation. 
   !erefore, with higher pitch, formant tuning becomes increasingly 
   necessary, the higher the fundamental, the more essential and greater the 
   necessary modi/f_ication. Should the /f_irst harmonic rise above the /f_irst 
   resonance enough to fall out of its bandwidth, since there are no rising 
   harmonics below the /f_irst resonance for it to feature, the overall timbre 
   will quickly thin, and formant retuning (raising fR1 in tandem with the 
   pitch being sung) becomes essential.
   Non-Linear Source-Filter Theory 
   Revisited
   !e purpose of active vowel modi/f_ication just discussed is to adjust 
   resonances in order to /f_ind a better match with available source harmonics. 
   As mentioned rather earlier, in certain circumstances acoustic energy 
   reverberating within the vocal tract can be productively re&ected back 
   toward the vocal folds, assisting the e#ciency and power of the glottal 
   oscillation. Such interactivity can eﬀectively increase the closed quotient 
   PRAA.indb   72PRAA.indb   72 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Lower Passaggio Training 73
   (percentage of closure time in each vibrational cycle) of the vibrator. !is 
   makes the resonator more e#cient for the upper end of the spectrum: 
   the longer the closed phase , the stronger the reinforcement of higher 
   harmonics. It also reduces air&ow without the need for greater muscular 
   glottal closure force (glottal squeeze). !e circumstances in which 
   interactivity is enhanced are: some resonator convergence, a settled larynx, 
   an unconstricted throat, narrowed exit of the epilaryngeal tube, and other 
   micro-adjustments along the vocal tract. All of these conditions increase 
   acoustic inertive reactance, a factor that can improve glottal e#ciency. 
   !ese characteristics (unconstricted throat, resonator convergence) are 
   especially typical of unampli/f_ied Western classical singing in the creation 
   of its chiaroscuro (bright/dark) balanced resonance and singer’s formant 
   cluster. (Sundberg 1974; Titze and Story 1997; Titze and Verdolini 2012).
   !e in&uence of the resonator upon the vibrator had been anticipated 
   by earlier voice teacher/researchers such as Berton Co#n, Ralph 
   Appleman, and John Large, and intuited by voice teachers for centuries, 
   but the physics involved has only more recently been explained by voice 
   science researchers such as Johan Sundberg, Ingo Titze, and Donald 
   Miller. At present, non-linear interactivity as described above seems to 
   apply to vocal tract strategies (a convergent resonator) especially present 
   in unampli/f_ied Western classical singing. Other singing styles that employ 
   a more divergent oral shape access bene/f_icial interactivity via alternative 
   pharyngeal narrowing strategies that prioritize greater high-frequency 
   spectral content.
   Resonator Convergence
   Voice scientist Ingo Titze has observed that Western classical singers use a 
   vocal tract shape that is relatively convergent, as in an inverted megaphone, 
   presumably in order to maximize inertive reactance for classical resonance 
   e#ciency. As explained above, the creation of the singer’s formant cluster 
   and chiaroscuro timbre requires a relatively convergent resonator shape. 
   Close front vowels, given their tongue contact with the upper molars, 
   are inherently convergent, and also create greater pharyngeal space and 
   verticality behind the tongue’s dorsum-palatal-narrowing. Open vowels 
   PRAA.indb   73PRAA.indb   73 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  74
   will need some adjustment to achieve maximal convergence, within the 
   limits of acceptable vowel intelligibility. For example, pronouncing open 
   vowels within speech range with the least amount of jaw drop and tongue 
   &attening—retaining as high a tongue dorsum as can be managed with good 
   diction—will increase their convergence. Convergence is also dependent 
   upon a relatively settled, neutral throat, in which the thyrohyoid space has 
   not been compressed (Figure 8.1, p. 66), and appropriate, fronted height 
   of the so/f_t palate. A convergent vocal tract poise can be more naturally 
   achieved through the use of motivational aﬀects, such as inner pleasure, 
   suppressed laugh, mischief, empathy, sobbiness, and so on, aﬀects that 
   stimulate a settled larynx, unconstricted throat, li/f_ted palate, and closer 
   vowel postures. All such adjustments should result in better chiaroscuro 
   resonance, comfortably and freely achieved.
   Vocal Cover/Turning Over
   If vowels turn over at their predicted locations—slightly less than an 
   octave below the resonant speech locations of their /f_irst formants—that 
   is evidence of tube length stability, a desirable characteristic in Western 
   classical singing. !erefore, observing the location of vowel turnings is 
   useful for assessing and training tube length stability. Raising the larynx 
   with pitch ascent—due to the yell instinct—is a remarkably strong and 
   universal response. Countering this instinct by training the larynx to &oat 
   low even when ascending, constitutes a signi/f_icant part of training singers, 
   especially non-treble singers. It is important for the teacher and singer 
   to understand, respect, and allow the necessary harmonic:resonance 
   crossings that result from a stable tube length during pitch ascent, and 
   the subtle passive vowel migrations that accompany each successive 
   timbral closing. Furthermore, understanding the relationship between 
   vowel closeness and /f_irst formant locations provides an eﬀective tool for 
   encouraging vowel turning when habit and instinct undermine tube length 
   stability and resist vowel turning. If the tube shortening/vowel opening 
   “yell” response is stubbornly persistent, deepening the timbre along with 
   some degree of vowel closing can be temporarily used to precipitate 
   turning over until the habitual response is retrained. !e degree of closure 
   PRAA.indb   74PRAA.indb   74 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Lower Passaggio Training 75
   can be accomplished by vowel choice or closeness of vowel pronunciation, 
   such as insisting on a “Minnesotan” (or German closed) [o]; moving to 
   a closer neighbor vowel: [o] to [u]; [e] to [ ɪ]; or by physical directions 
   that accomplish su#cient closeness, such as maintaining contact between 
   the tongue dorsum and molars, making sure the so/f_t palate is li/f_ted, or 
   maintaining lip or tongue shape.
   Secondary Acoustic Registration 
   Events
   As mentioned above, there are audible eﬀects whenever any harmonic 
   crosses the /f_irst resonance—closings when a harmonic rises through 
   the /f_irst resonance, and openings when a harmonic drops below the 
   /f_irst resonance. Like the primary turning of the voice when 2fo rises 
   through fR1, the locations of all other harmonic:resonance crossings are 
   predictable, and therefore pedagogically useful. !e third harmonic 
   will cross a /f_irst resonance when singing an octave and a /f_i/f_th below the 
   resonance; the fourth harmonic two octaves below, and so forth. Given 
   these larger intervallic distances below /f_irst resonance locations, the 
   resultant intersections necessarily happen more o/f_ten on low pitches and 
   on open vowels, hence more o/f_ten within the bass clef, or in non-treble 
   voices.
   Handling the Lower Zona di 
   Passaggio
   All of these crossings provide opportunities for inappropriate adjustments 
   of tube length or shape (shortening and opening). !is is more o/f_ten an 
   issue when ascending, since the /f_irst resonance tends to lock on and track 
   rising harmonics, making timbral closing less instinctive than timbral 
   opening. For example, the third harmonic crosses the /f_irst resonance of 
   the open vowels [ɛ], [ɔ], and [ɑ] just below the traditional location of the 
   PRAA.indb   75PRAA.indb   75 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  76
   primo passaggio, generating an audible “mini-turning” or “mini-closing” 
   that is o/f_ten described as seeming acoustically “narrower, ” “taller, ” with 
   a slight increase in the vowel’s complementary tone color, and more 
   internal to the singer. If this crossing does not happen, the larynx has 
   been raised and the vowel opened, causing the zona di passaggio  to 
   sound too blatant or wide open, and setting the singer up for a more 
   di#cult transition through the secondo passaggio. !is may explain the 
   frequently encountered “hourglass” perception or description of the zona 
   di passaggio and directives to “narrow, ” “gather, ” or “collect” the voice 
   through the passaggio.
   Furthermore, the /f_irst resonances of the close vowels [i] and [u] in 
   their normal speech shapes lie in the vicinity of the secondo passaggio,  
   and pitches on those vowels would have closed acoustically an octave 
   lower, well below even the primo passaggio. Unless wanting to move into 
   whoop coupling (fR1:1fo) at the secondo passaggio, the singer will need to 
   open [i] and [u] through the zona di passaggio in order to avoid or delay 
   whoop timbre and stay in a more virile or belt-like mixed timbre. In any 
   case singers will certainly need to open [i] and [u] at the secondo 
   passaggio and above to avoid thinness of timbre and tightness of 
   phonation, since in their normal speech shapes, whoop coupling ( fR1:1fo) 
   would have been reached by then, and—if no adjustment is made—
   exceeded above it. !ough it is counterintuitive, due to their relative F1 
   locations, an [i] in the upper voice will need to be more orally open in 
   shape than an [ ɑ] at the same pitch in order to match in registrational 
   timbre.
   !ese secondary acoustic registration events create opportunities not 
   only for inappropriate adjustments, but also can be fruitfully exploited for 
   training purposes. !e primary acoustic transition of the fR1:2fo crossing of 
   the open vowels near the secondo passaggio can be reliably modeled an 
   octave lower, where 4 fo crosses f R1, or a /f_i/f_th lower where 3fo crosses fR1. 
   !ough the passive vowel migrations are subtler at these interactions, they 
   oﬀer the advantage of rehearsing acoustic transitions that parallel the 
   fR1:2fo intersection without the challenges of laryngeal registration faced at 
   that higher transition. Learning how to maintain tube shape and to allow 
   the resultant vowel migration (passive modi/f_ication) is crucial to the 
   training of Western classical singing.
   PRAA.indb   76PRAA.indb   76 7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM7/10/2025   4:38:52 PM
