   Registration and Expression/
   Artistry
   Along with the primary turn, these secondary timbral events are 
   not only important for elegant range negotiation, but can also be 
   exploited for artistic purposes, since their passive timbral migrations 
   are often associated with expression. A non-treble voice that “opens” 
   further upon descending through a harmonic/formant crossing can 
   sound richer and emotionally fuller. Conversely, the deepening and 
   rounding of timbre that accompanies ascending timbral closures 
   (cover) is colorful and emotionally warming and/or exciting. One 
   can actively precipitate these effects safely—when sufficiently near 
   their predictable locations—by subtle degrees of vowel opening or 
   closing. The thrill of a high note can be increased by accessing the 
   resonance boost of a second resonance:higher harmonic coupling 
   upon turning over. If a soft, sweet yet full head tone effect is sought 
   on an [i] or an [u] near the secondo passaggio , the singer can stay in 
   close pronunciation to access full “whoop” timbre, and open gradually 
   above that to continue to track 1 fo wi th fR1. These phenomena have 
   parallels in the lower passaggio  of treble voices at approximately the 
   same pitches and benefit from the same strategies. In treble voices, 
   there is a greater tendency to transition into mechanism two earlier, 
   especially in classical genres, but this can be delayed as in non-
   treble upper voices if appropriate to the genre aesthetic. See Effect of 
   Approach by Leap, p. 85, and Timbral Openings and Closings Other 
   than fR1:2fo, p. 89.

   Practical Vocal Acoustics
   9
   Perceptions of Turning Over
   !e reader may wonder, “When a voice turns over—when 2fo rises through 
   fR1—what does it sound like to the audience? And what does it sound and 
   feel like to the singer?”
   First it should be noted that this question is especially signi/f_icant for 
   non-treble voices to access their upper range, since all vowels will have 
   turned over in the octave below G4, and for deep voices as early as by D4. 
   And yet there are parallel percepts for the lower passaggio of treble voices 
   in the same range where transitioning from laryngeal mechanism one to 
   mechanism two commences—the range ca. between the bass and treble 
   clefs. Furthermore, the answer is complicated by the fact that it will vary 
   somewhat from vowel to vowel, from voice type to voice type, and from 
   whether the cause is active or passive vowel modi/f_ication. Finally, since 
   subjective, sensory perceptions are individual, predictive descriptions of 
   perception are inherently variable and therefore cannot be in&exibly 
   described. All that said, the following descriptions and pedagogic 
   opinions—based on historic pedagogic terminology descriptive of 
   procedural knowledge, personal experience, anecdote, impression, and 
   student response, though not subjected to the rigorous testing of scienti/f_ic 
   methodology—are typical and, in the author’s experience, reliably useful.
   Perception of Vowel and Timbre
   When well executed, the timbral change and passive migrations associated 
   with turning over are relatively subtle. If vowels are divided into front and 
   back “families” or sequences, [i ɪ e ɛ æ a] and [u o ʊ ɔ ɑ],1 listed from 
   PRAA.indb   81PRAA.indb   81 7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  82
   Perceptions of Turning Over
   closest to most open, then the passive migration of the vowel will be in the 
   same vowel family or sequence. !e more open, back, or rounded vowels 
   move toward a closer neighbor: [ ɔ] will migrate toward [ ʊ]; [o] toward 
   [ʊ] or [u]. !e more open front vowels close slightly and also medialize 
   or neutralize somewhat: [ɛ] will seem to migrate in the direction of [e] or 
   [ɪ] with a hint of [œ], but with no change of lip shape. Indeed, if these are 
   truly passive migrations, no shape change in the direction of those vowel 
   migrations should be made. !e original vowel and vocal tract shape 
   should be deliberately and expressively maintained through the turn. 
   In particular, while care should be taken to keep the laryngopharyngeal 
   depth factor stable (the unconstricted throat and settled larynx), aspects of 
   the vocal tract shape that are expressively toned and tuned for perceptual 
   “height” and “ring” should be emphasized to avoid excessive dulling of the 
   timbre across the turn. In this regard, active expression “tunes” the height, 
   width, and convergent angle of the tonal “ceiling” somatosense, avoiding a 
   passive, lax, internal droop. !e student may be tempted to move toward 
   excessive orality (yell response), to modify the vowel heavily (by tube 
   lengthening, active vowel substitution, or closing the vowel shape), or 
   simply to collapse the shape toward a neutral “anonymous vowel, ” all of 
   which should be avoided. When well produced, the timbral shi/f_t will feel 
   acoustic, not muscular, and even the change in acoustic sensation will be 
   subtle, not heavy.
   Perception of Tonal Sensation
   !e very term “turning over” is descriptive of the acoustic sensation. 
   !ere is a sense of the top of the tonal sensation arching, internalizing, 
   rounding, tipping or rolling up above the palate and into the head toward 
   the front, in other words, “turning over” and reverberating in the head 
   more as opposed to coming directly out of the mouth as in open timbre. 
   Care should be taken to avoid an instinctive postural chin-tucking in an 
   attempt to precipitate or assist this turning percept. At the same time, 
   there may be an oppositional sensation of the “underbelly” of the tone 
   settling through a relaxed neck into the body and rolling down and under. 
   !is latter aspect counters the reaching up and out sensation typical of the 
   throat constriction of a yell. Together these sensations might seem like the 
   PRAA.indb   82PRAA.indb   82 7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM
   Perceptions of Turning Over 83
   two ends of a scroll rolling up and over toward the front, and down and 
   under into the body. !e “back wall” of an open-throated tonal sensation 
   should neither be moved forward nor moved further back through the 
   turn. !ere is also the sense that the tone is increasingly settled and open 
   in the torso, acoustically collected, with a more concentrated sensation 
   in the oropharynx (rather than hollowed and yawny), and convergent or 
   narrowing to the front, rather than widening.
   Unless whoop timbre is desired, passive migration will not su#ce for 
   the two closest vowels, [i] and [u], which will have closed already in mid-
   bass clef, well below the lower passaggio. !ey will need to be actively 
   opened through the non-treble zona di passaggio to retain a virile quality 
   and continue to open with further ascent. In treble voices, those close 
   vowels will need to be gradually opened early in the treble clef to track 
   whoop timbre. In general, when opening a vowel, the vertical stretching of 
   its internal acoustic sensation should seem to lead the opening. !e jaw 
   opening should follow rather than lead, and feel more like settling in, 
   back, and down than like reaching out and down. While the mouth is 
   opening, though counterintuitive, the tongue dorsum should remain as 
   high as the vowel and pitch will allow to maintain the “bene/f_icial 
   narrowing” needed for the interactive acoustic feedback of convergence.
   Perception by Voice Type and 
   Vowel
   !e lighter the voice type, usually the lighter the turn and modi/f_ication 
   will sound. !e lower, heavier, or deeper the voice type, the more 
   noticeable the turn will likely sound. Leggiero tenors may have rather 
   subtle timbral shi/f_ts, while basses, deep baritones, and dramatic tenors 
   tend to have more obvious, even dramatic shi/f_ts. Since this passaggio is 
   low in treble voices and at lower pressure levels, its eﬀects are noticeable 
   but more subtle. !e turn is also more obvious on open vowels, especially 
   open back vowels such as [ ɔ] and [ɑ]. !e stronger the ring component, 
   if freely gotten and sensed high in the vertical “back room” through the 
   turn, the better the turn and the less overly “covered” the resultant tone. 
   !is may be due to a su#ciently strong singer’s formant or to an eﬀective 
   second resonance:higher harmonic coupling above the turn.
   PRAA.indb   83PRAA.indb   83 7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  84
   Di/uniFB00erence in E/uniFB00ect between Active 
   and Passive Modiﬁcation
   In scalar passages it is most elegant to accomplish a smooth, gradual turning 
   across the span of several pitches. However, the turn can be exaggerated, 
   particularly in non-treble voices, by active vowel modi/f_ication: choosing a 
   more open timbre just below the turn, then creating a deliberate, heavier 
   cover at the turn, especially if coordinated with a shi/f_t to F2 prominence on 
   a higher harmonic. Speci/f_ically, this involves initially opening the timbre, 
   postponing the turn, then subsequently lowering F1 by closing the vowel 
   shape and/or lengthening the vocal tract. Such a move will precipitate a 
   more dramatic, deliberate turn, which—if not too exaggerated—might be 
   eﬀective for strong expressive eﬀect, but should be reserved for special 
   circumstances. Multiple examples of such a use can be heard in tenor 
   Nicolai Gedda’s recording of the Rachmaninoﬀ Vocalise (See Appendix 
   5: Y ouTube Examples: Example 2). Whether or not using this strategy to 
   this extent is ideal in this song is debatable. In this recording Gedda tends 
   not to smooth the transition from open to close. Instead, he o/f_ten sings 
   deliberately open timbres just below the turn, and then overtly “hooks” 
   into the turn. For example, about three minutes into the Vocalise, Gedda 
   chooses to sing an E4 to F#4 from rather open to close timbre, then 
   immediately a/f_terwards sings the F#4 in open timbre, closing only as he 
   moves to G#4 ( Figure 9.1). A skillful singer does have some latitude in 
   having the turn occur at slightly diﬀerent pitches on the same vowel—
   within narrow pitch limits. If the pitch of turning is moved away from its 
   normal location, it is usually delayed to a higher pitch (via opening) for 
   loud dynamic levels, or done at lower pitches (via closing) as a strategy 
   to so/f_ten the dynamic. An [ɑ] vowel held in open timbre higher than its 
   normal turning location will sound a bit spread and blatant, as it does in 
   a few instances in this recording of Gedda on some open F#4’s. !at said, 
   Figure 9.1 Rachmaninoff vocalize excerpt, mm. 26–29.
   PRAA.indb   84PRAA.indb   84 7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM7/10/2025   4:38:53 PM

   Gedda’s excellent, smooth laryngeal registration shi/f_t to mechanism two

   Practical Vocal Acoustics
   10
   Pedagogic Implications of 
   Tube Acoustics
   Since the vocal tract is essentially a quarter-wave tube resonator with 
   natural resonances that vary by tube shape (i.e., that vary by vowel), then 
   the following principles and strategies can form a reliable basis for a 
   coherent, practical acoustic pedagogy.
   General Principles
   z In seeking harmonic:resonance matches, singers are not really 
   free to tune their harmonics. Harmonics automatically occur at 
   mathematical relationships above the fundamental frequency (pitch) 
   upon which they are built and which they are given by the composer 
   to sing. Singers do have the ability to shape the vocal tract to retune 
   the resonances, especially fR1 and fR2, that determine which harmonics 
   are activated and selectively featured.
   z !ere are four possible resonance:harmonic interactions:
   − A resonance can allow a moving harmonic to pass through it (a 
   change of acoustic register).
   − A resonance can be tuned to move across a stable harmonic (a 
   change of vowel and acoustic register on the same pitch).
   − A resonance can be tuned to match and steadily retuned to track 
   a moving harmonic (yell, whoop).
   PRAA.indb   93PRAA.indb   93 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  94
   Pedagogic Implications of Tube Acoustics
   − A resonance can avoid a harmonic coupling: (fR1 kept above 1fo to 
   avoid whoop timbre; or fR2 tuned away from a higher harmonic 
   to avoid shrillness).
   z All acoustic registration events occur relative to the /f_irst resonance/
   /f_irst formant location of each vowel. (!e second formant may also 
   be secondarily involved.)
   z Laryngeal registration adjustments are sometimes independent 
   of acoustic registration events but are also o/f_ten in&uenced by 
   interaction with acoustic registration phenomena.
   z !ere is an audible event whenever any harmonic crosses the /f_irst 
   resonance: a timbral closing when a harmonic rises through fR1, and 
   a timbral opening when a harmonic drops below fR1.
   z If tube length and shape are kept the same, the vowel and timbre 
   will increasingly close with ascending pitch up to whoop timbre and 
   increasingly open with descending pitch.
   z A voice does not turn over at the same pitch for all vowels.
   z Each vowel will turn over just above an octave below its /f_irst formant, 
   where the 2 fo of the pitch being sung rises su#ciently above the 
   vowel’s /f_irst resonance.
   z Other mini-closings or openings—involving higher harmonics 
   intersecting fR1—also occur at locations that are predictable by 
   vowel, pitch, and vocal Fach.
   z Predictable, stable turning/crossing locations indicate a stable tube 
   length and shape.
   z !ere are only two ways to raise the /f_irst formant:
   − tube shortening
   − vowel opening (mouth and jaw opening and dorsum lowering)
   z !ere are only two ways to lower the /f_irst formant:
   − tube lengthening
   − vowel closing (mouth and jaw closing and dorsum raising)
   z Changes in tube length are rarely an appropriate strategy in Western 
   classical technique, except for the extreme high treble register. 
   However, they are typical in most CCM genres.
   z Exercises that explore the shi/f_ts of harmonic:resonance crossings 
   assist students in sorting out:
   − what can stay the same across range and vowel
   − what must change across range and vowel
   PRAA.indb   94PRAA.indb   94 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   Pedagogic Implications of Tube Acoustics 95
   z Awareness of /f_irst formant locations and of the characteristics of 
   resonance:harmonic interactions can form the basis for creative, 
   eﬀective strategies:
   − for basic range development
   − for range or registration challenges that arise within the repertoire
   Acoustic Strategies across Range
   z As long as the pitch being sung is low enough, that is, an octave 
   or more below the /f_irst formant of the vowel being sung, there 
   are enough harmonics available for resonation by the vocal tract 
   formants to preclude the need for signi/f_icant formant tuning beyond 
   assuming a generally resonant posture (an unconstricted throat, 
   li/f_ted palate, fronted tongue, that is, adjusted for convergent, singer’s 
   formant/chiaroscuro timbre).
   z For Western classical timbre, all harmonics other than 1 fo should 
   be allowed to pass through fR1, that is, without formant tracking. 
   !is takes training, since it is instinctive for the /f_irst resonance to 
   track rising harmonics, especially 2 fo (yell timbre) and 1 fo (whoop 
   timbre). For musical theater belt and many world music styles, fR1 
   should track 2fo—as in the yell or call—up to ca. Bb4, but at healthy 
   transglottal pressure diﬀerence and air&ow levels.
   z Below the primary open to close timbre transition (the fR1:2fo 
   crossing), stay relatively close in pronunciation, keeping the /f_irst 
   formant comfortably low for the vowel being sung.
   z Allow 2 fo to rise above fR1 at the /f_irst formant’s normal resonant 
   speech location, causing the primary acoustic transition of “turning 
   over” at that point.
   z Above the fR1:2fo crossing into close timbre, if the vocal tract length 
   and/or vowel closeness are increased, fR1 will be lowered further and 
   the voice will move into whoop timbre earlier (lower), that is, in well 
   less than an octave. !is is a strategy typical of some treble voices, 
   who prefer the more “head-dominant” timbre of Western classical 
   singing.
   PRAA.indb   95PRAA.indb   95 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  96
   z Above the fR1:2fo crossing into close timbre, if the vocal tract length 
   and closeness are maintained, the timbre will become increasingly 
   close until full whoop timbre is reached at the fR1:1fo juncture. !is is 
   also a strategy typical of treble voices, which prefer the more “head-
   dominant” timbre of Western classical singing.
   z Above the fR1:2fo crossing into close timbre, if the vowel is gradually 
   opened within c l o s e  t i m b r e ,  t h a t  i s ,  n o t  s o  m u c h  a s  t o  a l l o w  fR1 
   to overtake 2 fo, whoop timbre can be postponed or even avoided 
   altogether, and more of an extended close/“chest mix” timbre will 
   be maintained. !is is the typical strategy of non-treble voices, who 
   prefer a more virile upper voice, unless seeking a heady, so/f_ter eﬀect. 
   !is is especially needed on those close vowels whose F1 locations 
   are within non-treble range. !is is also a strategy used in high belt 
   mix.
   z At the fR1:1fo coupling, full whoop timbre (very heady/falsetto) is 
   achieved, which is very typical and desirable for treble voices in 
   Western classical singing.
   z When in whoop (fR1:1fo dominant) timbre, if fR2 is close to a higher 
   harmonic, the timbre will be brighter and possibly shrill, depending 
   on how much the higher harmonic is reinforced. In that situation, 
   detuning fR2 (moving it farther away from the harmonic) by active 
   vowel modi/f_ication will increase the timbral warmth and roundness.
   z Above the fR1:1fo (whoop timbre) juncture, the /f_irst resonance/formant 
   must be raised in tandem with the rising fundamental (1fo) in order 
   to maintain timbral fullness or avoid thinning. !is is accomplished 
   primarily by vowel opening, but eventually by tube shortening in the 
   extreme upper range of treble voices (ca. B5 and above).
   z An alternative strategy for treble voices above the fR1:1fo (whoop 
   timbre) juncture might be to allow 1fo (the sung pitch) to rise above 
   fR1 by retaining a closer vowel articulation if a high so/f_t tone with less 
   warmth but more sparkle (a silvery, but thinner “&oat” quality) is 
   sought. At loud dynamic levels, this strategy would result in a shrill 
   tone.
   z When fR1 can be raised no further, ending fR1 tracking of the sung 
   pitch’s 1fo, higher pitches will be in whistle register, being resonated 
   possibly by a clustering of fR1 and fR2 or just by fR2.
   PRAA.indb   96PRAA.indb   96 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   Pedagogic Implications of Tube Acoustics 97
   Dynamics
   z Crescendos increase the intensity of higher partials more than lower 
   partials, creating a shallower spectral slope and brighter timbre with 
   a higher percentage of over-vowel tone color.
   z Decrescendos decrease the intensity of higher partials more than of 
   lower partials, creating a steeper spectral slope and warmer timbre 
   with a lower percentage of over-vowel tone color, and relatively 
   more under-vowel. Since the under-vowel is most o/f_ten featuring 
   the complementary tone color, this results in a vowel migration that 
   is internalizing, rounding, and neutralizing.
   PRAA.indb   97PRAA.indb   97 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   PRAA.indb   98PRAA.indb   98 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics
   11
   Strategies That Encourage 
   Tube Stability and a 
   Convergent Resonator
   The Open Throat
   A vocal tract poised for resonance ( chiaroscuro, singer’s formant timbre) 
   should be prepared prior to singing by means of a relatively open 
   (unconstricted) throat and some degree of tube convergence. However, 
   /f_inding this pre-phonatory posture is challenging for students since our 
   kinesthesia for throat shape is quite misleading. Most people perceive [ɑ] 
   to be the most “open throated” vowel posture and [i] to be the least open 
   throated, when the opposite is in fact the case (see Figure 11.1).
   Several strategies can circumvent this false kinesthesia: a noiseless 
   inhalation, an appropriate F2 frequency target (not too high per vowel), an 
   accurate remapping of the throat and tongue, articulatory e#ciency, and 
   the use of appropriate motivational aﬀects.
   Noiseless Inhalation
   A noiseless inhalation  is evidence  of a su#ciently open throat. It can 
   be accomplished by a subtle palatal li/f_t (felt more frontally, toward the 
   hard palate) accompanied by inhaling in such a way that the front of the 
   PRAA.indb   99PRAA.indb   99 7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM7/10/2025   4:38:54 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  100
   Strategies for Tube Stability and Resonator Convergence
   mouth—the teeth, tongue blade, and hard palate—is cooled rather than 
   the throat. Wherever the vocal tract is the coolest, it is either the point of 
   the earliest air contact and heat exchange, or the narrowest, since that is 
   where air speed will be the quickest and the resultant wind chill eﬀect the 
   strongest. Shaping the mouth and throat so that the cooling is in the front 
   of the mouth causes the throat to be more open and the resonator more 
   convergent.
   Lowering the Second Formant 
   Frequency
   A second strategy to achieve a convergent resonator for a noiseless 
   inhalation is to shape the mouth and throat in a way that lowers the “pitch” 
   and reduces the intensity of inhalatory noise until it is inaudible. High-
   frequency inhalatory noise highlights a raised F2 location, indicating a 
   narrowed throat as is typical in whispering. Lowering the “pitch” and 
   audibility of inhalatory noise deconstricts the throat, lowers the F2 pitch to 
   its more passive pitch location, and allows enough low-frequency content 
   to subtly perceive the F1 location as well. !is does not require laryngeal 
   depression or any sensation of eﬀort in the neck; rather, it is helped by 
   Figure 11.1 Side view of the vocal tract shape of [i] and [ ɑ]. Paradoxical 
   to one’s kinesthesia, [i] is open throated and [ɑ] is fairly closed throated.
   PRAA.indb   100PRAA.indb   100 7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM
   Strategies for Tube Stability and Resonator Convergence 101
   avoiding over-articulation, using internalizing aﬀects such as concerned 
   skepticism, and reconceiving dark timbre as being in front and bright 
   timbre in back.
   Remapping the Throat and Tongue
   When making the false open-throated sensation of the “yawny” [ ɑ], the 
   location of the back throat wall seems to be behind (and below) the ears, 
   an anatomical impossibility. What is most likely being felt are those lateral 
   tongue muscles (styloglossus) that attach to the styloid processes beneath 
   the ears and retract the tongue toward the back throat wall. Remapping the 
   throat wall to its actual location in front of (and below) the ears releases 
   tongue retraction, allowing a passively fronted tongue and more throat 
   space. Furthermore, many 
   singers map the tongue 
   like an inverted “L ” that 
   descends vertically down to 
   the larynx. Although there 
   are some lateral muscle 
   attachments below to the 
   hyoid bone (hyoglossus), 
   the largest portion of the 
   tongue muscle, forming 
   its body, the genioglossus, 
   loops back under the 
   tongue blade, originating 
   at its attachment on the 
   inside back of the chin. Remapping the tongue in this way creates more 
   independence of tongue and larynx and releases the tongue for greater 
   height and passive fronting. (see Figure 11.2.)
   Monitoring Tube Length Stability
   As explained above, the eﬀect of laryngeal position on tube length 
   stability can be monitored—in students whose anatomy lends itself 
   Figure 11.2 Mapping the tongue.
   PRAA.indb   101PRAA.indb   101 7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  102
   to easy identi/f_ication of laryngeal structure—by gently palpating the 
   thyrohyoid space (see Figure 8.1 , p. 66). !e thyrohyoid space should 
   be relatively open and not rise and close appreciably (as it does in 
   swallowing) with pitch ascent. !ough this space varies in size between 
   individuals, it may also have been reduced in size by residual tension 
   from the unnecessary, habitual activation of swallowing muscles 
   during speech production. If such behavior is entrenched, it should 
   be patiently but persistently countered and broken, preferably through 
   therapy with a licensed, voice-specialized speech-language pathologist 
   familiar with appropriate therapies. Eﬀective strategies include massage 
   and stretching of the thyrohyoid space, monitoring the looseness and 
   openness of the thyrohyoid space during speech production, and 
   vocalizing with a passively low larynx and a wide, extended “rolled” 
   tongue position. Second, tube length stability can be assessed by 
   visually monitoring the location of the Adam’s apple. !is method is 
   more useful for biological males, due to their larger average laryngeal 
   anatomy, and for those with less &eshy, trimmer necks in which 
   structures are more easily visible. Finally, tube length stability can be 
   assessed by aurally monitoring the consistency of timbral depth. !is 
   is less reliable, since it can be falsely mimicked by various articulatory 
   manipulations of tongue and lips, but careful listening and observation 
   can reduce this liability.
   E/uniFB03ciency of Articulation
   Another strategy for maintaining a convergent resonator while singing 
   is to minimize the jawing of diction, reducing excessive orality. Neither 
   vocal resonance, nor vowel articulation, nor vowel diﬀerentiation require 
   exaggerated jaw drop or excessive articulatory change in the lower and 
   middle range, as long as there is su#cient internal space. On the contrary, 
   vowels can seem fairly close to each other in formation. All of these 
   strategies improve throat ease and openness, and a passively settled F1, 
   increasing resonator convergence.
   PRAA.indb   102PRAA.indb   102 7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM
   Strategies for Tube Stability and Resonator Convergence 103
   Use of A/uniFB00ect to Position and 
   Stabilize the Resonator
   A completely lax vocal structure is vulnerable to sudden changes of 
   pressure or situation and is therefore incompatible with optimal 
   resonance, extremes of dynamic and range, and control of expressive 
   timbral variety. Aﬀect can position and stabilize the vocal apparatus 
   more eﬀectively than direct physical manipulation. Speci/f_ic, ample, and 
   sincere expressions are typically accompanied by poised, toned vocal 
   tract con/f_igurations which are physically stable but stop short of overt 
   muscular manipulation or rigidity. Early Italian pedagogues maintained 
   that inhaling “through a smile” opened the throat. !is is more a 
   matter of the internal, pharyngeal poise of genuine, deep expression 
   of pleasure than about lip shape, though facial expression follows 
   secondarily. More recently a number of these aﬀective positionings 
   of the vocal tract have been tested and documented by voice teacher/
   researcher Jo Estill and reported by Kimberly Steinhauer (2008). For 
   example, a suppressed laugh may stimulate retraction of the false vocal 
   folds, reducing the possibility of pressed phonation. Strong amusement 
   or mischief can li/f_t and stabilize the so/f_t palate and comfortably /f_irm 
   the pharynx.
   Tonal “Placement” Sensations
   Any discussion about tonal “placement” sensation, that is, locational 
   somatosense, is risky, since the placement of sound is an illusion in the 
   /f_irst place, and perception of tonal sensations are somewhat variable 
   from individual to individual in the second place. Furthermore, tonal 
   sensation is comprised of various components, probably due to a voice 
   having multiple resonances and complex pressure waves with multiple 
   nodes and antinodes. Attention can therefore be drawn to any single 
   aspect of a tone’s overall sensation. Finally, the spectral content (number 
   and spacing of harmonics) of low-pitched sounds is dramatically diﬀerent 
   PRAA.indb   103PRAA.indb   103 7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM7/10/2025   4:38:55 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  104
   from that of high-pitched sounds, as is frequency-dependent vibrotactile 
   sensation (Ballantyne 2025), generating changes in somatosense across 
   range. It is therefore prudent to proceed with caution and &exibility 
   when suggesting tonal “placement” sensations. Nonetheless, there 
   is both considerable overlap in historically reported sensation, with 
   successful historic pedagogy being rife with the sensorial terminology 
   of procedural knowledge —what it feels like to do an activity. !ere is 
   also potential acoustic basis for locations of tonal sensations. Consider 
   the diﬀerences in acoustic sensation between yelling (2 fo do mina n t fR1, 
   with strong upper partials) and whooping (1fo dominant fR1, with weaker 
   upper partials). Although we now know that vibrotactile sensation only 
   extends to 1000 Hz, the presence of strong, high partials aﬀects overall 
   intensity and may thereby strengthen the somatosense of lower, feelable 
   spectral content. High frontal sensory feedback is o/f_ten reported by the 
   singer, corollary to high spectral content in the sound, even though high-
   frequency content is out of range of somatosense (Ballantyne 2025). 
   First formant/fundamental frequency-dominant sounds are reported as 
   feeling rounder and sensed more centrally in the head and pharyngeal 
   column.
   Second Formant “Placement” 
   Sensations
   In this regard, front and back vowel placement sensations are paradoxical. 
   It is innate and universal in humans to perceive bright as forward and 
   dark as in back. One might then assume that front vowels are felt in 
   the front and back vowels in the back. Curiously, almost the opposite 
   is the case (see Figure 11.3 ) when acoustically e#cient. !is may be 
   due to the locations of the pressure nodes of the standing waveform of 
   vowel formants. For a balanced overall timbre and sensation, noticing or 
   anticipating the acoustic energy location of the target vowel tone color—
   for speech range pitches at least—according to the locations mapped 
   below, helps to keep vowels “aligned, ” the tongue dorsum su#ciently

   Strategies for Tube Stability and Resonator Convergence 105
   high and fronted, the larynx and /f_irst formant low, the resonator 
   convergent, and the timbre balanced or chiaroscuro. !is is more evident 
   in non-treble range, since for higher pitches with appropriate vowel 
   modi/f_ication (vowel opening), tonal sensations become increasingly 
   vertical and centralized in the head.

   The Acoustics of Belting
   Increasingly over the last thirty years or so, voice teachers, including those 
   trained primarily or exclusively in unampli/f_ied Western classical singing, 
   have been called upon to address a wider variety of singing styles. While 
   this text focuses more on the acoustics of unampli/f_ied classical singing 
   practice, another dominant, alternative muscular and acoustic strategy 
   appears to be the basis of—or at least to play a signi/f_icant role in—many 
   other styles of singing. !at vocal style possibly evolved from “calling” 
   over distances, but is now referred to in musical theater circles as belting, 
   and will be brie&y addressed below. Research and conclusions about 
   belting are still evolving. As of this publication, the dominant paradigm 
   observes that belting, acoustically speaking, is a skillful, sustainable form 
   of what we have here de/f_ined acoustically as the yell: a strong fR1 tracking 
   of 2fo above the normal frequency of F1 for the vowel(s) in question. It 
   is no surprise that this mode of phonation is a very common strategy in 
   popular music, world music, as well as in musical theater. Its unskillful, 
   raw form—yelling—is universal among humans. It is powerful, usually 
   clear, high in energy, expressive, and emotionally strong. Because of its 
   higher energy and potentially high transglottal pressure diﬀerence levels, 
   it also poses higher health risks if unskillfully executed. Y et it is also 
   apparent that those singers who master the skills necessary for this form 
   of vocalism manage to sustain long and healthy careers. Let’s /f_irst review 
   the acoustic characteristics of the raw yell:
   Yell Characteristics Reviewed
   Y ell timbre and function are accomplished by raising the /f_irst formant 
   through:
   PRAA.indb   107PRAA.indb   107 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
   Practical Vocal Acoustics  108
   The Acoustics of Belting
4) tube shortening (larynx raising),
5) pharyngeal narrowing, and
6) mouth widening;
7) that is, by creating a more divergent resonator shape.
   !e /f_irst two of these maneuvers are normally accomplished by 
   activating swallowing muscles, which raise and partially constrict the 
   laryngopharynx, narrowing the throat and preventing the use of the 
   singer’s formant chiaroscuro timbre. Furthermore, a higher laryngeal 
   position facilitates glottal closure strength, potentially in&uencing the 
   vibrator function toward pressed phonation, especially if the brighter 
   “over-vowel” component is conceptually fronted.
   Y elling is typically done on open vowels like [a] or [ ɛ], because their 
   high F1 locations and inherently divergent shapes make them more 
   compatible with the acoustic requirements of the yell. !e yell is necessarily 
   chest register (TA) or mechanism one dominant.
   Belting: The Skillful Yell or Call
   (see Semantic Diﬀerences/Belting and Y elling, p. 122.)
   Which characteristics of yelling are necessary for belting and which 
   modi/f_ications of raw yelling are needed for skillful belting? !e issue is in 
   large part how to accomplish the acoustic eﬀect of yelling at breath pressure 
   (transglottal pressure diﬀerence), breath &ow, and glottal resistance levels 
   that are sustainable and healthy.
   At present, there seems to be general agreement on the following 
   principles:
8) Belting is bright and perceptually speech- or call-like in quality.
9) Belting calls for some divergence in vocal tract shaping.
10) !erefore, the three characteristics of yelling listed above seem 
    necessary to some degree (elevated larynx, narrowed pharynx, 
    widened mouth).
11) Vibrational mechanism one is extended higher—than in classical 
    treble voice singing at least—but may be achieved with a 
    somewhat thinner vocal fold shape (still thicker than mechanism 
    PRAA.indb   108PRAA.indb   108 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    The Acoustics of Belting 109
    two, but somewhat less thick—referred to by some as “mix” when 
    su#ciently high), that retains the auditory roughness (buzziness) 
    of “chest” timbre but with less he/f_t, sheer breath force, and air&ow.
12) Belt only really commences above the normal fR1:2fo intersections, 
    especially of the mid to open vowels, where the possibility of yelling 
    would also start. In other words, belt is initially an extension of 
    open timbre, but not all open timbre is in belt mode.
    Mouth widening is usually necessary in order to achieve and maintain fR1 
    tracking of 2fo. However, in order to reduce transglottal pressure diﬀerence 
    and resultant glottal resistance to sustainable levels:
13) !e degree of larynx raising and pharynx narrowing is probably 
    moderated somewhat (some belt specialists even maintain that 
    while the larynx is not lowered for belt, raising the larynx is not 
    necessary);
14) Contraction/adduction of the false vocal folds may be inhibited by 
    means of strategies such as the “suppressed laugh” aﬀect (Estill);
15) Breath &ow is signi/f_icantly reduced to avoid the “air blast” of a raw yell;
16) Some downstream “bene/f_icial narrowing” in the pharynx and/or 
    at the tongue dorsum:palatal narrowing is employed to achieve 
    “twang, ” (a brassy quality created by stronger high harmonics 
    and strong higher formant components analogous to the singer’s 
    formant cluster of classical sound, but more spread out, higher, 
    and without the complementary depth). !is strategy improves 
    e#ciency: more acoustic output for less pressure input.
    Among specialists in belt, strategies are employed that:
17) achieve clean, /f_irm, sustainable glottal closure throughout the 
    range to ensure high-frequency spectral content.
18) maximize ring/twang within the limits of the character being 
    portrayed.
19) extend a bright, speech-like vowel shaping and quality across 
    the entire range, sacri/f_icing the depth and roundness of Western 
    classical timbre to this brighter aesthetic. It might be termed 
    chiarochiaro (bright/bright) timbre, in contrast and comparison to 
    the chiaroscuro (bright/dark) timbre of classical singing.
20) the choice of brighter vowels  (higher frequency F2s and target 
    spectral tone colors).

21) though the resultant sound can move in the direction of pressed 
    phonation and may sound pressed to ears accustomed to Western 
    classical timbre, the actual function can be accomplished by 
    acoustic strategies that emphasize upper partials and formants, 
    coupled with a level of glottal closure that is clean and strong but 
    is nonetheless comfortable, sustainable, and healthy, in which the 
    transglottal pressure diﬀerence, which determines the necessary 
    glottal resistance level, is kept su#ciently low.
    Muscular Factors in Belting
    !ere are muscular or biomechanical factors involving the vocal folds, the 
    vocal tract, and breath management that accompany skillful, sustainable 
    belting.
    Vocal Fold Factors
    As mentioned above, the thicker vocal fold shape of vibrational mechanism 
    one (chest) is maintained into the upper voice of non-treble belters and 
    extended through the middle voice of treble voice belters. Opinions vary 
    as to just how far treble voices can extend this mechanism, but range at 
    least to C5, if not higher. Individuals will certainly vary in achievable 
    ability. However, one respected source reports that Broadway casters 
    typically post the following expectations: “Must belt to D, must mix to F , 
    must sing legit to A. ” (Lovetri 2012).
    Mix
    !e question of “mix” is controverted. !ere is no actual separate physical 
    register for mix, if by register we mean a discrete laryngeal, muscularly 
    caused function with its own distinct timbre. Many teachers—and 
    Broadway casting agents—nonetheless use the term. What is probably 
    meant by most for the term “mix” is:
    PRAA.indb   110PRAA.indb   110 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    The Acoustics of Belting 111
22) an area of cooperation between the vocal fold shortening, 
    thickening thyroarytenoids and the vocal fold-stretching, thinning 
    cricothyroids; that nonetheless
23) stays essentially in mechanism one (and retains its characteristic 
    “buzziness”); but
24) with a moderated thickness; and
25) an acoustic strategy that enables sustainably lower transglottal 
    pressure diﬀerence levels.
    A more recent investigator (Howell) observes that, although the fR1:2fo 
    tracking strategy that extends open timbre higher does maintain the 
    intensity of that tracked second harmonic, the auditory roughness 
    (“buzziness”) and brassy quality that characterize belt do not come 
    from that strong 2 fo. !e second harmonics of belt range sounds 
    remain within the spectral tone color range of [~ ɔ] and [~ ɑ] and can 
    only add a warming, rounding eﬀect. Higher-numbered harmonics 
    are needed, both for brighter timbre and for buzzier roughness. !ose 
    belt characteristics are supplied by stronger, closely clustered, higher 
    harmonics ((6 fo) being resonated by higher vocal tract resonances. 
    Apparently, 2fo tracking and intensity facilitate stronger, higher partials 
    and formants as well.
    Vocal Tract Factors
    In addition to the generally more divergent vocal tract shape, most belt 
    specialists would agree on the need for a relatively poised, stabilized vocal 
    tract, toned and tuned for maximal ring. !is would involve:
26) a /f_irmed, li/f_ted, fronted so/f_t palate sensation, done in such a way as 
    not to darken the timbre, rather in a way that facilitates ring;
27) a narrowed aryepiglottic exit and/or pharyngeal narrowing to 
    maximize twang/upper harmonics;
28) avoidance of false vocal fold compression, which might introduce 
    a noise element and require higher, less sustainable breath pressure 
    levels;
29) more lateral lip opening than is typical for Western classical 
    timbre.
    PRAA.indb   111PRAA.indb   111 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  112
    All of these shaping, positioning factors should be accomplished as 
    comfortably as possible. !e muscles involved in shaping will not be 
    lax; rather, expressively, comfortably /f_irm, though not felt to be tight or 
    constricted. Safe motivations for such positioning include various strong 
    aﬀects (such as con/f_ident assertion) that stimulate the desired response. 
    When the optimal tuning of the resonator is achieved, the sounds produced 
    will have maximal comfort at the vocal folds and neck and e#cient air&ow 
    levels, as if the sound originates from the top of the pharynx or higher, 
    not from the lower throat or larynx. Strategies (sensorial, procedural 
    intentions) that can assist the e#cient, brighter timbres required of the 
    belt aesthetic include: a relatively high tongue dorsum; pleasurable aﬀects 
    that cause a stretchy smile sensation across the mid-hard palate just in 
    front of the molars; tipping the head up a bit to “expose” your hard palate; 
    avoidance of dorsum lowering and tongue &attening; the eye-squint/nose-
    scrunch look that accompanies con/f_ident assertion; sensing the sound 
    higher, in and above the hard palate rather than low in the fore-mouth; 
    and a short mouth-to-back wall distance perception.
    Breath Management Factors
    While sharing the characteristics of good function common to other 
    styles, breath management for belting does call for especially high 
    energization, lower air&ow, and somewhat higher air pressure levels. 
    Although active contraction of the thorax (bearing down in a Valsalva 
    maneuver) to achieve these diﬀerences is not recommended, there may 
    well be an awareness of the higher abdominal muscle contraction levels 
    that accompany the stronger, dramatic aﬀects expressed in belting. Excess 
    thoracic compression can be avoided by stabilizing ribcage expansion to 
    some degree. For example, the muscles that pull the shoulders laterally 
    down and out are attached to and expand the ribs, inhibiting their 
    downward and inward compression, as can the external intercostal 
    muscles. Motivating this upper chest expansion with an aﬀective stimulus 
    (such as excitement) can reduce the risk of counterproductive rigidity or 
    active chest compression. A sense of whole-body involvement and full 
    but unforced commitment to expression will help distribute the extra 
    PRAA.indb   112PRAA.indb   112 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    The Acoustics of Belting 113
    work that the high energy levels of belting require. A tonal locational 
    somatosense that is higher, above the palate or in the area of the 
    nasopharynx rather than in the throat, recruits less forceful breath use. 
    !e singer should not perceive the higher pressure levels to be localized 
    just below the vocal folds or in the neck; rather, they should have a sense 
    of a higher energy involvement of the entire body that is best organized 
    by sincere expression.

    Semantic Di/uniFB00erences
    It is an understatement to observe that there has always been a variety 
    of vocal pedagogic opinion and terminology. To be sure, a teacher’s 
    choice of words—given the variety of possible attendant associations and 
    implications—can have signi/f_icant pedagogic rami/f_ications. Furthermore, 
    the procedural knowledge needed for motor intentions, being subjective 
    and sensorial, is di#cult to articulate and requires descriptive &exibility. 
    !is leads to inevitable semantic diﬀerences and disagreements. However, 
    the extent of disagreement may well be reduced by honest, open dialogue 
    and a clearer de/f_inition of terms. Several such controverted terms will be 
    discussed below for the sake of clarity and good will.
    Cover Terminology
    As has been pointed out earlier, the primary turn of the voice from voce 
    aperta to voce chiusa at the fR1:2fo crossing is associated with the historic 
    terms vocal cover and covered. !is nomenclature unfortunately has 
    signi/f_icant baggage and does not mean the same thing to all teachers or 
    past great singers. In a public master class, Italian tenor Luciano Pavarotti 
    demonstrated and used the term cover—in the opinion of this author—
    for the same phenomenon that this text refers to as the passive vowel 
    migration that accompanies a well-executed, resonant turn of the voice. 
    (See Appendix 5: Y ouTube Examples: Example 7, p. 184.) Furthermore, in 
    another interview, Pavarotti described cover as “closing, ” a darker timbre, 
    and more elegant (See Y ouTube Examples: Example 8); and /f_inally, in a 
    third interview, went as far as to claim that if you do not cover, you are “not 
    PRAA.indb   115PRAA.indb   115 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  116
    Semantic Di/uniFB00erences
    a real tenor” (See Y ouTube Examples: Example 9). Spanish tenor Alfredo 
    Kraus maintained that he didn’t believe in cover, and though his timbre 
    was in general on the bright and at times open side, his voice did in fact 
    turn over at or near predictable locations. !ere are many such variant 
    views and uses of terms to be found, expressed by people that nonetheless 
    seem to sing and/or teach successfully. Some prefer to eliminate or at 
    least reduce any awareness of the passaggio or passaggio events. Indeed, 
    when well executed, sensations accompanying the turn will usually seem 
    subtle, acoustic, even automatic, rather than muscular, enabling an ease of 
    production. Others, such as American tenor Rockwell Blake (in a public 
    master class), described the zona di passaggio—as Garcia did—as a matter 
    of “adding dark timbre” in order to preserve an adult sound (See Appendix 
    5: Y ouTube Examples: Example 10). It is fairly easy to appreciate that an 
    open yell will sound younger, less elegant, immature, shallower, and more 
    strained in timbre. Allowing the fR1:2fo turn adds or retains depth and 
    color, results in a greater percentage of the “under-vowel” tone color, and 
    may indeed seem darker in timbre to the singer, but need not sacri/f_ice ring 
    to any great extent for the audience, and will enhance a more organized 
    version of timbral ring than that of the yell.
    Connecting Terms with Acoustic 
    Strategies
    Just below the point of the fR1:2fo crossing there are at least three possible 
    strategies that parallel the three strategies listed earlier just above the turn 
    (see Resonance Strategies between Turning and Whoop, p. 52):
30) !e /f_irst resonance//f_irst formant could be actively lowered to 
    precipitate the turn earlier/lower. It is precisely this type of 
    heavy c o v e r—accompanied by a more dramatic darkening and 
    modi/f_ication of the vowel—which many /f_ind objectionable. 
    Unfortunately, this strategy is equated by some teachers and 
    singers with the very de/f_inition of cover. !e physical correlates 
    to this approach include a closer vowel pronunciation, a lowered 
    larynx, and rounded or even trumpeted (extended) lips—in other 
    words, active tube lengthening and vowel closing.
    PRAA.indb   116PRAA.indb   116 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Semantic Di/uniFB00erences117
31) !e /f_irst resonance//f_irst formant location could be deliberately 
    maintained, allowing the passive migration that occurs as 2fo slips 
    above fR1 as the sung pitch is raised. !is is what the term cover 
    means in this text. !e vowel is allowed to migrate smoothly to 
    a slightly closer neighbor by means of pitch change, not shape 
    change. !e original vocal tract and vowel shape and internal 
    height is in fact deliberately maintained through the passaggio, 
    while the vowel quality and locational somatosense is allowed to 
    migrate subtly. With this approach it is important not to collapse 
    the sense of internal height (top back “corners” near the jaw joints) 
    or vowel shape, not to “give in” to the vowel migration with shape 
    changes, nor to form the speech-level shape of the resultant tone 
    color or more neutral under-vowel as if it were the target vowel 
    (unless a more dramatic turn is desired). !e vocal tract form is 
    most eﬀectively maintained by sustaining the motivating aﬀect.
32) !e /f_irst resonance//f_irst formant could be either deliberately or 
    instinctively raised by vowel opening, extending open timbre 
    upward and delaying the turn of the voice. !is can only be done 
    well if diction allows and just for a few pitches higher without 
    turning into an open yell.
    Furthermore, the terms cover and covered, while accurately describing the 
    subjective procedural perception that the sound feels more internalized 
    and less exposed, might imply a muting or mu)ing of the sound, qualities 
    not usually desired. For these reasons, most teachers avoid these terms 
    and substitute instead “turning over” and “turned over” as well as “the 
    turn of the voice. ” In working out the transition zone with a young non-
    treble singer, it may be temporarily necessary and useful to overcorrect a 
    strong yell instinct with strategy number one: a deliberate precipitation of 
    the turn, by lowering fR1 through moving to a closer vowel and/or slightly 
    longer tube. !is maneuver may result in a tone that is too dark, dull, 
    and potentially too heavy for a /f_inal product. !at would require eventual 
    rebalancing. Subsequently, attempting a smooth “timbral glissando” on a 
    single pitch—from an open timbre slowly and smoothly through the turn 
    to a close timbre—may pass through an ideal timbral balance along the 
    way. For example: have a tenor voice sing the following progression: [ɛ ɪ e i]  
    on a D4, gradually and simultaneously closing the vowel and deepening 
    the timbre by elevating the tongue dorsum in back. If the larynx is stably 
    PRAA.indb   117PRAA.indb   117 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  118
    low, the timbre should close either on the [e] or between the [ ɪ] and the 
    [e], but most de/f_initely by the [i]. A sequence of words containing those 
    vowels may facilitate naturalness of expression and articulation: “bet, bit, 
    bait, beet, ” or “bed, bid, bayed, bead. ” !en try this exercise on more or 
    less open versions of the same vowel. If very near its pitch of turning, 
    it should be possible to perform a vowel in open, turning, and turned/
    close timbres. All of these explorations will bene/f_it from some expressive 
    motivation, since expression coordinates phonation at a deep level.
    Timbral Terminology: Close or 
    Closed
    Both adjectives close /klo ʊs/ and closed /klo ʊzd/ are found in phonetic 
    literature to describe vowels with a low /f_irst formant and convergent 
    resonator shape. !ere are advantages and disadvantages with either 
    term: closed is more properly the opposite of open since close means near 
    rather than closed. On the other hand, a vowel can be more or less open, 
    but can something be more or less closed? To use an analogy, a door is 
    either open or closed. Once it is open, it can be more or less open, but it 
    can’t be more or less closed. Closed does not allow for degrees of a quality. 
    Furthermore, close /kloʊz/ can also be a verb, like open, and as such is 
    useful as a pedagogic directive.
    Physically the terms originate from the degree of closeness of the hump 
    of the tongue dorsum to the hard palate. A close vowel has the tongue 
    hump close to the hard palate, and an open vowel does not. !e tongue 
    can be closer or less close, but it doesn’t achieve any physical state that 
    could literally be called closed with vowel articulation, since with vowels 
    there is always some space between the tongue and palate for air and 
    sound to &ow out of the mouth.
    !e International Phonetic Association uses the term close /kloʊs/. 
    !is author has chosen to follow that usage, personally /f_inding it more 
    compatible with the pedagogic concept and use of degrees of closeness 
    (close, more or less closely), opening or closing a vowel further, open and 
    close timbre, and so on. Arguments can be made against close for the term 
    closed. It is hoped that this semantic diﬀerence will neither diminish 
    appreciation of the concept nor prevent its useful practical application.
    PRAA.indb   118PRAA.indb   118 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Semantic Di/uniFB00erences119
    Open and Close
    A further conundrum exists with the terms open a n d  close, having to 
    do with their use for two diﬀerent, though related conditions: open and 
    close vowel designations and open and close acoustic registers. Open 
    and close vowels were described immediately above. Open and close 
    registers have to do with harmonic:resonance relationships, speci/f_ically 
    whether or not the second harmonic of a sound has surpassed the /f_irst 
    resonance of the vowel being sung. Paradoxically, an open vowel can be 
    sung in close timbre if the sung pitch is su#ciently high, that is, less than 
    an octave below the formant of the vowel being sung. An [a] is the most 
    open vowel but will be in close timbre above ca. F4 in most voices and in 
    whoop timbre at and above ca. F5. And [i] is the closest cardinal vowel but 
    can be in open timbre below ca. E3, again depending upon the voice type. 
    !ese relationships can be dependably, predictably charted by voice type, 
    as in earlier Figures 6.4 and 8.2, and in Appendices II & III.
    Laryngeal Register Terminology
    !is discussion has a long history! Many terms have been employed in the 
    voice science literature of the past /f_i/f_ty years: heavy and light mechanisms, 
    modal and lo/f_t, along with chest, head, and falsetto.
    Vibrational Mechanisms1 versus 
    Head and Chest
    !e most current (though not yet prevalent) designations for vocal 
    registers are:
33) Mechanism 0 for vocal fry/pulse
34) Mechanism 1 for thick vocal folds, TA involvement in vibration, 
    “chest voice” (higher contact quotient: greater than 50 percent)
35) Mechanism 2 for thin vocal folds, vocal ligament vibration, “head 
    voice” (lower contact quotient: less than 40 percent)
    PRAA.indb   119PRAA.indb   119 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  120
36) Mechanism 3 for whistle register, epithelial vibration.
    !ese designations reference declarative knowledge of the varieties of 
    vocal fold tissue physiology involved rather than the procedural knowledge 
    conveyed by the locational somatosense of vibration (head, chest). !ey 
    arise from particular muscular functions and shapings, plus aerodynamic 
    and acoustic factors. Mechanism one is thought to be TA dominant, 
    involving the vocalis muscle in its vibration. Mechanism two is thought to 
    be CT dominant, involving the vibration of the vocal ligament. Recently 
    mechanism three has been observed to only involve vibration of the 
    epithelial cover when above E6 (Titze 2025). !ese demarcations appear 
    to be due to the stretch/strain resistance of diﬀerent kinds of tissue, which 
    both facilitate and limit the frequencies at which they can be brought into 
    and sustain vibration. To some extent, the mechanisms also have attendant 
    acoustic characteristics, with thicker folds and higher contact quotients 
    having more high spectral content and buzziness, and thinner folds with 
    lower contact quotients having fewer partials and less auditory roughness. 
    In certain circumstances, toggling from one mechanism to another may 
    be triggered by destabilizing acoustic factors.
    !is author happily embraces the new terminology, especially for its 
    use in declarative discussions of the eﬀect of vibrational characteristics on 
    spectral slope and timbre. Vibrational mechanism presents as binary in 
    novice singers and tends to remain so in almost all advanced singers that 
    have been measured with EGG. !at is to say, though excellent singers 
    o/f_ten accomplish smooth perceptual aural transitions, few singers 
    accomplish an absolutely smooth, gradual shi/f_t in vocal fold shape and 
    contact quotient. However, thyroarytenoid and cricothyroid muscle 
    involvement across range is not purely binary (on/oﬀ). Both are involved 
    to varying extents in many of the vocal fold shapes we use. And there are 
    also shapes for which one or the other is mostly or entirely passive.
    !ere are some who, in their zeal for the advantages of this new 
    terminology, are rather intolerant of—even indignant toward—use of the 
    old. !is is unnecessary. Some continued use of the older terminology has 
    this in its favor: most literature prior to the recent decades used it. Unless 
    we refuse to read the historic pedagogic literature, we will at least need to 
    be able to understand and translate these terms. Furthermore, the terms 
    head and chest arose out of the real, commonly shared physical vibrotactile 
    sensations of procedural knowledge. Human perception of such 
    PRAA.indb   120PRAA.indb   120 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Semantic Di/uniFB00erences121
    biofeedback will not disappear with new nomenclature. In that regard, the 
    terms head and chest are general enough in location to allow teachers 
    latitude in specifying locational somatosense further or not. Finally, it is 
    pedagogically useful to have terms that can be adapted to indicate degrees 
    of a quality, such as “headier” and “chestier. ” (One-ier and two-ier just don’t 
    work.) We can also add to our procedural output target descriptors the 
    registration-relevant psychoacoustic percepts, “rougher” and “smoother. ”
    Some have argued that the old terms indicate a gender bias. On the 
    contrary, we all have heads and chests and can experience related locational 
    somatosensory percepts. Only within pedagogies that strongly associate 
    one or the other with a gender, and mistakenly limit by gender the use of 
    its complement, is there potential bias. !ere is no inherent bias in the 
    words themselves, only this possible associative one, which is surely a 
    dying association. Even babies have and use both laryngeal registers. So 
    do well-trained singers of any gender identity.
    !is author anticipates and welcomes increasing usage of the 
    terminology of laryngeal mechanisms. In our acceptance of the new, there 
    is no real need either to erase or avoid historic terminology. It is a fruitless, 
    divisive battle, not worth the trouble, and not likely to be won.
    Mix and Falsetto
    Another semantic dilemma involves the terms mix and falsetto. Mix is used 
    in musical theater circles as well as in some Western classical pedagogies 
    to refer to an area of cooperation between TA and CT muscles. It seems 
    perhaps more o/f_ten to be used to describe a variant of mechanism one, that 
    is, to be TA dominant, but with less thickness or vertical phase diﬀerence 
    and with a squaring up of the opposing vocal fold vertical surfaces, which 
    facilitates ease through a lower phonation threshold pressure. !ere are no 
    new muscles involved, and therefore there is no separate mix register per 
    se, but it does seem to indicate a subtle but important acoustically induced 
    valve behavior and timbral distinction and a less heavy production. !is 
    facilitates extending the buzzier timbre of mechanism one and competent 
    vocal fold closure at higher pitches without resorting to the excessive 
    levels of transglottal pressure diﬀerence and glottal resistance that occur 
    in pressed phonation.
    PRAA.indb   121PRAA.indb   121 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  122
    Falsetto is o/f_ten understood to be a breathy version of mechanism two, 
    with a glottal gap between the arytenoids allowing air leakage and a 
    resultant weaker phonation. To others, falsetto is analogous to an e#cient 
    non-breathy mechanism two, something that some have called “reinforced 
    falsetto. ” !e migration of auditory roughness across range can be a 
    helpful metric, both for training smooth laryngeal registration as well as 
    for labeling its transitional levels. It focuses on intention and perception of 
    the output targets rather than on control of their physiologic mechanisms, 
    in agreement with the recommendations of motor learning research. If we 
    understand “chest mix” to be a lighter extension of mechanism one via a 
    migration of its auditory roughness to a less coarse buzziness, “head mix” 
    to be a version of mechanism two that has both competent closure and an 
    even more re/f_ined “sizzle, ” and falsetto to be the smooth-timbred 
    culmination of mechanism two, fully realized with no auditory roughness, 
    we might be able to overcome semantic diﬀerences and continue 
    pedagogically productive conversation.
    Belting and Yelling
    !is text refers to belting by de/f_inition as a skillful form of yelling. !is 
    is not intended to be pejorative nor to suggest that eﬀective belters are 
    just yelling in the manner found, for example, at sports events. !is is 
    essentially an acoustic observation. !e same basic acoustic strategy 
    that is employed in the yell is also employed in belting: a /f_irst resonance 
    tracking of the second harmonic. However, unskillful yelling is done at 
    injurious transglottal pressure diﬀerence and glottal resistance levels, 
    o/f_ten with noise elements that further raise health risks, both of which 
    can result in hoarseness. Well-performed belting is sustainable and does 
    not result in hoarseness or other unhealthy consequences, even with the 
    heavy performance schedules of the musical theater industry. Any high-
    energy vocalism (operatic singing included)—if done poorly, for too long, 
    within too short of a time span, and without su#cient recovery time—can 
    lead to fatigue, in&ammation, and further health issues. !ere is, however, 
    another justi/f_ication for relating belting to yelling. One of the reasons that 
    belting and its relatives in many forms of world music are so compellingly 
    expressive is precisely their natural connection to yelling. We yell in times 
    PRAA.indb   122PRAA.indb   122 7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM7/10/2025   4:38:56 PM
    Semantic Di/uniFB00erences123
    of high energy, high drama, and high stakes. Belting takes full advantage of 
    that natural expressive association. Anyone who teaches healthy belting, 
    of course, must exercise great care in distinguishing that skill from raw, 
    injurious yelling. Some may prefer to use the term “call” to avoid implying 
    the rough force that can attach to the term “yelling. ” Nonetheless, from a 
    purely acoustic point of view, the main tuning factor that de/f_ines both belt 
    and yell is fR1:2fo tracking, hence their associative connection in this text 
    on vocal acoustics. Belting is, however, a modi/f_ied, skillful, and—when 
    properly done—completely healthy form of the yell acoustic strategy.

    community prefers mechanism since mode has been used for other 
    aspects of vocal fold vibrational physiology (eigenmodes). I defer to that 
    preference in this edition.

    Acoustic Explorations
    Exploring Vowel Turning
    !e following explorations and exercises are good for experiencing 
    the phenomenon and sensations of vowel turning with beginning 
    students as well as for re/f_ining its coordination with intermediate and 
    advanced students. Once the basic principles of tube acoustics and 
    harmonic:resonance interactions are understood, one’s creativity in 
    cra/f_ting explorations may be endless. Most of these exercises also assist 
    with exploring the dynamic laryngeal registration needed for free and 
    smooth range negotiation, since the acoustic poles of open timbre and 
    whoop timbre tend respectively to trigger full chest and full head laryngeal 
    register adjustments, and any attempted smooth movement between them 
    provides opportunities for intermediate laryngeal adjustments to occur. 
    (For examples, see Appendix 4 and http://www .kenbozeman .com/)
    Di/uniFB00erences between Treble and 
    Non-Treble Ranges
    Acoustic transitions involve relationships between the potential 
    harmonics of the sung pitch and /f_irst resonance//f_irst formant locations. 
    All of the latter lie in the treble clef, and the intervallic spread of the 
    /f_irst several harmonics signi/f_icantly reduces the number of interactions 
    treble voices will experience. Non-treble voices are primarily either in 
    open or close timbre, rarely in whoop timbre. Treble voices are mostly in 
    PRAA.indb   125PRAA.indb   125 7/10/2025   4:38:57 PM7/10/2025   4:38:57 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  126
    Acoustic Explorations
    close timbre or whoop timbre, only occasionally in open timbre (belters 
    somewhat more). !is means that non-treble voices, which span most of 
    the bass clef and only some of the treble clef, will experience rather more 
    acoustic transitions (mini-closures and closures) than treble voices. !e 
    explorations below will therefore need to be adjusted according to range, 
    sung pitch, and formant locations. !e essential training goal is learning 
    to sense and allow appropriate migrations per vowel across range and 
    especially at these harmonic:resonance intersections.
    Beginning Explorations
    z On ascending leaps, encourage timbral depth (fronted under-
    vowel tone color), vowel closeness, and internalized aﬀects and 
    direction (such as sobbiness, empathy, mischief, skepticism, 
    suppressed laugh, or deep conviction). !e aﬀective and 
    internalized directional thoughts are meant to counter the 
    “up and out” reaching tendency that typically accompanies the 
    rising larynx of the “yell” instinct. It is also vital to allow timbral 
    migrations from low range, where the speech-level target vowel 
    tone color and generous auditory roughness (buzziness) obtain, 
    toward more of the sung vowel’s complementary tone color, a 
    smoother timbre, and the locational somatosense of higher range 
    that shi/f_ts from being mostly oral to extending both above the 
    palate and below the larynx.
    z Expressively glissando up from a buzzy open vowel on a lower 
    pitch over a large interval to a smooth close vowel (and if high 
    enough, into whoop timbre) on a high pitch to identify the poles of 
    kinesthetic and aural sensation. Tube length and laryngeal position 
    should remain passively constant across the leaps.
    z Repeat this on the same vowel across its pitch of turning, anticipating, 
    allowing, or if necessary, encouraging its passive migration (by 
    means of the suggestions in the /f_irst exploration above).
    z For the upper passaggio of non-treble voices and the lower passaggio 
    of treble voices (pitches can be adjusted as long as they span the area 
    between ca. A3 to G4) (Figure 14.1):

    Exploring the fR1:1fo Transition and 
    Tracking
    !e concept of allowing the sung pitch to ascend into and match the 
    /f_irst resonance of the vowel being sung, and therea/f_ter of raising the /f_irst 
    resonance to track the /f_irst harmonic with further pitch ascent to preserve 
    whoop timbre is simple enough. Once the singer reaches the pitch of the 
    /f_irst formant, from there on up the /f_irst resonance must be raised by vowel 
    opening in tandem with the rising pitch to maintain full resonance and for 
    bene/f_icial feedback on the vibrator. In practice, however, /f_inding the best 
    way to open a vowel is more challenging and nuanced and requires careful 
    Figure 14.8  Ascending and then descending stepwise through the 
    primary register shift.

    guidance by an expert teacher. As the jaw is lowered further for increased 
    openness of pronunciation and to raise fR1, what exactly do the lips and 
    tongue do? !ough lip spreading is usually avoided except for the extreme 
    high range, a deadpan facial expression is not necessary for freedom or 
    resonance; rather, there may there be some expressive li/f_t in the zygomatic 
    area (pleasantly li/f_ted cheeks). Does the tongue dorsum maintain contact 
    with the upper molars in back for front vowels, and if so, how high in 
    the range? How much lip rounding can be accommodated in the upper 
    voice for the normally lip-rounded vowels? How are these changes from 
    speech shapes best motivated? All of these questions have to do with the 
    issues surrounding active vowel modi/f_ication and are especially relevant 
    for treble voices who frequently sing above the /f_irst formant locations of 
    resonant speech vowels. From an acoustic perspective—combined with 
    the pedagogic opinion of the author—maximizing resonator convergence 
    for the vowel and pitch in question to ensure the best resonator feedback 
    and assistance for the vibrator, while extending intelligibility as high as 
    comfortably possible, are appropriate goals. In pursuing these goals, the 
    singer should be guided by naturalness and pleasantness of expression 
    and appearance along with comfort and resonance of production. On 
    any given pitch, vowels can retain some visual identity relative to other 
    vowels at that same pitch, regardless of degree of openness. !ere is no 
    compelling acoustic reason for strangeness of articulation. An unforced 
    call can be used as an approximate shape guide for the appearance of 
    upper voice diction. While the instinctively exaggerated divergence of 
    “called” diction may need modi/f_ication, some diﬀerentiation between 
    vowels is still apparent until quite high. Western classical timbre will need 
    a fronted, mid-mouth sense of palatal li/f_t (rather than yawny) closer to 
    attachment to the hard palate, maintenance of tongue dorsum height for 
    F2 tuning as far as vowel and pitch will allow, and a relaxed openness of 
    throat and settled larynx below that dorsum height.

    Concerning the latter recommendation, a singer’s default expression 
    should not be deadpan. In avoiding excessive lateral lip spreading, 
    it is not necessary to remove the subtle lip shaping that comes from 
    light facial expression. In other words, the lip muscles are relaxed 
    and not spread, but “go along for the ride” with the light expressive 
    activation of the cheeks. !is strategy assists in maintaining the 
    sensation of internal height of the tone (Figure 14.11). 
    z Finally, begin this exercise (Figure 14.12) with the [zi] appropriately 
    opened, open further as you ascend, and then close as you descend.

    to close into whoop timbre, and only subsequently opening it to track 
    1fo (while retaining other important articulatory aspects: a fronted, li/f_ted 
    so/f_t palate, inner smile, unconstricted throat, tongue dorsum height), 
    vowel adjustments can be made in any vocalize or in the literature 
    itself. Further attention will be needed to re/f_ine the precise manner of 
    opening for each vowel, naturalness of appearance, and to train fR1:1fo 
    tracking until it is instinctive. (See resources at kenbozeman  .c om on the 
    chiaroscuro whisper for training on how to shape for reliable resonance

    sensation shi/f_ts up and forward in order to avoid chin-tucking.
    The Use of Close [o] in Whoop 
    Timbre
    Entering and experiencing whoop timbre is fairly clear on a close [o] vowel 
    at C5 in treble voices, at which pitch an [~u]-like tone color component 
    will noticeably increase. !at warmer, darker tone color is best conceived 
    as higher, above the palate, and to the front. !e following exercise may 
    help. Use a rather close version of [o] as in a German close [o]:
    Sing an F major triad with the following text:
    F4    A4    C5    A4    F4
    So…….....you.. .kn ow….
    !e C5 should clearly shi/f_t into whoop timbre with an increase in [u] tone 
    color. Follow that with an [o] vowel melisma that tracks the same pitches, 
    sensations, and timbral migrations.
    !en sing this higher sequence:
    Ab4   C5      Eb5       C5      Ab4
    So…..you…would. .y ou…know
    !e C5 shi/f_ts into whoop with more [u] tone color, which is then tracked 
    by slight mouth opening to [ ʊ] to track whoop on the Eb5 “would, ” 
    retraces its migration, still in whoop timbre to [o] on C5, and /f_inally 
    passively migrates back to a close timbre [o] on Ab4. Follow that with an 
    PRAA.indb   135PRAA.indb   135 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  136
    [o] vowel melisma that tracks the same pitches, sensations, and timbral 
    migrations: [o u ʊ u o], mostly with passive migrations—only the Eb [ ʊ] 
    requires slight active (opening) modi/f_ication.
    Finally, sing this higher sequence:
    C5      E5      G5     E5    C5
    Y ou…would…………..too.
    !is sequence deliberately starts in whoop timbre on an [o]-shaped [u]-
    ish sound, and is subsequently tracked higher by subtle vowel opening for 
    the [ʊ] vowel of “would” as if howling. Follow that with an [o] melisma 
    that tracks the same pitches, sensations, and migrations: [o:u-ʊ-ʊ-ʊ-o:u]. 
    Motivate all three of these migration explorations with a warm, reassuring, 
    aﬀect.
