    Practical Vocal Acoustics
    15
    Corollary Strategies
    !is text is focused on practical, applied, acoustic vocal pedagogy. !ough 
    the principles presented here are vital, acoustic pedagogy will not function 
    well in a pedagogic vacuum, being but one aspect of the whole equation. 
    It is prudent for us to remember not to take part of the truth and make 
    it the whole truth. All of the things that voice teachers routinely do to 
    establish balanced poise of the body, an e#cient, responsive breath-to-
    voice connection, &ow phonation, a freely &owing vibrancy, continuity 
    and suppleness of line, and so on, continue to be necessary. Acoustic 
    pedagogy is a valuable partner in this enterprise and can facilitate vocal 
    ease through better interactive coupling of resonation with phonation. A 
    few corollary strategies worth noting in this regard are:
    Dynamic Control
    Avoid inevitable overt crescendi with pitch ascent. If an increase in 
    loudness is married to pitch ascent, then probably a corollary excessive 
    increase in pressure is as well, and some resultant movement in the 
    direction of pressed phonation. !e student should be able to maintain 
    the same dynamic level during ascending passages. !is will be easier 
    when coupled with acoustic pedagogy since a crescendo on ascent is part 
    of the yell instinct, which is primarily about making very loud sounds and 
    invites frontal over-articulation of the brighter vowel tone color. Avoiding 
    opening the vowel and/or raising the larynx at the fR1: 2fo or the fR1: 1fo 
    junctures. Instead, allowing the voice to migrate, turn over, or enter whoop 
    timbre will enable ascent without overt crescendo. Also, motivating the 
    PRAA.indb   137PRAA.indb   137 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  138
    Corollary Strategies
    ascent with some meaningful in&ection that does not require a crescendo 
    is a productive corollary strategy (see !e Use of Aﬀect below).
    Pressure Reduction
    A related strategy to counter the tendency to raise breath pressure upon 
    ascent is to use active inhalatory checking of the exhalatory forces of the 
    respiratory system during phonation, speci/f_ically by attempting more 
    expansion of the lower chest/middle of the torso to avoid increasing 
    compression of the chest. !is is done with a feeling of strong yet supple, 
    buoyant, resilient fullness in the breath supply. It is especially helpful to 
    strive for a sense of opening in the chest simultaneous with the perception 
    of a narrowing, collecting concentration of the acoustic sensation of 
    the tone when entering and traversing the passaggio. !is can best be 
    facilitated further by carefully chosen aﬀective responses, such as listening 
    with intent concern to something in the distance, or like a child’s concern 
    about what’s under the bed, both of which evoke an inhalatory, pressure-
    reducing response from the chest wall.
    Vibrancy and Flow
    Maintaining vibrancy on all pitches, so that there are no intermittent 
    straight tones in the exercises nor losses of vibrancy at the seams between 
    vowels and/or pitches, also helps avoid pressed phonation and the pressure 
    increase that accompanies it. Movement, either of the entire body or with 
    arm gestures to model the &ow and shape of the phrase, is a familiar 
    strategy to avoid holding or locking up in the respiratory/phonatory 
    connection/coordination.
    The Use of A/uniFB00ect
    Voice is motivated by expression. !is is apparently inherent and primal. 
    !e /f_irst thing a newborn does is to inhale. !e very next thing most 
    PRAA.indb   138PRAA.indb   138 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Corollary Strategies 139
    newborns do is to phonate, communicating quite expressively how they 
    feel about what just happened. Very few spontaneous phonations in 
    life are not caused by the impulse to communicate (exceptions include 
    sounds accompanying sneezing, coughing, throat clearing, hiccups). Not 
    only emotional quality, but also tessitura and pitch change are motivated 
    by expressive in&ection. We change pitch in&ection without planning, 
    merely by the expressive intent that is motivating our speaking. In music, 
    melodic notation is really a kind of choreography for expressive in&ection. 
    !e task of the singer is to intuit the expressive motivation that would 
    automatically result in the given melodic contour. Aﬀect can be very 
    useful in inspiring in&ective loops across the pitch of turning. In general, 
    deepening, internalizing aﬀects are most useful for ascending in&ections 
    as well as for upward melodic leaps. For example, speak the phrase, “Oh, 
    I get it, ” with an in&ective loop up and back down on the “oh, ” agreeably 
    expressing the deep realization of an epiphany. Do this while thinking 
    a close [o] and an internalized (in, above, and below) direction into the 
    body, as if “drinking the tone. ” Explore allowing aﬀect to motivate subtle, 
    spontaneous, simultaneous bodily/gestural/facial expressional responses. 
    Since expression motivates both voice and gesture, such uninhibited, 
    playful exploration can help facilitate improved acoustic and laryngeal 
    registration and su#ciently suppress the yell instinct—during the upward 
    pitch in&ections. Aﬀects that encourage greater CT involvement (i.e., a 
    lighter, “headier” laryngeal registration) as well as a favorable convergent 
    poise of the resonator also can assist upper range excursions. Examples 
    of such aﬀects are sweetness, love, reassurance, compassion, sobbiness, 
    self-pity, mischief, and self-satisfaction, most of which are in some 
    sense internalizing aﬀects. Examples of aﬀects that tend to increase TA 
    involvement are authority, con/f_idence, anger, hatred, machismo, and 
    projected egotism, that is, aﬀects that are more externally focused. For 
    a robust, yet lyric laryngeal registration, enthusiasm, joy, and hilarity are 
    motivationally helpful.
    The Problem of Micromanagement
    Like most physical coordinations, singing is not an activity that responds 
    well to micromanagement. Timbral transitions that should occur purely 
    PRAA.indb   139PRAA.indb   139 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  140
    as the result of pitch change—and the resultant changing relationships 
    between harmonics and the /f_irst resonance—ideally should not be 
    manipulated or micromanaged. !ey should feel to the singer more like 
    acoustic phenomena than like muscular phenomena. While speci/f_ic 
    awareness and attention to turning over may initially be needed to 
    overcome the yell instinct and habit, the more generalized and holistic the 
    eventual motivation, and the subtler the eﬀects of turning, the better. It is 
    o/f_ten preferable to stimulate appropriate vowel turning indirectly as a by-
    product of a diﬀerent directive or motivation, such as an increase of the 
    complementary tone color output target, aﬀect, or a directional gesture. 
    Furthermore, once a voice becomes accustomed through repetition 
    and habituation to allowing vowels to close at their predicted locations, 
    turning over need neither be volitional nor self-conscious.

    Laryngeal Registration 
    Revisited
    !is text is deliberately focused on voice acoustics, with minimal 
    reference to other crucial biomechanical aspects of voice pedagogy 
    (anatomy, breathing, etc.), all of which are important in their own right 
    and interact signi/f_icantly with the acoustics of production. However, 
    laryngeal registration is so directly involved in and interactive with 
    voice source acoustics, it will receive some brief attention here. From an 
    acoustic point of view, what is needed from the voice source is a clean, 
    relatively clear excitation of the vocal tract air column with a spectral slope 
    appropriate to the range and dynamic level required for the situation. !e 
    singer must also be capable of traversing the range without perceptibly 
    abrupt changes in the source spectral slope, even when crossing from 
    one vibrational mechanism to another, such as from “chest” to “head. ” 
    Acoustic interaction and feedback are o/f_ten involved in smoothing these 
    transitions, but we should /f_irst consider the necessary muscular valve 
    adjustments themselves.
    Muscle Action
    A prominent theory postulates that the two primary, mutually antagonistic 
    muscular functions of the larynx that shape and tauten the vocal folds—
    the shortening, thickening TA ’s and the lengthening, thinning CT’s—
    cooperate in an overlapping middle range to achieve a smooth “baton 
    exchange” by very gradually shi/f_ting the proportions of their involvement, 
    PRAA.indb   141PRAA.indb   141 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  142
    Laryngeal Registration Revisited
    all while the muscles that approximate the vocal folds typically maintain 
    competent, comfortable closure. !ough perceptually smooth register 
    transitions are heard in performances of excellent singers, a perfectly 
    smooth transfer of muscle activity, in which one group tapers involvement 
    gradually down to zero, has rarely been measured. !ere is almost always 
    a point at which a binary toggle from greater to thinner vocal fold 
    contact is measurable, even when accomplished essentially inaudibly. 
    !ere is probably an aerodynamic or acoustic factor at play that triggers 
    this observable change. Nevertheless, it is both an important goal and a 
    fruitful strategy to practice absolute perceptual smoothness of laryngeal 
    registration across range.
    Downstream Resistors and  
    Semi-Occluded Vocal Tracts
    One eﬀective approach to smoothing range negotiation is to glissando 
    across the entire range using some helpful downstream resistor or semi-
    occluded vocal tract articulation. A downstream resistor is any articulator 
    position or function that requires or resists air&ow and is downstream 
    from the vocal folds, that is, nearer to or at the lips. If the resistor is a 
    vibrator, like a tongue or lip trill, it will require steady air&ow and pressure 
    to maintain oscillation. !e vocal folds must allow enough &ow through 
    the glottis to be in balance with the downstream resistor, whose semi-
    occlusion generates a back pressure that bene/f_icially reduces the upstream 
    transglottal pressure diﬀerence. A semi-occluded articulation at or near 
    the lips or at the dorsum/palatal narrowing can increase inertive reactance, 
    lowering the phonation threshold pressure (the amount of pressure needed 
    to bring the vocal folds into vibration). Either strategy reduces pressed 
    phonation. Downstream resistors or semi-occluded articulations that can 
    be used in glissandi for smoothing laryngeal register shi/f_ts include:
37) Tongue trills
38) Lip trills/raspberries/blubbering
39) Phonating through a short straw
40) A buzzy hum or buzzing through a lightly bite-held lower lip 
    (modi/f_ied [v])
    PRAA.indb   142PRAA.indb   142 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Laryngeal Registration Revisited 143
41) “Kazooing” through a tiny [w] or fricative [β] (“puﬀy cheeks”)
42) Open-mouthed [ŋ] or other nasal consonants such as [n m]
    Motivating Laryngeal Stability
    With any of these glissandi, care should be taken to facilitate a &exible, 
    comfortable stability in which the larynx does not rise appreciably when 
    approaching the primary register transition zone (ca. B3-G4). !is can 
    be helped by motivating the phonation in that transitional range with 
    expressive in&ection or with aﬀects that stabilize the throat shape and 
    laryngeal depth, such as:
43) Self-pitying whimper
44) “Up to no good but pleased with yourself ” mischief
45) Suppressed laughter
46) Sobbiness
47) Hilarity
    !ese aﬀects can apparently be done rather strongly without the risk of 
    injury or even of muscle rigidity. As with yawning, these aﬀective actions 
    are physically self-limiting, with muscles working in supple antagonism to 
    inherent tissue elasticity. !ough the student may be instinctively inclined 
    to relax aﬀective positioning when opening for the upper range, resonance 
    and vocal tract structural poise are better served by strengthening the 
    aﬀect with ascent as occurs “in the wild. ” !e expressional migration of 
    aﬀect motivates pitch change.
    Laryngeal Registration Goals
    !e goals of laryngeal registration are a clean, clear, light voice source 
    “buzz” that migrates from buzzier to smoother throughout the entire 
    compass, with no pressure surges, minimal pressure increase, and with a 
    stable larynx and throat space. Maintaining throat poise when ascending 
    through the zona di passaggio  may need to be more deliberate . Since 
    semi-occluded exercises are not executed on open-mouthed vowels, they 
    PRAA.indb   143PRAA.indb   143 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  144
    moderate some of the acoustic eﬀects that might otherwise destabilize 
    register transitions. !is allows the focus of attention to be exclusively on 
    the smoothness and ease of a voice source that is acoustically organized 
    across the range.
    Glissandoing on a vowel poses more challenges but would be the next 
    step a/f_ter downstream resistors. It is usually easier to glissando on naturally 
    convergent vowels, such as [i], provided the mouth is opened gradually 
    and su#ciently to formant track 1 fo a/f_ter arrival in whoop timbre for 
    pitches above fR1. All vowels will necessarily migrate in timbre across range 
    with an increase in the vowel’s complementary tone color relative to its 
    identifying target tone color. !e complementary tone color for most 
    vowels is the lower, /f_irst formant contribution, which adds a warming, 
    rounding, and possibly neutralizing element. Language targets, originating 
    at speech range, cannot be aggressively maintained without risking higher 
    pressure and glottal resistance levels. Nonetheless, intelligible diction is 
    possible for most voices surprisingly high. Pitches sung above the treble 
    clef will have reduced vowel de/f_inition options.
    The Use of Nasalance
    !e use of a subtle amount of nasalance can assist with training laryngeal 
    register transitions across range, at least in SOVT vocal explorations. 
    Nasalance broadens and weakens the intensity of the /f_irst resonance and 
    the percentage of its resultant warming, rounding, /f_irst formant tone 
    color contribution (Sundberg et al. 2016). !is has two eﬀects: a weaker 
    /f_irst resonance reduces its potential to acoustically destabilize phonation 
    at laryngeal register shi/f_ts; its weakening also creates the impression 
    of strengthening the timbral singer’s formant cluster contribution, 
    brightening the overall timbral mix percept. !ough smooth laryngeal 
    registration can be successfully negotiated and trained without the use 
    of nasalance, judicious use of it in early training can be a very bene/f_icial 
    exploratory strategy. Its persistence in the /f_inished product of unampli/f_ied 
    singing is controverted as potentially compromising overall power (Perna 
    2020) and risking an undesirable percept of actual nasality.

          Practical Vocal Acoustics

          Instructional Technology
          As mentioned in the introduction, instructional technology is now 
          readily available, inexpensive, and rather user-friendly. Each application 
          or program does take some training to understand its most appropriate 
          uses. Other texts have written more extensively on this, but this chapter 
          will give a summary of two instruments and their use.
          The Madde Voice Synthesizer
          !roughout this text, explorations using the Madde [m ɑdːə] voice 
          synthesizer have been recommended to illustrate both aurally and visually 
          various aspects of vocal acoustics. !is author considers Madde to be a 
          most helpful tool for vocal acoustics explanations ( Figure 17.1 ). It is a 
          free shareware product from Swedish engineer Svante Granqvist and is 
          programmable to represent any voice type on any vowel singing any pitch 
          within a standard keyboard. Madde clearly displays both harmonics  (with 
          numbered lines) and formants (with shaded bands) above a keyboard, 
          an especially musician-friendly feature. !is format provides the 
          simplest, most direct way to model and explain to students the eﬀects of 
          harmonic:resonance interactions so crucial to acoustic vocal registration. 
          Madde can also be useful for occasional explanations in the studio and 
          even for audio demonstrations of harmonic:resonance interactions and 
          the passive vowel migrations that accompany them, but it is especially 
          well-suited for voice pedagogy classes. Madde can be used to illustrate 
          (1) the eﬀect of the sung pitch on the number of harmonics available for 
          resonation per primary audible range, (2) the roll-oﬀ (tilt) in power of the 
          PRAA.indb   145PRAA.indb   145 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
          Practical Vocal Acoustics  146
          Instructional Technology
          source harmonics, (3) the eﬀect of the presence or absence of resonances/
          formants, and (4) the aural eﬀects of all harmonic:resonance interactions.
          Limitations of Madde
          Madde and Modes of Phonation
          Madde can roughly model diﬀerent vibrational mechanisms (head or 
          chest) by varying the tilt or “roll-oﬀ ” of the voice source (see Appendix 1: 
          Madde Exploration 2) and, to some extent, modes of phonation (pressed, 
          breathy, &ow). However, since the voice source input of harmonics in 
          Madde is always “clean” or noise-free, it cannot accurately model breathy 
          phonation, which typically has an air-noise element. Since no muscles are 
          involved in its voice source, neither can it really model all of the timbral 
          changes that accompany pressed phonation.
          Madde and Non-Linearity
          Madde is strictly linear (non-interactive) and therefore cannot model 
          the eﬀects of non-linearity on the voice source or on resonation. In other 
          Figure 17 .1 Madde voice synthesizer, displaying voice source harmonics 
          (numbered lines) and formants (shaded bands) just above the keyboard.
          PRAA.indb   146PRAA.indb   146 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
          Instructional Technology 147
          words, the resonances/formants one programs do not cause changes in 
          the source input—as can happen in a human voice—but merely selectively 
          resonate its harmonic spectral content.
          Madde and Vibrato
          Since Madde’s programmed vibrato cycles—though adjustable in rate 
          and extent—are essentially uniform, audible pitch undulations are more 
          apparent and distracting than in a real voice with an ideal vibrato. With 
          actual voices, minute waveform variability can help to create a perceptual 
          “singleness of pitch, ” if rate and extent parameters are within certain limits. 
          !at is to say, a tone can be consistently vibrant (have measurable, regular 
          frequency undulations) without perceivable pitch undulation. Bear in mind 
          that pitch is not the same as frequency. It is the term for our perception of 
          frequency. In a real voice, if the vibrato frequency is close to six per second 
          and the extent (excursion above and below the target frequency) is a semi-
          tone or less, while we perceive that something is moving, the pitch sounds 
          stable and steady rather than wobbly or tremulous. One student described 
          his perception upon hearing an ideal vibrato as “movement within the pitch 
          rather than movement of the pitch. ” !is is analogous to our perceiving a 
          smoothly moving picture when, in fact, twenty-four still frames per second 
          are &ashed on the screen in the theater. Perceptual and actual phenomena 
          diverge. Madde does not replicate this “singleness of pitch” phenomenon.
          In spite of these few limitations, because of its musician-friendly keyboard 
          display and general excellence of design, Svante Granqvist’s Madde voice 
          synthesizer is an outstanding instructional tool—one of the best available to 
          date—for acoustics presentations/explanations for undergraduate voice 
          pedagogy classes or for any audience. It is recommended that you download 
          and use Madde for the explorations described in this text for the best 
          understanding of the principles and sounds being discussed. Madde can be 
          downloaded to PCs or to Macs that can handle Windows applications from 
          the following site:
          http://www .tolvan .com1
          Spectrography
          !e instructional technology that is the simplest to read and so far the 
          most useful for the voice studio is real-time spectrography . Real-time 
          PRAA.indb   147PRAA.indb   147 7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM7/10/2025   4:38:58 PM
          Practical Vocal Acoustics  148
          means that the analysis is simultaneous and the image display immediate—
          essentially as the sound is happening, that is, in real-time. Spectrography is 
          an analysis of sound that isolates the frequency components of the sound 
          and displays them on the vertical dimension, with higher frequencies 
          lying higher across the graph, and lower frequencies lying lower. Time 
          moves horizontally across the graph, with the present instant entering on 
          the right edge of the screen and then scrolling to the le/f_t. Saved images are 
          then read from le/f_t (earliest sound) to right (most recent sound). A third 
          dimension—the intensity or power of the frequency components—is 
          displayed on a color or darkness scale. Of all the ways we analyze and graph 
          sound, so far, the spectrographic image is visually the most analogous to 
          sound over time, and therefore the most approachable, understandable, 
          and even intuitive for students. With minimal initial explanation, students 
          can both see and hear important characteristics of their singing in the 
          playback, leading to productive commentary or discussion. !is author 
          experimented with real-time spectrography starting in 1989 and had the 
          privilege of using spectrography in his voice studio from 1993 until 2019. 
          It was as ever-present as the mirror on the wall and was referred to at a 
          comparable rate—that is, only as needed and when helpful.
          VoceVista
          Real-time spectrography is readily available from various sources in 
          various forms, including the deservedly popular VoceVi sta Video Pro  
          program, developed by Donald Miller, Harm Schutte, and their research 
          team in Groningen, Netherlands, and managed and updated by engineer 
          Bodo Maas. (Figure 17.2). If the program’s analysis parameters are set to 
          display a narrow frequency bandwidth (see De/f_initions), which is the 
          default setting for Voce Vista, individual harmonics of the fundamental 
          frequency are modeled and easily seen &owing horizontally across 
          the screen, and therefore characteristics of vibrato and vocal line can 
          be monitored and evaluated. While broad bandwidth  settings more 
          accurately model the individual impulses of the voice and show vocal 
          tract formants even more clearly, formants are usually reasonably 
          apparent with the narrow width default settings, especially for low and 
          mid-range pitches, such that overall resonance and resonance balance  
          can also be monitored with them. Durational parameters can be set to 
          PRAA.indb   148PRAA.indb   148 7/10/2025   4:38:59 PM7/10/2025   4:38:59 PM
          Instructional Technology 149
          save short segments of time for near immediate retrieval, listening, and 
          viewing. Continuity and consistency of vibrancy, brevity of consonantal 
          interruption, and consistency of resonance balance, among other things, 
          can be readily seen and heard on playback.
          Power Spectra
          If more detailed display or research is needed, a power spectrum  
          display, and even electroglottography  (EGG) can be used (both 
          available with VoceVista). Power spectra display power or intensity 
          on the vertical and frequency (spectrum) on the horizontal. Unlike 
          spectrography, which graphs segments of time on the horizontal, power 
          spectra usually only graph an instant (either the present instant or an 
          instant selected from a saved spectrograph) of time. Power spectra can 
          therefore show formants, harmonics, and their relative strengths in 
          great detail ( Figure 17.3 ).
          Figure 17 .2 VoceVista real-time spectrography, with frequency on the 
          vertical, time on the horizontal, intensity on the color scale, and the 
          pressure waveform displayed above.
          PRAA.indb   149PRAA.indb   149 7/10/2025   4:38:59 PM7/10/2025   4:38:59 PM
          Practical Vocal Acoustics  150
          Electroglottography (EGG)
          Electroglottography is a charting of the amount of glottal contact in 
          each vibratory cycle, from which a closed quotient (percentage of time 
          the glottis is closed), contact quotient (amount of glottal contact), and 
          laryngeal register can be postulated. Data is collected by means of a small 
          electric current passing between two electrodes placed one on either side 
          of the larynx. !e greater the conductivity between them, the greater the 
          vocal fold contact. Chest voice has greater vocal fold contact and a larger 
          contact quotient than head voice. !erefore, one can—roughly at least—
          estimate vibrational mechanism from the contact quotient.
          !ough EGG signals can give very speci/f_ic information about laryngeal 
          registration and its coordination with acoustic factors, both of which are 
          essential in ongoing research, it is not yet clear that what electroglottography 
          adds is su#ciently crucial to application to be necessary for regular weekly 
          lesson use. For more information on EGG (as well as for any matters of 
          vocal resonance), this author highly recommends Donald Miller’s 
          Figure 17 .3 VoceVista with waveform (top left), spectrograph (bottom 
          left), and power spectrum (right) displayed. The vertical time cursor on 
          the left selects the moment being displayed on the power spectrum. The 
          short horizontal frequency cursor on the spectrograph (the third harmonic 
          from the bottom) becomes the vertical cursor on the power spectrum.

          Resonance in Singing.  (See Selected References). Suggested uses of 
          VoceVista in the studio will be addressed below.
          Studio Uses of Voce Vista
          VoceVista is a powerful, well-designed tool for voice analysis and research. 
          As argued earlier, real-time spectrography—which is available from various 
          sources including VoceVista—is currently the most intuitively analogous way 
          to graph a number of pedagogically relevant sound characteristics visually. 
          With frequency on the vertical, time on the horizontal, and intensity on 
          the color or brightness scale, its display is understandable and readable by 
          students a/f_ter minimal training. Very practical matters of vocal line are readily 
          apparent: continuousness and consistency of vibrancy, dynamic shaping, 
          duration of any interruption of the tonal &ow, and at least in a general sense, 
          timbral balance. !is author therefore has real-time spectrography running 
          essentially continuously in lessons as the standard technology for playback. 
          If a more detailed look at the relative strength of harmonics is needed, 
          a screen display that includes power spectrum can be selected. A power 
          spectrum typically displays one instant of time, with power (intensity) on the 
          vertical and frequency (spectrum) on the horizontal. VoceVista very handily 
          coordinates the spectrograph display with the power spectrum display so 
          that one can scroll through the time dimension of the spectrograph and 
          examine each instant in detail on the power spectrum.
          Spectrographic display is best for assessing:
48) Vibrancy (immediacy, consistency, continuity)
49) Vocal line (tonal and general timbral continuity)
50) General sense of overall resonance
51) General presence and strength of the singer’s formant cluster
    !ings that spectrography is not best suited for include:
52) Close comparisons between fairly similar vowels (power spectra 
    are better).
53) Subtle timbral distinctions.
54) Determining which sounds are correct (your trained ear decides. 
    !e picture may or may not show evidence, depending upon the 
    nature and degree of diﬀerence).
    PRAA.indb   151PRAA.indb   151 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Practical Vocal Acoustics  152
55) Best laryngeal height (your ear, eye, and the student’s freedom of 
    function are better).
56) Precise resonance:harmonic interactions (power spectra and your 
    ear are better).
    !at said, the audio playback of VoceVista that accompanies the visual 
    display is good enough to “complete the picture” of most issues being 
    discussed. Ultimately, it is the sound—and accompanying freedom and 
    comfort of sensation—that determine the success of the singing being 
    evaluated. Spectrography doesn’t tell you what is good, bad, or indiﬀerent. 
    It simply displays what is. !e teacher and student look, listen and discuss 
    to determine what the sounds and the graph are re&ecting.

    Deﬁnitions
    Absolute spectral tone color: the characteristic of individual frequencies 
    to be perceived by humans as having a speci/f_ic vowel-like quality, relative 
    height, and relative darkness or brightness. For example, low-frequency 
    harmonics have a dark [~u]-like quality; high frequencies have a bright 
    [~i]-like quality. Vowels are perceived to be a combination of the absolute 
    spectral tone colors of the harmonics being reinforced by the /f_irst and 
    second resonances of the vocal tract and featured as the /f_irst and second 
    formants in the radiated sound. !is approximation of a vowel quality is 
    indicated by a tilde plus the vowel. (Howell 2016)
    Acoustic coupling: coupling of a vocal tract resonance with a source 
    harmonic.
    Acoustic registration: the timbral transitions that occur as a result of 
    resonance:harmonic interactions. All acoustic register events involve 
    harmonic interactions with the /f_irst resonance. Many also involve 
    interaction with the second resonance.
    Acoustic registers: vocal registers due exclusively to relationships between 
    acoustic parameters, speci/f_ically the harmonics of the fundamental 
    frequency relative to the /f_irst resonance of the vocal tract. See: open timbre 
    (voce aperta), close timbre (voce chiusa), and whoop timbre.
    Active vowel modi/f_ication: a change in the vowel quality accomplished 
    by moving the vowel formants from their normal speech locations by 
    means of changes in the shape of the vocal tract in an attempt to /f_ind 
    better (more resonant) resonance:harmonic matches.
    Aﬀect: emotive expression, important for the spontaneous motivation 
    and organization of voice and the maintenance of the pharyngeal poise 
    necessary for vocal tract stability across range and vowels.
    PRAA.indb   153PRAA.indb   153 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 154
    Antinode: see pressure antinode.
    Articulation: in speech and singing, the shaping or tuning of the vocal 
    tract to modify the /f_iltered voice source signal; articulation also includes 
    how the tongue, lips, jaw, and other speech organs are involved in the 
    production of speech sounds.
    Aryepiglottic exit:  the mouth of the epilarynx, formed by the tissues 
    connecting the epiglottis with the arytenoids. A narrowing of the 
    aryepiglottic exit is associated with a boost in upper partials from the 
    voice source. Along with other perturbations further downstream along 
    the vocal tract, it is part of the resonance strategy of the singer’s formant 
    cluster in unampli/f_ied Western classical singing and possibly also of the 
    ring or twang of belt production.
    Auditory roughness:  the psychoacoustic percept of buzziness due to 
    closely clustered (usually harmonic) frequencies, that is, frequencies a 
    minor third or less apart (harmonics ( 6fo). Auditory roughness migrates 
    from rough low to smooth high across range, register, and voice type, 
    and is associated with laryngeal registers: rough with “chest voice, ” an 
    intermediate sizzle with “mix, ” and smooth with “head voice. ”
    Bandwidth: a tube resonance has a certain frequency range contour; that 
    is, it will reinforce frequencies on either side of its peak frequency response, 
    covering a range of frequencies. If the contour of the resonance is a tall, 
    narrow peak, it has a narrow bandwidth with higher potential intensity. 
    If its contour is rounder, it has a broad bandwidth  with less potential 
    intensity. Brass instrument resonances have very narrow bandwidths and 
    will therefore only pass speci/f_ic harmonics of a given tube through but 
    with higher intensity. Vocal tract resonances and formants typically have 
    wider bandwidths, allowing more voice source harmonics through on 
    either side of the peak formant frequency.
    Belting: a singing style with a mechanism one dominant laryngeal 
    registration, prioritizing the extension of a bright, speech-like timbre 
    higher, using an acoustic strategy in which the /f_irst resonance tracks the 
    second harmonic; in other words, a kind of skillful yelling or calling. 
    Some form of belting is prevalent in many forms of popular, folk, and 
    world music and in American musical theater.
    PRAA.indb   154PRAA.indb   154 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 155
    Belt mix: a delayed (higher) version of close timbre, in which the second 
    harmonic of the voice source lies between the /f_irst and second resonances, 
    and the second resonance highlights a higher source harmonic, enabling 
    the singer to extend a belt-like, bright timbre into the top of the treble clef 
    and above.
    Breathy phonation: (see Mode of phonation)
    Chesty: (see Laryngeal register)
    Chiaroscuro: an Italian term meaning “bright-dark” or “clear-dark” 
    borrowed from art history to describe an ideal resonance balance between 
    low and high-frequency components in Western classical singing. It is 
    usually accomplished (in middle and lower ranges) by some balance in 
    power between the /f_irst formant and the singer’s formant cluster. Also 
    termed chiaroscuro balanced resonance.
    Closed Quotient: the average percentage of time the glottis is closed 
    during one vibratory cycle. A large closed quotient would usually indicate 
    a chestier laryngeal register with a short, thick vocal fold shape (vibrational 
    mechanism one). A smaller closed quotient would usually indicate a 
    headier laryngeal register with a long, thin vocal fold shape (vibrational 
    mechanism two). (See Contact quotient)
    Close: as a verb, to close; an acoustic concentration of the sound that occurs 
    when a source harmonic, particularly the second harmonic, surpasses the 
    /f_irst vocal tract resonance. Also called covering or turning over.
    Close timbre: a vocal quality and acoustic register in which the second 
    harmonic has risen above the /f_irst resonance peak or /f_irst formant, causing 
    the voice to seem to “turn over. ” Also termed voce chiusa in Italian. Belt 
    “mix” is a variant of close timbre.
    Close vowel: a vowel with a low /f_irst formant, such as [i] and [u], and an 
    inherently convergent resonator shape.
    Closed phase: the portion of a periodic glottal cycle during which the 
    glottis is closed and air&ow is zero.
    PRAA.indb   155PRAA.indb   155 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 156
    Contact quotient: the degree of vocal fold tissue in contact in a given 
    vibratory cycle. A large contact quotient would usually indicate a chestier 
    laryngeal register with a short, thick shape (vibrational mechanism one). 
    A smaller contact quotient would usually indicate a headier laryngeal 
    register with a long, thin shape (vibrational mechanism two). !ough 
    related to closed quotient, it is possible to have a high contact quotient 
    without complete glottal closure, or a high closed quotient (duration 
    of closure) without deep contact, but it is more typical to have a high 
    closed quotient when there is a high contact quotient. EGG displays 
    contact quotient reliably, from which closed quotient may be less reliably 
    inferred.
    Contemporary Commercial Music (CCM): popular music genres other 
    than Western classical.
    Convergent resonator: a vocal tract shape, which for the vowel being 
    produced is relatively more open near the glottis and narrower near the 
    lips (like an inverted megaphone). Such a resonance strategy is typical of 
    unampli/f_ied Western classical singing and voce chiusa.
    Coup de glotte: a clean, gentle glottal onset, in which the vocal folds close 
    immediately prior to phonation; the most e#cient, expressive way to 
    onset phonation; advocated by Manuel Garcia.
    Cover: the timbral shi/f_t corollary to the ascending fR1:2fo crossing, also 
    termed “turning over” or “closing, ” and causing a more internal and less 
    “exposed” timbral and somatosensory percept.
    Cricothyroid muscles (CT): (see Laryngeal register)
    Declarative knowledge: the relatively objective, measurable, quantitative 
    knowledge of physical realities, such as vocal physiology and acoustics. 
    Such knowledge is available to scienti/f_ic investigation and clear, 
    straightforward articulation. (see Procedural knowledge.)
    Depth: a description of the timbre of a sound that has signi/f_icant strength 
    in one of its lowest harmonics, as resonated by the /f_irst resonance and 
    featured as the /f_irst formant.
    PRAA.indb   156PRAA.indb   156 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 157
    Divergent resonator: a description of a resonator shape that is smaller in 
    the pharynx and more open nearer the lips. Open vowels are inherently 
    divergent in shape. !is resonance strategy is more typical of musical 
    theater as well as many world music singing styles.
    Downstream resistor: any articulator position or device that resists 
    air&ow to some extent that is downstream from the vocal folds, that is, 
    nearer to or at the lips. Downstream resistors share the pressure load from 
    the lungs with the vocal folds, necessitating higher air&ow through the 
    glottis, reducing the transglottal pressure diﬀerence, thus discouraging 
    pressed phonation. (See also Semi-occluded vocal tract)
    Electroglottography (EGG): a charting of the amount of glottal contact 
    in each vibratory cycle, from which a closed quotient (the percentage 
    of time the glottis is closed) and laryngeal register can be postulated. 
    Data is collected by means of a small electric current passing between 
    two electrodes placed one on either side of the larynx. !e greater the 
    conductivity between them, the greater the vocal fold contact. Chest 
    voice (mechanism one) has greater vocal fold contact and a larger contact 
    quotient than head voice (mechanism two).
    Epilarynx, epilaryngeal tube: the short tube formed by the epiglottis 
    and the aryepiglottic folds and arytenoids just above the vocal 
    folds. !e epilarynx forms a small tube within the larger tube of the 
    laryngopharynx. Narrowing of the aryepiglottic opening (the exit of the 
    epilarynx) increases acoustic reactance and boosts higher frequencies. It 
    is surrounded by the piriform sinus spaces and valleculae, both of which 
    create anti-resonances.
    Fach: a German term for speci/f_ic voice categories, such as soubrette 
    soprano, Heldentenor, Verdi baritone, and so on, which are determined 
    by the tessitura capacity, vocal “weight, ” and timbre of a voice. !e overall 
    location of vocal tract formants, as well as the speci/f_ic location of the 
    singer’s formant cluster are signi/f_icant factors in determining quality and 
    therefore the Fach of a singer.
    Filter function: the characteristic of the vocal tract sound transfer function 
    to selectively prefer and pass some frequencies through over others, and 
    PRAA.indb   157PRAA.indb   157 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 158
    of the periodic vocal fold oscillation to reinforce those frequencies in sync 
    with its periodicity.
    First formant: the lowest radiated spectral peak of a voice, typically C4 
    or above. Most /f_irst formants lie in contact with the treble clef and cover 
    about one octave from the lowest of an [i] to the highest of an [a].
    Flow phonation: (see Mode of phonation)
    Formant: a frequency peak in the radiated spectrum, caused by a natural 
    resonance of the vocal tract, that is, by a standing wave of the quarter-
    wave resonator.
    Vowel formants: the /f_irst (lowest) two formants are the most easily tunable 
    and also span the range of all relevant vowel-like tone colors; therefore, 
    they are the most signi/f_icant in de/f_ining the vowel quality of a sound. 
    Together they are called the vowel formants.
    Singer’s formant cluster (SFC): a clustering of formants three and above 
    (usually formants 3–5) generated by a low larynx, open laryngopharynx, 
    narrowed epilaryngeal tube exit, and various additional perturbations 
    along the vocal tract length, that results in an increase in power in the 
    high-frequency components of a sung sound. It is especially characteristic 
    of non-treble voices in Western classical singing, enabling them to carry 
    over orchestral accompaniment.
    Formant detuning: the tuning of a resonance frequency peak away from 
    a harmonic to weaken its eﬀect. Used with F2 in the upper range of treble 
    voices to reduce shrillness and with F1 in non-treble voices and some 
    CCM aesthetics, such as belting, to avoid whoop timbre on close vowels.
    Formant tracking: the tuning of a resonance to follow or track a speci/f_ic 
    harmonic, such as fR1:1fo tracking of whoop timbre (upper treble voice 
    strategy) or fR1:2fo tracking of the yell or belting.
    Formant tuning: the tuning of one or both of the /f_irst two resonances in 
    order to /f_ind a better resonance:harmonic match and formant radiation 
    for more e#cient output.
    PRAA.indb   158PRAA.indb   158 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 159
    Front-back vowel designation: a description either of a physical 
    articulatory characteristic (a fronted tongue dorsum-palatal-narrowing 
    versus a tongue hump further back), or of an acoustic characteristic (a 
    sound with a high second formant and therefore stronger high partials, 
    i.e., a front vowel; or a low second formant and therefore weaker high 
    partials and potentially stronger low partials, i.e., a back vowel).
    Fullness: a typical descriptor of a sound with signi/f_icant strength in its 
    lowest harmonics, as featured by the /f_irst resonance as the /f_irst formant. 
    Other similar terms are: depth, warmth, roundness.
    Fundamental frequency (1fo): the lowest harmonic of a sound (previously 
    H1), more or less equivalent to the perceived pitch.
    Harmonic: a frequency component of a periodic sound. Harmonic 
    frequency components are either the lowest component (the fundamental 
    frequency) or whole integer multiples of the lowest frequency component. 
    Together, they generate a pressure waveform that repeats at the frequency 
    of that lowest common denominator.
    Harmonic to noise ratio (HNR): the percentage of periodic to aperiodic 
    spectral content in a sound. A “pure” pitch percept arises from sounds 
    that have a high HNR, that is, those that contain only or mostly harmonic 
    frequencies. !e lower the HNR, the noisier the sound percept.
    Headier: a tone with a steeper spectral slope and smoother timbral 
    percept; therefore, associated with “head voice” or mechanism two. (See 
    laryngeal register)
    Heavy Cover: deliberate lowering of fR1 (by tube lengthening and vowel 
    closing) to below 2fo to precipitate the turning over of the voice. Strategies 
    used include lowering the larynx, lip rounding and/or trumpeting, and 
    vowel darkening.
    Helmholtz resonator:  a hollow enclosure having a smaller opening 
    consisting of such dimensions that the enclosure resonates at a single 
    frequency determined by the relative dimensions of its space and opening: 
    the larger the space and smaller the exit, the lower the frequency.
    PRAA.indb   159PRAA.indb   159 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 160
    Inertive reactance: a sluggishness of air molecule movement created by 
    resonator convergence, which can improve resonator interactivity with the 
    vibrator by reducing the transglottal pressure diﬀerence, glottal resistance, 
    and phonation threshold pressure.
    Intensity: the power of a sound measurable in decibels; the physical 
    correlate to perceptual loudness.
    Laryngeal register: the muscular adjustment of the vocal folds; the 
    relative participation of the thyroarytenoid (TA—shortening, thickening) 
    muscles and the cricothyroid (CT—stretching, thinning) muscles.
    Chesty: historic description of a sound assumed to be TA dominant, with 
    shorter, thicker, looser vocal folds, and which has a shallower spectral 
    slope with more strong, high harmonic component frequencies, and a 
    buzzier timbre. Now called vibrational mechanism one.
    Cricothyroid muscles (CT): the muscles, which upon contracting stretch 
    and thin the vocal folds for higher pitches. !ey are mostly situated outside 
    of the larynx and tilt the laryngeal cartilages to stretch the vocal folds. !ey 
    are responsible for what has historically been called “head” or “falsetto” 
    register (now vibrational mechanism two ), with its thinner vocal fold 
    contact, fundamental frequency dominance, and smaller number of 
    harmonics.
    Heady: historic description of a sound assumed to be CT dominant, with 
    thinner, longer, tauter vocal folds, and which has a stronger fundamental, 
    a steeper spectral slope, fewer and weaker high harmonic component 
    frequencies, and a smoother timbre. Now called vibrational mechanism 
    two.
    /T_hyroarytenoid muscles (TA): the muscles, which upon contracting 
    shorten and thicken the vocal folds for lower pitches. !ey comprise the 
    body of the vocal folds and are responsible for what has historically been 
    called “chest” register (now vibrational mechanism one), with its thicker 
    vocal fold contact, vertical phase diﬀerence , and larger number of 
    harmonics.
    Linear source-/f_ilter model: an earlier model of vocal resonance that was 
    sequentially, chronologically linear; that is, that postulated a power source 
    (breath) providing air&ow to a vibrator (the vocal folds), bringing them 
    into vibration, generating a voice source signal (sound wave) containing 
    PRAA.indb   160PRAA.indb   160 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 161
    harmonics, which are then selectively resonated by a /f_ilter (resonator; the 
    vocal tract), which then transfers the /f_iltered harmonics to the outside 
    world through the lips. (See non-linear source-/f_ilter model.)
    Locational somatosense: the perceptual location of sensation of a sound; 
    historically referred to as “placement. ” Sound sensation migrates across 
    range from predominantly oral on low pitches to increasingly internal, 
    above the palate, and into the torso with ascent.
    Maximum Flow Declination Rate: the rate of reduction of air&ow volume 
    of the glottal impulses from peak (maximum) &ow to zero or baseline 
    &ow; the more rapid the declination, the stronger the high-frequency 
    content of the voice source excitation.
    Mechanism one: a vibrational mode of the larynx in which the vocal fold 
    shape is short and thick, the vocal fold cover loose, and in which the TA 
    muscle is involved in the vibration. It has a vertical phase diﬀerence , 
    generating more and stronger high partials. It is caused by greater TA 
    laryngeal muscle participation. (See Laryngeal register)
    Mechanism three: a vibrational mode of the larynx in which the vocal 
    fold shape is long and thin, the vocal fold cover is taut, eventually involving 
    epithelial vibration. However, the vocal folds may not be fully adducted 
    nor vibrate along their full length.
    Mechanism two: a vibrational mode of the larynx in which the vocal 
    fold shape is long and thin, the vocal fold cover is taut, involving vocal 
    ligament vibration. It has little vertical phase diﬀerence , resulting in 
    a more sinusoidal pressure wave that is more fundamental frequency 
    dominant. It is caused by greater CT laryngeal muscle participation and 
    usually passive TAs. (See Laryngeal register)
    Mechanism zero: a vibrational mode of the voice source in which the 
    vocal fold shape is short and thick, and the vocalis muscle and vocal fold 
    cover are loose. Also called pulse or vocal fry register.
    Mode of phonation: a way of categorizing the ratios of the three elements 
    of phonation: breath pressure (the transglottal pressure diﬀerence), 
    PRAA.indb   161PRAA.indb   161 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 162
    air&ow (through the glottis), and glottal resistance (vocal valve closure 
    force). Although these can vary along a continuum, they can be modeled 
    by representative numbers in a phonation equation as follows: breath 
    pressure/air&ow = glottal resistance. One of three ratios of pressure, &ow, 
    and glottal resistance in phonation: breathy (excess air&ow: 1/ 2 = 0.5); 
    pressed (excess glottal resistance or glottal closure force: 1/0.5 = 2); &ow 
    (a balance between pressure, &ow, and resistance: 1/1 = 1). (Not to be 
    confused with vibrational mechanism; see Laryngeal registration above.)
    Breathy phonation: a phonation equation model with excess air&ow, such 
    as 1/2 = 0.5 would be termed breathy phonation.
    Flow phonation: a phonation equation model with an ideal balance for a 
    given situation would be 1/1=1, and is termed &ow phonation.
    Pressed phonation: a phonation equation model with excess glottal 
    resistance, such as 1/0.5 = 2 would be termed pressed phonation.
    Narrow frequency bandwidth: see Bandwidth.
    Nasalance: using a (usually) slight opening of the velopharyngeal port 
    to divert and allow a small percentage of air&ow and sound through 
    the nares. Nasalance broadens the bandwidth of the /f_irst resonance, 
    weakening its intensity. A broader, weaker fR1 reduces potential interactive 
    interference with laryngeal register transitions and the percentage of tone 
    color contributed by the /f_irst formant, thus brightening the timbre.
    Necessary roughness: a perception of buzziness caused by high, closely 
    clustered and resonated spectral content. Frequencies less than a m3 
    apart beat against each other to human hearing perception, introducing a 
    buzziness or sizzle to the timbral percept.
    Node: see pressure node.
    Non-linear source-/f_ilter model: a source-/f_ilter model of vocal resonance 
    with the added component of possible interactivity between the /f_ilter 
    and the source, such that certain resonation postures of the /f_ilter cause 
    feedback on the vibrator that alters the function and contribution of the 
    vibrator, ideally by assisting its e#ciency.
    PRAA.indb   162PRAA.indb   162 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 163
    Openness-closeness dimension:  descriptions of a vocal sound relative 
    to the frequency location of its /f_irst formant and the relationship of its 
    harmonics to its /f_irst formant. (See Voce chiusa, Close timbre, Close vowel, 
    and Voce aperta, Open timbre, Open vowel)
    Open timbre: a vocal sound in which two or more harmonics are at or 
    below the /f_irst formant of the vowel being sung. Also termed voce aperta 
    in Italian. Sometimes also used to describe a generally divergent resonance 
    shape strategy.
    Open vowel: a vowel with a high /f_irst formant, such as [ɑ], and an 
    inherently divergent resonator shape.
    Overtones: a designation for frequency components of a sound that lie 
    above the fundamental frequency. Overtones usually refer to harmonic 
    frequencies. !e /f_irst overtone is the second harmonic.
    Over-vowel tone color: the vowel-like spectral tone color tuned by the 
    second resonance of the vocal tract and featured by the radiated second 
    formant. Its pitch ranges from the top of the treble clef to just into the top 
    octave of the keyboard. In isolation, it has the spectral tone color range of [~ɔ 
    ɑ a æ ɛ ɪ e i]. As such, it is usually the target tone color of the intended vowel.
    Partials: another term for frequency components of a sound; partials may 
    also refer to non-harmonic frequencies, that is, frequencies that are not 
    multiples of the lowest frequency component.
    Passaggio: Italian for passage or transition into the upper voice.
    Primo passaggio: the lower entry into the transition zone.
    Secondo passaggio:  the upper exit of the transition zone, entry into the 
    upper voice.
    Zona di passaggio: the transition zone, a range segment of about a 4th or 
    5th in non-treble voice singers, during which it is thought that the laryngeal 
    registration should shi/f_t from a TA dominant mechanism one to a lighter 
    mechanism one with greater CT participation. In treble voice registration 
    this transition area is longer and is sub-divided into a lower middle and 
    upper middle, during which the voice transitions into CT dominant 
    vibrational mechanism two.
    PRAA.indb   163PRAA.indb   163 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 164
    Passive vowel migration, passive vowel modi/f_ication: this author’s 
    proposed designations for a change in vowel quality accomplished 
    by retaining the vocal tract shape but moving the pitch with its subset 
    of harmonics. !e change in vowel quality results from the changing 
    relationships and interactions between the stable resonances and the 
    moving harmonics. (See Vowel modi/f_ication.)
    Perturbation /T_heory: a theory modeling the vocal tract as a quarter-
    wave resonator whose frequency responses (resonances) can be retuned 
    by local perturbations (changes of dimension: narrowings or widenings) 
    near pressure wave nodes or antinodes.
    Pharyngeal voice:  a vocal production typically utilizing the thin vocal 
    ligament of mechanism two with enhanced vocal fold closure, pharyngeal 
    narrowing, a shallow spectral slope, and a peculiarly bright timbre. Also 
    called “witch voice” ( voce di strega ). Used to train the bridge between 
    mechanisms one and two and ease of upper voice phonation.
    Piriform Sinuses (Fossae): the small side branches of the lower pharynx 
    on either side of the laryngopharynx, which create an anti-resonance in 
    the 4000–6000 Hz region, highlighting the singer’s formant cluster just 
    below (2000–4000 Hz).
    Pitch: our perception of the frequency of a sound. It is the frequency of 
    the composite pressure pattern of multiple harmonic frequencies of a 
    tone. !ough derived from processing the /f_irst /f_ive to eight harmonics 
    of a tone, in most practical circumstances, it can be considered to be the 
    psychoacoustic equivalent of the fundamental frequency.
    Pitch of turning: the pitch at which a voice turns over, just inside of an 
    octave below the pitch of the /f_irst formant of the vowel being sung. !e 
    pitch at which the 2fo crosses above the fR1 of the vowel being sung.
    Power spectrum: an analysis of sound that displays power on the vertical 
    axis and frequency components on the horizontal axis.
    Pressed phonation: (see Mode of phonation)
    PRAA.indb   164PRAA.indb   164 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 165
    Pressure antinode:  a point of maximum pressure variation within an 
    average pressure level context.
    Pressure node: a point of minimum pressure variation within an average 
    pressure level context.
    Pressure waveform: the pattern of air pressure variations (compressions 
    and rarefactions) that form a sound. If there is a regular, periodically 
    repeating pattern, the sound will have a clear pitch. If there is no regularly 
    repeating pattern, but rather random pressure variations, the sound will 
    be perceived as noise.
    Primary acoustic register transitions: prominent acoustic registration 
    events at the point at which 2fo crosses fR1, transitioning from open to close 
    timbre, and the point at which 1 fo intersects fR1, attaining whoop timbre. 
    Re/f_ining these two events is a major goal of Western classical singing 
    pedagogy for non-treble and treble voices, respectively. !e former is 
    most o/f_ten associated with the turning over of the open vowels in the 
    neighborhood of C4-G4 in non-treble voices. !e latter is the preferred 
    strategy for the upper middle range of Western classical treble voices.
    Primary laryngeal register transition: the shi/f_t from chest to falsetto, 
    or vibrational mechanism one to vibrational mechanism two. Non-
    treble classical singers tend to avoid this shi/f_t, staying in some form 
    of mechanism one for the entire range. Treble classical singers tend to 
    shi/f_t from mechanism one to mechanism two between C4 and G4, 
    usually before or by the fR1:2fo acoustic register transition. Belters extend 
    mechanism one or chest register to ca. C5.
    Primo passaggio: see passaggio.
    Procedural knowledge: the subjective, sensorial knowledge of motor 
    behavior, what it feels like to do the thing; the goal of motor learning for 
    skillful function; in short, the automated basis of ability. Being subjective 
    and sensorial, procedural knowledge is challenging to articulate but is 
    essential for intentional, skillful physical activity of any kind.
    PRAA.indb   165PRAA.indb   165 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 166
    Quarter-wave resonator: a resonator that is a tube open at one end 
    and closed at the other. Such a resonator can establish standing waves 
    (resonances) at the quarter-wavelength and odd-numbered multiples of 
    that length: 3/4 wavelength, 5/4 wavelength, 7/4 wavelength, and so on, 
    that is, wavelength segments with nodes and antinodes at opposite ends of 
    the tubular resonator. !e vocal tract is a form of quarter-wave resonator, 
    but with more variable, tunable wavelengths.
    Reactance: a matching of resistance between the glottis and the vocal 
    tract or epilarynx that increases the interactivity of the system (feedback 
    on the vibrator).
    Resonator convergence: an overall downstream narrowing of the vocal 
    tract shape from a relatively open area near the glottis to a narrowing 
    nearer the lips.
    Resonance: Resonance is the tendency of an object or system to respond 
    (oscillate) more strongly and longer to particular frequencies introduced 
    into it. A vocal tract resonance is one of several potential peak frequency 
    responses of the vocal tract to acoustic input.
    Real-time spectrography: an ever-changing, immediately current 
    analysis and visual representation of sound that displays frequency on the 
    vertical, time on the horizontal, and intensity on the gray or color scale. 
    !is form of sound display is currently the most intuitive and informative 
    as a visual learning aid for students.
    Roll-oﬀ: the drop in intensity of harmonics above the /f_irst harmonic; 
    typically indicated in decibels per octave (dBs/octave). Called “Tilt” on 
    the Madde synthesizer. Also termed spectral slope.
    Secondo passaggio: see passaggio.
    Semi-occluded vocal tract (SOVT):  a vocal tract with a location or exit 
    su#ciently narrowed to generate &ow resistance or acoustic impedance. 
    A short thin straw is one mechanical example. A very small, kazoo-like 
    [w] is an articulatory example. (See also downstream resistor.) SOVT 
    exercises are used to rebalance pressure, &ow, and resistance levels, 
    reducing transglottal pressure diﬀerence and glottal resistance.
    PRAA.indb   166PRAA.indb   166 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 167
    Singer’s formant cluster: (see Formant)
    Somatosense: the body’s perceptual feel of a phenomenon or behavior, 
    such as the somatosense of a sung sound.
    Spectral envelope: an outline of the power dimension of a power spectrum. 
    It is useful for revealing the resonance characteristics (formants) of a 
    particular vocal tract shape.
    Spectral slope: the rate at which higher frequency spectral content of the 
    voice source or the radiated sound weakens in intensity per octave.
    Spectral tilt: another name for spectral slope.
    Spectrum: a chart of the frequency content of a sound, usually with 
    frequency on the horizontal axis and intensity on the vertical axis.
    Spectrography: a spectrograph displays frequency on the vertical axis, 
    time on the horizontal axis, and power on a gray or color scale. It is 
    perhaps the most accessible display of sound for studio use.
    /T_hyroarytenoid muscles (TA): (see Laryngeal register)
    Transfer function of the vocal tract:  the capacity of a particular shape 
    of the vocal tract to transfer sound energy from the glottis to the outside 
    world, which capacity varies preferentially by frequency, resulting in 
    “peaks and valleys” of frequency response.
    Transglottal pressure diﬀerence: the diﬀerence in pressure between the 
    average subglottal (tracheal) and supraglottal air pressures. !e transglottal 
    pressure diﬀerence determines the necessary glottal resistance level and 
    resultant vocal fold collision force. A comfortably low transglottal pressure 
    diﬀerence is therefore essential to maintaining healthy vocal fold tissue.
    Transient model of voice acoustics: a time and pressure dominant model 
    of voice acoustics in contrast to the frequency-dominant source-/f_ilter 
    model. !e transient model more accurately portrays the way in which 
    the complex pressure waves of voice are actually generated.
    PRAA.indb   167PRAA.indb   167 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 168
    Turning over: a term descriptive of the passing of 2fo above fR1 of a vowel 
    when ascending, and its accompanying sound and somatosense.
    Twang: a (controverted) term descriptive of a bright, edgy quality used in 
    CCM genres, accomplished by various pharyngeal narrowing strategies. 
    Associated by some more with Country Music vocal style, but used in 
    music theater belt and other styles as well.
    Under-vowel tone color: the vowel-like spectral tone color tuned by the 
    /f_irst resonance of the vocal tract and featured by the /f_irst formant. Its pitch 
    range largely overlaps the treble clef, and therefore in isolation has the 
    spectral tone color range of [~o-u] but in context is usually perceived as 
    mixed with the tone color of the second formant over-vowel.
    Valleculae: spaces between the epilarynx and the back of the tongue. Like 
    the piriform sinuses behind the epilarynx, open valleculae create a high-
    frequency anti-resonance. Hence, closing them increases timbal ring.
    Valsalva maneuver:  a forceful compression of the thorax (ribcage), 
    similar to eﬀortful elimination, childbearing, and heavy li/f_ting. If resisted 
    by glottal closure, it stimulates excessively strong glottal resistance and 
    should not be used in singing.
    Vertical phase diﬀerence : a diﬀerence in the timing of opening and 
    closing of the vocal folds’ vertical contact. It is caused by thicker vocal 
    folds whose contact area opens and closes at its lower margins ahead of 
    the opening and closing of its upper margins.
    Vocal ligament: the intermediate layer of the three-layer vocal fold 
    structure: the epithelial mucosal cover, the vocal ligament, and the vocalis 
    (thyroarytenoid) muscle body. Vocal fold vibrational load shi/f_ts from the 
    full body in mechanism one to the vocal ligament in mechanism two to 
    the epithelial layer in mechanism three.
    Vocal tract /f_ilter: the tube resonator comprised of the vocal tract from the 
    glottis to the lips. !is resonator “/f_ilters” spectral partials passing through 
    it, strengthening some and weakening others to create the vowels and 
    timbre of the sound radiated from the lips.
    PRAA.indb   168PRAA.indb   168 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 169
    Vocal tract resonances: potential peak frequency responses of the vocal 
    tract /f_ilter function to acoustic input.
    Voce aperta: open timbre; any sung sounds in which two or more 
    harmonics are at or below the /f_irst resonance//f_irst formant—in other 
    words, the timbre of any pitches sung an octave or more below the /f_irst 
    formant of the vowel being sung. !is term has also been used historically 
    to describe an overall divergent resonator shape characterized by a timbre 
    in which vowels can tend to widen and move into yell timbre.
    Voce chiusa: close timbre; any sung sounds in which the second harmonic 
    is above the /f_irst resonance//f_irst formant—in other words, the timbre of 
    any sung pitches less than an octave below the /f_irst formant. !is term 
    has also been used historically to describe an overall convergent resonator 
    poise that results in the chiaroscuro timbre of Western classical singing, in 
    which vowels close when and where they should relative to vowels’ /f_irst 
    resonance/formant locations, in contrast to a predominantly divergent, 
    open timbre resonator adjustment.
    Voice source: the sound signal produced by the excitation of the air 
    column in the vocal tract by the abrupt closure of the glottic valve (vocal 
    folds). Each such closure introduces a noise spectrum with a theoretically 
    in/f_inite number of frequencies that excite the transfer function of the 
    vocal tract. !us, both the vocal tract and the vocal folds cooperate in 
    generating the voice source of a single impulse, which is then /f_iltered 
    further—by the periodic reiteration of that pulse—for frequencies in sync 
    with its fundamental frequency, that is, harmonic frequencies.
    Vowel designations: close or open (due to low or high /f_irst formant 
    locations), front or back (due to high or low second formant locations) 
    categorizations.
    Vowel formants: (see Formant)
    Vowel integrity: maintaining acceptable vowel identity even when vowel 
    modi/f_ication is necessary; avoidance of vowel distortion.
    PRAA.indb   169PRAA.indb   169 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Deﬁnitions 170
    Vowel migration: the inevitable, gradual migration or change of timbre 
    of vowels across range, due to frequency-dependent spectral tone color 
    and the changing interactions of frequencies with vocal tract resonances.
    Vowel modi/f_ication: historically understood to refer to a change in the 
    vowel quality accomplished by moving the vowel formants from their 
    normal speech locations by means of changes in the shape of the vocal 
    tract in an attempt to /f_ind better (more resonant) resonance:harmonic 
    matches. !is text proposes the subcategories: active and passive vowel 
    modi/f_ication. (See Passive vowel modi/f_ication)
    Whoop timbre: the timbre of a sung sound that is dominated by the tone 
    color of its fundamental frequency. Whoop timbre occurs when the sung 
    pitch approximately matches (and is tracked by) the frequency of the 
    /f_irst resonance and is therefore prominently featured in the radiated /f_irst 
    formant: a fR1:1fo coupling. It begins to appear from the mid-treble clef, 
    especially in treble voices. Since the fundamental frequency in that range 
    has an [~u], [~o], or [~ɔ]-like tone color, whoop timbre is round, domed, 
    and “whoop” or “hoot” like and is associated with head voice or M2.
    Y ell coupling, yell timbre: an acoustic coupling of fR1 and 2fo that has been 
    carried higher than normal through tube shortening and mouth widening 
    (fR1:2fo tracking); bright and o/f_ten pressed and spread in quality.
    Zona di passaggio: transition zone. (See passaggio)
    PRAA.indb   170PRAA.indb   170 7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM7/10/2025   4:39:00 PM
    Abbreviations
    Abbreviations
    A4, B3, C5, etc.: pitch designations by octave with A4 corresponding to 
    440 Hz and C4 corresponding to middle C.
    CCM: Contemporary Commercial Music; designation of vocal styles 
    other than Western classical, o/f_ten electronically ampli/f_ied.
    CT: Cricothyroid muscle
    fo, 1 fo: the fundamental frequency or lowest frequency component of a 
    periodic sound (the same as H1).
    F1, F2, F3, etc.: formants resulting from the vocal tract /f_ilter function. !e 
    vocal tract is a resonator with multiple natural resonances, resulting in 
    radiated spectral peaks called formants that are numbered from lowest to 
    highest. 
    1fo, 2 fo, 3 fo, etc.: harmonic frequency components of a periodic sound 
    having “pitch”: “one times the fundamental oscillation; two times the 
    fundamental frequency oscillation, ” and so on; more simply stated, /f_irst 
    harmonic, second harmonic, third harmonic, and so on. Formerly labeled 
    H1, H2, H3, and so on.
    IP A: International Phonetic Alphabet. IPA symbols for vowels appear in 
    the following form in this text: [ɑ].
    M0, M1, M2, M3: vibrational mechanisms of the vocal folds, corresponding 
    roughly to the historic terms pulse, chest, head, and whistle.
    SFC: Singer’s formant cluster, a clustering of formants three and 
    higher.
    TA: !yroarytenoid muscle

    Appendix 1
    Appendix 1
    Madde Explorations
    Madde Exploration 1: 
    The Unresonated Voice Source
    Check the “Show formants/partials” box (bottom right just above the 
    keyboard). Uncheck the formants boxes (upper le/f_t, labeled F1[Hz], 
    F2[Hz], F3[Hz], etc.). Activate a set of harmonics by clicking on a pitch 
    on the keyboard at or below middle C (middle C4 is marked with a black 
    dot). Y ou will see a set of numbered lines, representing the harmonics 
    of the pitch you selected, just above the keyboard. (See Figure 18.1 .) 
    Activate the play arrow (top le/f_t). !is will approximate the pitched buzz 
    of an unresonated set of harmonics as it might sound if exposed in open 
    space. Y ou will probably want to deactivate this unattractive sound fairly 
    quickly.
    Madde Exploration 2: The E/uniFB00ect of 
    Mode of Phonation and Range on 
    Source Harmonics
    Deactivate the formant boxes as you did for Madde Example 1. To 
    experience the eﬀect of laryngeal register on the voice source, adjust the 
    Tilt [dB/oct] level in the Source spectrum area (upper right) by clicking 
    in the tilt box and using the up and down arrows on your keyboard. !e 
    Tilt represents the roll-oﬀ in power per octave of the harmonics. !e 
    larger the negative number, the greater the tilt. !e default Tilt is set for 
    Appendix 1
    PRAA.indb   172PRAA.indb   172 7/10/2025   4:39:01 PM7/10/2025   4:39:01 PM
    Appendix 1 173
    Appendix 1
    a roll-oﬀ of 6 decibels per octave (i.e., -6). A “headier” tone would have a 
    steeper roll-oﬀ (try -12). A pressed tone would have a shallower roll-oﬀ  
    (try -3). (!is author advises against trying positive tilt numbers, at least 
    not with formants deactivated.) ( Figure 18.2) 
    Activate the play arrow (top le/f_t) for each of these. !e steeper the tilt 
    or roll-oﬀ, the milder the source will sound since it will have weaker high 
    harmonics. Since these higher frequencies are precisely where our ears are 
    the most sensitive to intensity, the signal will also sound quieter at steeper 
    tilts (-12), and louder (and more annoying!) at shallower tilts (-3). Y ou 
    will again want to deactivate the sound.
    In order to see the eﬀect of pitch on the number of available harmonics, 
    click on successively higher pitches up to soprano high C (C6). !e Madde 
    keyboard will only show three harmonics at that pitch, since the top C 
    (C8) is missing from its keyboard display. It is dramatically clear that high 
    sopranos face a rather diﬀerent resonance challenge/situation than a non-
    treble voice faces.

    showing and playing an unresonated harmonic frequency set.

    Appendix 1  174
    Madde Exploration 3: Formant 
    Resonation of Source Harmonics; 
    Harmonic:formant Crossings
    Make sure the “Show formants/partials” box (bottom right just above 
    the keyboard) is checked. Check the formants boxes (upper le/f_t, labeled 
    F1[Hz], F2[Hz], F3[Hz], etc.). Y ou will notice a red band appearing for 
    each formant just above its frequency center on the keyboard. Y ou can set 
    the formant frequencies for various voice types and vowels. For example, 
    bass baritone formants for an [e] vowel might be set as follows: F1 440; F2 
    1400; F3 2200; F4 2500; F5 2800. Set those and play the scale C3 D3 E3 F3 
    G3. If you continue playing, you may notice timbral changes as harmonics 
    pass through formants (visible just above the keyboard), especially through 
    formant one (more on that later). Y ou will certainly notice that harmonics, 
    once resonated by the vocal tract, are rather more pleasant than with the 
    formants removed. Other vowels can be modeled by varying the frequencies 
    of the /f_irst two formants (more on that later).
    Figure 18.2 Madde voice synthesizer Tilt function (in the Source spectrum 
    box, upper right-hand side): Tilt reﬂects source roll-off. Steeper tilt or 
    roll-off (a higher negative value) models a headier laryngeal registration. 
    Shallower tilt or roll-off (a small negative value) models a chestier laryngeal 
    registration.

    Appendix 1 175
    Madde Exploration 4: Open and 
    Close Timbre
    Make sure the “Show formants/partials” box (bottom right just above the 
    keyboard) is activated. Use the bass baritone formant settings for an [e] 
    vowel of Exploration 3, Figure 18.3. !e /f_irst formant for this example 
    is set at A440. Try the following explorations: Play pitches that lie more 
    than an octave below F1 to experience open timbre. Play an ascending 
    scale starting on C3 an octave below middle C. Y ou may notice a timbral 
    “closure” when 3fo rises through F1, that is, from ca. C3 to E3, and even 
    more prominently when 2fo rises through F1, from G3 to B3, the primary 
    turning of the voice (Figure 18.4). 
    Madde Exploration 5: Whoop 
    Timbre and Beyond
    Make sure the “Show formants/partials” box (bottom right just above the 
    keyboard) is activated. Use the bass baritone formant settings for an [e] 
    Figure 18.3 Formants of the vocal tract set for an [e] in a bass voice.
    PRAA.indb   175PRAA.indb   175 7/10/2025   4:39:02 PM7/10/2025   4:39:02 PM
    Appendix 1  176
    vowel as in Example 3, 4 above. !e /f_irst formant for this example is set at 
    A440. Continue with the following explorations:
1. Ascend stepwise from C4 without adjusting the formants. !e 
   implication of leaving the formants the same is that the “singer” 
   is not changing his vocal tract (throat/vowel) shape at all. As 
   1fo approaches F1, the timbre will become increasingly close 
   until full whoop timbre is achieved at the F1:1fo juncture at A4. 
   Notice that this rather falsetto/feminine timbre was achieved 
   with no adjustment to the Source spectrum Tilt, therefore no 
   source change, rather was accomplished entirely by changing 
   harmonic:/f_ilter interactions, that is, entirely through changes 
   in resonance. !e implication is that source changes (laryngeal 
   registration changes) are not necessarily required for this timbre. 
   It must be noted, however, that in a human subject, source changes 
   are likely to accompany these resonance changes.
2. If you continue to raise 1 fo above F1 (A4), what is now a counter 
   tenor “whoop” timbre will quickly thin, demonstrating the 
   necessity of formant tracking above this point.
   Figure 18.4 An exploration of turning over, the primary acoustic register 
   transition of a voice, when 2fo rises above F1.
   PRAA.indb   176PRAA.indb   176 7/10/2025   4:39:02 PM7/10/2025   4:39:02 PM
   Appendix 1 177
3. Have the synthesizer “sing” D5. Click on the F1[Hz] box. 
   Repeatedly click the up arrow on your computer keyboard, 
   gradually raising the F1 frequency until it matches D5 (until the 
   red F1 band joins 1fo on the pitch D5). Y ou will notice that timbral 
   fullness/depth will return.
4. Click the keyboard down arrow to return F1 to A440 and deactivate 
   the sound (Figure 18.5).

   Any of the exercises listed above (see Exploring Vowel Turning, p. 125 
   and Exploring F1:1fo Transition and Tracking, p. 131) can be modeled on 
   Madde to demonstrate an approximation of the passive vowel migrations 
   that accompany the primary F1:2fo crossing, or any secondary crossings 
   (F1:3fo, F1:4fo, etc.) and the timbral fullness of F1:1fo tracking.

   Appendix 1  178
   F1/F2 Map
   Vowels can be more &uidly modeled and changed by selecting “Show 
   F1/F2 map” under the Settings menu. A chart will display with F1 on the 
   horizontal dimension and F2 on the vertical. Clicking and dragging the 
   cursor on this map will manipulate F1 and F2 locations, even while the 
   “voice” is activated. !is capability will allow exploration not only of 
   vowels, but also of opening and closing vowels by modifying a vowel to 
   move its /f_irst formant across the 2fo of the pitch being “sung. ” (Figure 18.6) 
   Modeling Student Voices
   If a student’s formants (from a comfortable part of the range) are derived, 
   perhaps by using VoceVista, the student’s voice can be modeled in Madde 
   for further exploration. For example, the passive vowel modi/f_ications of 
   a non-treble singer’s primary register transition on various vowels can 
   be modeled. While it is not usually necessary to model a speci/f_ic voice 
   to begin to understand passive modi/f_ication, it may be helpful for some. 
   More o/f_ten a generic model of the same voice type will su#ce. Also, fR1:1fo 
   tracking of treble voices can be convincingly demonstrated, and their 
   resultant necessary active vowel modi/f_ications.

   Appendix 2
   Appendix 2
   Approximate F1 
   Locations by Voice
   Why Approximate First Formant 
   Locations?
   !e chart on the following page gives approximate /f_irst formant locations 
   by general voice type. !e formant boxes are deliberately non-speci/f_ic 
   (covering more than one pitch) for several reasons: general voice types 
   have varieties of subdivisions that accommodate some variety of vocal 
   tract length, which overlap with adjacent voice types and subdivisions. 
   For example, a high lyric baritone may have formants that are higher than 
   those of a Heldentenor, or a deeper mezzo-soprano may have formants 
   that are lower than those of a leggiero tenor. Voices are individual, as are 
   their formant locations. However, /f_irst formant locations will not vary 
   very far from the approximate locations given, and the general contour of 
   formant locations from vowel to vowel will be relatively stable. One could 
   use VoceVista to identify speci/f_ic spoken formants or formants of vowels 
   sung near speech range if speci/f_icity is desired. However, the teacher’s 
   ear and the ease and e#ciency of vocal function are usually su#cient for 
   re/f_ining the locations of acoustic registration events. Once a teacher knows 
   approximate locations by voice type, the passive vowel modi/f_ications that 
   accompany these events, and strategies to encourage timbral consistency 
   (i.e., tube stability) across these events, the student’s vocal function will 
   guide further adjustments.
